Subject: [PLUG] VMware Install on SuSE
In-Reply-To: <451F4327.6090805@comcast.net>

On Sat, 30 Sep 2006, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> I got the tarball for VMware Server from:
> http://register.vmware.com/content/download.html
> It's the first one on the Linux list, about halfway down the page. They
> have several other downloads that it looks like they want me to need, but
> I haven't gotten that far.

Dick,

   I don't understand why you chose that product. They've certainly
re-organized their web site and product naming since selling the company,
but that looks like an enterprise product that's meant to be used by
multiple people simultaneously.

   Several years ago they forked their one product into the "workstation"
version and the "enterprise server" solution. I've been using the former
since version 0.7 or 0.8 and now have 5.5 installed. It's been hosting
win98se for seeral years and does just fine. I suspect that you don't need
the server edition. And, the workstation (look under Products->Developers,
that's where it's now tucked away) is sufficiently expensive by itself.

   The server will partition your hard drive into multiple virtual machines.
Is that what you want? The workstation will install into an existing linux
partition and let you install a guest OS that's there when you need it.

Rich

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sun Oct  1 09:56:59 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sun, 1 Oct 2006 10:56:59 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] VMware Install on SuSE
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610010812300.5274@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <451F1AFD.6020009@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0609302006210.28722@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<451F4327.6090805@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610010812300.5274@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610011049430.8203@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

Let me add to this that VMWare has a free PLAYER and a free SERVER
product.  I made heavy use of the free player until I recently
discovered the free server.  The server is an awesome product.  It
allows you to run your VMs on a remote machine, and attach to them using
the remote console (also free).  This splits the CPU time between 2
machines.  The machine running the guest OS does not have to deal with
the display, and the machine dealing with the display does not have to
deal with the running processes.  This arrangement gives me a great
performance boost.  Also, you can simply X out of the console, and the
VMs stay running.  It's like screen for virtual machines.

Go to http://www.vmware.com/products/server/ and get that puppy!  Or, if
you are running gentoo, simply do:

emerge app-emulation/vmware-server
emerge app-emulation/vmware-server-console
emerge app-emulation/vmware-modules
env-update
source /etc/profile
vmware-config.pl

Carlos Konstanski

My boss actually paid for my VMWare Workstation license 9 or 10 months
ago ($400 so I could run windows, so I could check my mail, because they
won't turn on IMAP, thus preventing me from using pine).  When I found out
about the free server, I put it on a spare machine under the pretense of
setting up a testbed for bug tracking software evaluation.  I quietly
moved my windows workstation over there, and have been running remotely
ever since.

They did buy me 2G of RAM to make my box run better with VMWare.  That's
why I am keeping the move quiet - I don't want to give up the RAM.

Carlos Konstanski

installation of the Sever somewhere on the box and pointing it to that
location.

> uname -r outputs:
> 2.6.17-1.2187_FC5
>
> The installed kernel_dev package under /usr/src/kernel is:
> 2.6.17-1.2187_FC5-i686
>
> Do I have the 386 instead of the 686 headers???
>
> The error I get:
> The kernel defined by this directory of header files does not have the same
> address space size as your running kernel.
>

One thing that happens when you get a kernel is that some of the headers are
not defined until you do 'make config'.  Until that time anything the
would normally
use the headers from the kernel tree will normally fail as the CPU
arch is not defined
and that determines which arch headers are linked into the include/linux dir.

"Eating your own dogfood" is a great way to end up with stuff that
tastes good to you but is no good for your customer's dogs.

I remember when MS started insisted on using MS SourceSafe for its own
major codebases.  SourceSafe is meant for *small* projects, not major
applications with hundreds of devs.  Code was lost, work was lost,
schedules slipped, and SS devs were universally reviled for having
produced a product that merely did what it was meant to.

processes.  You could probably write a script that does this
automatically, and then hook it up to some trigger -- a wireless
joystick or similar device, so you wouldn't need to do the "logging in
as root" part.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Oct  2 17:31:08 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)

Subject: [PLUG] [Week starts with Sunday (Was: Re: pilot-link 0.12.1

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610021726260.16226@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   A couple of weeks ago, Daniel was kind enough to look into this gtk+2 problem
and offer a solution. Unfortunately, that solution did not work for me.

   This one, from a subscriber to the pilot-link mail list, is simple and it
worked for me! Now all my gtk+2 calendars on this box begin on a Sunday. So,
I pass it on in case others come across the problem.

Rich

-- 
Richard B. Shepard, Ph.D.               |    The Environmental Permitting
Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc.(TM)    |            Accelerator
<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>     Voice: 503-667-4517      Fax: 503-667-8863

---------- Forwarded message ----------
-------- Original Message --------
Subject: Week starts with Sunday (Was: Re: pilot-link 0.12.1 problems)
Date: Mon, 2 Oct 2006 14:11:16 -0400
From: Jason Day <jasonday at worldnet.att.net>
Reply-To: pilot-link General List <pilot-link-general at pilot-link.org>

Actually, J-Pilot is not at fault here, it is just using the GTK calendar
widget, which itself relies on the locale to determine the first day of the
week.  There was a long thread about this on the jpilot list a while back,
and IIRC there is a bug reported against glibc.  One of the jpilot
developers posted a workaround which works for me.  I got tired of hunting
down the workaround every time I updated glibc, so I eventually wrote down
the required steps, reproduced here.  Adjust the locale as necessary, use at
your own risk, make sure to backup your files, etc.

1) run 'locale -k -c LC_TIME'

This should have a field called week-1stday. In my locale, en_US, using the
latest Gentoo libraries this field is 19971201. December 1st, 1997 is a Monday
which explains why the first day of the week is Monday, rather than Sunday as
it should be.

2) edit /usr/share/i18n/locales/en_US'

Find the line under LC_TIME which has '19971201' and change it to '19971130'.
November 30th, 1997 was a Sunday.

3) re-generate the binary locale data
  'localedef -v -c -i /usr/share/i18n/locales/en_US /usr/lib/locale/en_US'

4) Verify the change took place with 'locale -k -c LC_TIME'

HTH,
Jason

From tkubaska at charter.net  Tue Oct  3 10:33:21 2006
From: tkubaska at charter.net (tkubaska at charter.net)
Date: Tue, 3 Oct 2006 10:33:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Synaptic, Ubuntu, removing packages
Message-ID: <893659065.1159896802046.JavaMail.root@fepweb02>

In the Synaptic Package Manager (as it runs under Ubuntu), when I want uninstall a package, I have a choice between "Mark for Removal" and "Mark for Complete Removal".

The help seems ambiguous about which I should choose. A note says to use "Mark for Complete Removal" only for Debian, but since Ubuntu is a Debian derivative, I'm not sure if this applies.

Also, if I choose "Mark for Removal", the Synaptic Package Manager  checks for related packages, which it does not appear to do if I choose "Mark for Complete Removal".

What I want to do is do a clean uninstall of Mysql and then reinstall from scratch. Anyone have any advice?

Thanks.
 -Ted


From johnxj at comcast.net  Tue Oct  3 11:02:31 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Tue, 3 Oct 2006 11:02:31 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Synaptic, Ubuntu, removing packages
In-Reply-To: <893659065.1159896802046.JavaMail.root@fepweb02>
References: <893659065.1159896802046.JavaMail.root@fepweb02>
Message-ID: <20061003110231.66f0c420.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Tue, 3 Oct 2006 10:33:21 -0700
<tkubaska at charter.net> dijo:

> In the Synaptic Package Manager (as it runs under Ubuntu), when I want uninstall a package, I have a choice between "Mark for Removal" and "Mark for Complete Removal".
> 
> The help seems ambiguous about which I should choose. A note says to use "Mark for Complete Removal" only for Debian, but since Ubuntu is a Debian derivative, I'm not sure if this applies.
> 
> Also, if I choose "Mark for Removal", the Synaptic Package Manager  checks for related packages, which it does not appear to do if I choose "Mark for Complete Removal".
> 
> What I want to do is do a clean uninstall of Mysql and then reinstall from scratch. Anyone have any advice?

Most applications keep configuration files in your home folder (or
sometimes elsewhere). In other words, every time you change something
in the app's preferences, your change is saved in a config file
somewhere. Even things like the most recent files you opened with the
app, or the directory you looked in last, are saved somewhere.

If you do a regular removal it removes the program, but not the config
file(s). If you do a complete uninstall it wipes out all of it.

I think there may be other things that a complete removal nukes as well
-- libraries used only by the app, for example.

> I just installed Vmware player on my Linux laptop, with a Win98 guest.
> I used the www.easyvmx.com site to generate the .vmx config file.  I 
> know the free server provides more features, but for the intended
> application I needed ease of installation instead of features.  I will
> move to Vmware server if it is known to solve the problem below.
> 
> I started with generic vmplayer, and used the VM Tools "windows.iso"
> off a demo CD for workstation 5.5 to upgrade the vmplayer guest to
> the SVGA drivers.  Much faster, and 800x600x32b instead of 640x480x4b.

>

> screens, or will available vmx_XXXX.drv files for windows guests only
> do the standard size screens?   Is there a more capable driver than
> vmx_svga.drv for Win98 guests?

I don't know the answer to your question, but windows drivers in general
seem to be pretty limiting in terms of what resolutions they support;
I've never seen one support the equivalent of writing your own modeline
for the X server. But my experience with them is limited.

Does <ctrl>-<alt>-<return> not work in the vmware player, or is there
some reason why fullscreen mode won't work for what you're trying to use
it for?

On Tue, Oct 03, 2006 at 07:33:13PM -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> I have.  It looks like the rest of the "complete" mirrors out there,
> like mirrors.kernel.org/suse .  I would expect to see 4G worth of
> packages in a complete mirror.  These fall way short of that.  With my
> limited exposure to the suse way of doing things, it is likely that I am
> missing something.  If Novell's site lists a mirror as "complete", you'd
> think it were true.

Presumably you're looking for

  http://mirrors.kernel.org/opensuse/distribution/SL-10.1/inst-source/
  ftp://ftp.osuosl.org/pub/opensuse/distribution/SL-10.1/inst-source/

> Another interesting point comes up: are opensuse packages supported by
> Novell?  Can I pull a package from an opensuse mirror site without
> rendering the machine support-less?  

opensuse packages are as supported as SUSE packages were; they are
becoming one and the same. AFAIK (which isn't a whole lot) the 10.2
version will be called openSUSE[1]. So yes, you ought to be able to
install opensuse packages and get the same level of support as if you
had installed SUSE Linux 9.3 or 10.0 and installed additional SUSE
Linux packages.

> An even better question: can I convince my company that debian rocks,
> and we should quit using heinous corporate distros?

That's a question you'll have to answer for yourself. :-) (And no,
I am ~not~ soliciting for an advocacy thread.)

From johnxj at comcast.net  Tue Oct  3 20:36:48 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Tue, 3 Oct 2006 20:36:48 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Invisibility is not a Good Thing
Message-ID: <20061003203648.f134fe5b.johnxj@comcast.net>

OK, if you want to perform skullduggery on others, being invisible has
its points. But when it comes to files in a file browser, it is
definitely uncool.

I have this CD that came with a phonetics textbook. It has 411 MB of
sound files and also numerous PDF files of exercises and stuff.
Everything was fine until today when I went to access some exercises
for Chapter 2. Not there. The pdf files of exercises for the other
chapters are there, but not for Chapter 2. And previously I discovered
that some of the sound files alluded to in the textbook were also
missing. This is on Ubuntu Dapper amd64.

So today, at PSU, I went to a computer lab and slapped the CD in a
drive. Clicking on My Computer revealed that all the missing files are,
indeed, there. There is nothing wrong with the CD.

Windows reports that the CD contains 411 MB of data. Linux says the
same thing.

I copied the entire CD to a folder on my hard drive. But I get the same
results. Some of the files that appear on a Windows computer are simply
invisible.

On this computer I have Nautilus (default file browser), plus Thunar,
XFE, Konqueror and Krusader. I have Krusader set to launch as root. All
of them fail to show the "missing" files. I also navigated
to /media/cdom0 and used ls command, but the "missing" files still do
not show up.

Admittedly I am a dummy when it comes to file ownerships and
permissions, but shouldn't they all at least show up? If I lack
ownership or permissions I won't be able to open, print, save, move,
edit, etc. But the file should be there at least, shouldn't it? What do
I have to do to get to the "missing" files?

http://en.opensuse.org/Image:Download.png

Here is an exerpt from this web page...

BEFORE YOU INSTALL READ THIS:

The package manager in SUSE 10.1 is regrettably broken on most systems. 
To correct this you should do the following after you have installed SUSE:

     * Open Yast chose "online update configuration" , click next and 
wait until finished.
     * Press the updater icon on you taskbar/panel
     * If it updates, congratulations, all is well.
     * If it throws up an error. Close it.
     * Follow the instructions in this SDB article or this mail.

You probably also want to read about Using 10.1 and the Most Annoying Bugs.

Could be the reason Yast isn't playing nice.

Bruce

Message-ID: <20061004082608.GA28534@suse.de>


On Tue, Oct 03, 2006 at 10:40:25PM -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> The fact that a suse box can be updated from a publically-accessible
> repository warms my heart to it considerably.

Absolutely; not being able to do network installs and updates would
irritate me no end and I'd run a different distro.

> If this is true, [...]

I just verified the kernel.org url I gave:

  http://mirrors.kernel.org/opensuse/distribution/SL-10.1/inst-source/

works as a yast source (I'd been using a private mirror), both for the
gui and for command line package installs via yast -i by installing
some packages I had neglected to grab (how could I not have installed
nethack?!??! The horror!).

Note that yast treats updates as a seperate source from the
initial installation, though you add them as an installation
source in the smae location. So for the kernel.org mirror, you'd add
http://mirrors.kernel.org/suse/update/10.1/ as an installation source.
(And then you'll wait a while. the libzypp backend in 10.1 is really
hideous, though getting better. I'm hoping it'll be much improved
in 10.2.)

If your 10.1 installation is fresh, you'll probably want to do an
update cycle a couple of times; the first thing it will try to update
is yast/zypp itself, and then the update tool will want to restart.

On Tue, Oct 03, 2006 at 10:55:30PM -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> Thank you for this info.  I suspected foul play.  I do not have the suse
> box in front of me here; I will have to wait until tomorrow 9 am to see
> if this helps.

Note that yast will use an ncurses interface and so can be run through
an ssh session; there's no requirement to be in front of the box to
configure it.

You definitely will want to read the using suse 10.1 page at
http://en.opensuse.org/Using_10.1 .

-- 
Steve Beattie
SUSE Labs, Novell Inc. 
<sbeattie at suse.de>
http://NxNW.org/~steve/

From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 4 Oct 2006 08:29:28 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Invisibility is not a Good Thing
In-Reply-To: <20061003203648.f134fe5b.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20061003203648.f134fe5b.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061004082928.2232a636.johnxj@comcast.net>

After further poking around I am coming to the conclusion that the CD
is flaky. 

My Ubuntu laptop consistently fails to display one folder. While at PSU
yesterday I took the CD to a computer lab and stuck it in a Windows
machine. The Windows machine showed everything fine. The CD is from a
book that I bought a week ago. The very first thing I did after
removing the CD from its wrapper was to make a copy of it. And then I
copied its contents to a folder on my hard disk. However, the copy is
exhibiting the same behavior, and the folder is also lacking files.
Plus, my professor said that he was unable to copy the CD to his hard
drive on his Windows XP computer -- which he assumed was a copy
protection thing. Hence, my conclusion that there is something weird
going on with Linux reading CDs, or at least reading this particular
CD, or that there might be a copy protection issue. The latter would be
weird, though, as all the othe folders work fine.

But just now I put the CD into my Windows desktop. It has one SCSI CD
player and one ATAPI CD-RW, both of which are shared, so my plan was to
see if I could copy the CD onto the laptop hard disk from the drive in
the Windows machine over my home network. The Windows desktop machine
is several years old. Neither drive would even mount the CD, or the
copy. So something is going on with the CD. 

I wish I weren't so far fom PSU. I should just go back to the lab,
mount the CD, then e-mail its contents to myself or use my thumb drive.

Subject: [PLUG] Suse 10.1

I want to thank the members of this list for their help with my suse
growing pains, and for their patience with my ill-conceived, opinionated
remarks about certain linux distros.  I am glad to have been proven
wrong where suse is concerned.  People like me need to be put in their
place.

Once I plugged in the provided mirror source, yast worked like a charm,
needless to say.  Yum and apt are available from the provided mirror,
which is a nice added bonus.

I think I can now make a very strong case for my company to dispense
with redhat in favor of suse.  I loooovvve suse.

Carlos Konstanski
-------------- next part --------------

On Tue, Oct 03, 2006 at 10:40:25PM -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> The fact that a suse box can be updated from a publically-accessible
> repository warms my heart to it considerably.

Absolutely; not being able to do network installs and updates would
irritate me no end and I'd run a different distro.

> If this is true, [...]

I just verified the kernel.org url I gave:

  http://mirrors.kernel.org/opensuse/distribution/SL-10.1/inst-source/

works as a yast source (I'd been using a private mirror), both for the
gui and for command line package installs via yast -i by installing
some packages I had neglected to grab (how could I not have installed
nethack?!??! The horror!).

Note that yast treats updates as a seperate source from the
initial installation, though you add them as an installation
source in the smae location. So for the kernel.org mirror, you'd add
http://mirrors.kernel.org/suse/update/10.1/ as an installation source.
(And then you'll wait a while. the libzypp backend in 10.1 is really
hideous, though getting better. I'm hoping it'll be much improved
in 10.2.)

If your 10.1 installation is fresh, you'll probably want to do an
update cycle a couple of times; the first thing it will try to update
is yast/zypp itself, and then the update tool will want to restart.

On Tue, Oct 03, 2006 at 10:55:30PM -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> Thank you for this info.  I suspected foul play.  I do not have the suse
> box in front of me here; I will have to wait until tomorrow 9 am to see
> if this helps.

Note that yast will use an ncurses interface and so can be run through
an ssh session; there's no requirement to be in front of the box to
configure it.

You definitely will want to read the using suse 10.1 page at
http://en.opensuse.org/Using_10.1 .

-- 
Steve Beattie
SUSE Labs, Novell Inc. 
<sbeattie at suse.de>
http://NxNW.org/~steve/

Assuming you searched for lex and found nothing.

Do you have apt configured for "universe"?

[aka google for ubuntu lex and go for feeling lucky result]
shows ml-lex as the tool of choice as part of the universe repository.

From ehem at m5p.com  Wed Oct  4 17:08:08 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Wed, 4 Oct 2006 17:08:08 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] What do people use for lex and yacc on Ubuntu Dapper?
In-Reply-To: <20061004190008.9C0044764@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200610050008.k95088Uh063268@m5p.com>

>From: <tkubaska at charter.net>
> On Ubuntu Dapper, I'm trying to configure and build PHP5. configure tells me it cannot find lex. I don't see a lex in the Synaptic Package Manager.
> 
> What do people use for lex and yacc on Ubuntu Dapper? Thanks.

Fast-Lex for lex, package "flex". There is GNU's Bison, which isn't
entirely backwards compatible, or Berkeley-Yacc; packages "bison" and
"byacc".

> Absolutely; not being able to do network installs and updates would
> irritate me no end and I'd run a different distro.
>
> > If this is true, [...]
>
> I just verified the kernel.org url I gave:
>
>   http://mirrors.kernel.org/opensuse/distribution/SL-10.1/inst-source/
>
> works as a yast source (I'd been using a private mirror), both for the
> gui and for command line package installs via yast -i by installing
> some packages I had neglected to grab (how could I not have installed
> nethack?!??! The horror!).
>
> Note that yast treats updates as a seperate source from the
> initial installation, though you add them as an installation
> source in the smae location. So for the kernel.org mirror, you'd add
> http://mirrors.kernel.org/suse/update/10.1/ as an installation source.
> (And then you'll wait a while. the libzypp backend in 10.1 is really
> hideous, though getting better. I'm hoping it'll be much improved
> in 10.2.)
>
> If your 10.1 installation is fresh, you'll probably want to do an
> update cycle a couple of times; the first thing it will try to update
> is yast/zypp itself, and then the update tool will want to restart.
>
> On Tue, Oct 03, 2006 at 10:55:30PM -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> > Thank you for this info.  I suspected foul play.  I do not have the suse
> > box in front of me here; I will have to wait until tomorrow 9 am to see
> > if this helps.
>
> Note that yast will use an ncurses interface and so can be run through
> an ssh session; there's no requirement to be in front of the box to
> configure it.
>
> You definitely will want to read the using suse 10.1 page at
> http://en.opensuse.org/Using_10.1 .
>
> --
> Steve Beattie
> SUSE Labs, Novell Inc.
> <sbeattie at suse.de>
> http://NxNW.org/~steve/
>
>

> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Oct  4 18:00:24 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 4 Oct 2006 18:00:24 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Invisibility is not a Good Thing
In-Reply-To: <452449BC.8040101@dimstar.net>
References: <20061003203648.f134fe5b.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<20061004082928.2232a636.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Wed, 04 Oct 2006 16:54:36 -0700
Russ Johnson <russj at dimstar.net> dijo:

> John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > My Ubuntu laptop consistently fails to display one folder.
> 
> Is it possibly a multi-session CD, and that your CD-ROM doesn't work 
> well with those?

Beats me. 

First, others have suggested that it is a . file or folder, but it is
not. After all, this CD accompanies a textbook and is set up for
Windows and Mac users. No . files. As for multisession, I can't imagine
why the publisher would make it a multisession CD. But what do I know?

Second, I later surmised that the CD is just a bad burn, or however the
publisher makes them. Oddly, the CD drives in the computers at PSU seem
to read it the best. The CD has 411 MB on it, and my thumb drive
(FAT32) holds 512 MB, so today I went to PSU and copied the whole thing
to the thumb drive, and from there to the hard disk in the laptop. So
far everything is working correctly now, except that you are supposed
to launch the sound files and pdf files from an html page in your
browser. The html page thinks I'm going to launch something in Windows
to play the sound files. So instead I just look at the URL at the
bottom of the html page to see what file is being called, and then open
it in Audacity. (Audacity has its annoyances, but it happily plays WAV
and AIFF files, which is what the publisher used.)

I did have a major PITA copying the files to the thumb drive using one
of the Windows computers at PSU. Using Explorer, I dragged the whole CD
to the thumb drive and it started copying away. But then it got to a
file it couldn't read and Windows just aborted the whole copy sequence.
No option to continue copying the rest of the files and folders. Hence,
I had to try to copy folder by folder, being careful to recreate the
folders (and half a dozen subfolders).

It was so annoying doing this that I moved to a Mac, thinking that
perhaps its file drag and drop would be more sensible. But I totally
failed as a Mac user. I couldn't even figure out how to get the CD
drive open.

Guess I'm going to have to stick to Linux.

From krisa at subtend.net  Thu Oct  5 02:55:34 2006
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Thu, 05 Oct 2006 02:55:34 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Informal security chat
Message-ID: <4524D696.8000301@subtend.net>

Would anyone be interested in an informal round-table security discussion?

I have all these best practices and principles, but would like to
discuss what other people are using, and how conditions and process can
be improved.  I could gather a summary of our discussions and turn it
into a presentation for the monthly meeting.

Topics I'm interested in (both listening to and giving my experiences on):

 * Local storage encryption: performance and management
 * Key and passphrase management: multi-employee consulting
environments, change control
 * Passphrase generation
 * Tinfoil-hat vs. practical use of security practices
 * Log/IDS analysis: useful, and time effective methods of parsing large
amounts of data
 * Tying it all together: system, network, application security..
finding the weak points
 * vi vs. emacs (j/k)

I could host this at my place in Tigard.  Probably host it on a Friday
evening this month or early Nov.  If at least 5 others are interested
I'll make preparations.  Keep replies on list if possible.  Interest may
 spark interest. :)

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

From krisa at subtend.net  Thu Oct  5 15:05:05 2006
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Thu, 05 Oct 2006 15:05:05 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Invite: Informal security chat - Oct 20th
Message-ID: <45258191.4000609@subtend.net>

Ok, we have more than 5. :)


----------------------
Informal Security Chat
----------------------

What:

Geeks gather and talk about practical security practices and personal
experiences in attempting to provide a more secure computing
environment.  Emphasis on GNU/Linux, all UNIX's basically apply.

Why:

After working on a recent system with LVM, dm-crypt, OpenVPN, etc., I
was curious how others handled the problems I was trying to solve, to
see the creativity of others and to provide my own experiences to those
who might benefit.

When:

October 20th, 2006

Start gathering around 18:30 to begin at 19:00.  I can go fairly late,
but lets say we cap it at midnight.

Where:

http://www.subtend.net/contact

Informal agenda:

* Local storage encryption: performance and management
* Key and passphrase management: multi-employee consulting
environments, change control
* Passphrase generation
* Tinfoil-hat vs. practical use of security practices
* Log/IDS analysis: useful, and time effective methods of parsing large
amounts of data
* Tying it all together: system, network, application security..
finding the weak points
* securing off-site backups
* working with users to determine appropriate security policies
* administrative password change policies and practices
* wireless networking security practices and configurations

Notes:
* This isn't necessarily a meeting on crypto.  I doubt many of us will
be able to argue the strength and math of Blowfish vs. AES.  Flame wars
such as SSL vs. IPsec are more appropriate. :)
* Please RSVP.  If more than 15 want to show up, there might be a
seating/space problem.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

From tkubaska at charter.net  Fri Oct  6 01:51:51 2006
From: tkubaska at charter.net (TedK)
Date: Fri, 6 Oct 2006 01:51:51 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] lex and yacc
In-Reply-To: <c0a09e5c0610051631q183cf79ah43e9a119d27f2462@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <5c17f1$15k4md3@mxip31a.cluster1.charter.net>

It gives me greater control over the features built into the binary. It's
possible I might find a prebuilt version that has what I want (MySQL
support), but I feel safer knowing what I get. So I guess the real reason is
lack of knowledge about what a prebuilt version has. I used
--with-mysqli=/usr/local/mysql/bin/mysql_config
--with-apxs2=/usr/local/apache2/bin/apxs on my PHP configure line.

For example, I built apache also because I wanted to ensure I had support
for Dynamic Shared Objects (--enable-module=so on the apache configure
line).

What I'm trying to do is get a dev environment for LAMP, not necessarily to
run LAMP, although of course I will run it. But it's the dev environment I
want. And if I configure/make/make install and things break in the process
and I fix them, then at the end I have that dev environment.

It's also true (I hear but no data to support) that binary distributions do
perform better, however. Some rumor about people using commercial optimizers
for binary distributions, but I'm just starting out on this and performance
is not top on my list, but if all goes well, it will become so!


 -Ted

From ali.corbin at gmail.com  Fri Oct  6 12:23:49 2006
From: ali.corbin at gmail.com (Ali Corbin)
Date: Fri, 6 Oct 2006 12:23:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] VMware Management Interface 1st user
In-Reply-To: <4526A3CF.6030400@comcast.net>
References: <4526A3CF.6030400@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <965f90c20610061223h69f616d4r92ded9f297e8631f@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/6/06, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
> I've successfully (I think) installed VMware Server and the VMware
> Management Interface. I can display the login page of the Management
> Interface with Firefox. I've been following all the directions I've been
> able to find in
>
> http://pubs.vmware.com/server1/wwhelp/wwhimpl/js/html/wwhelp.htm115
>
> What I haven't found is where I enter a user and password for the first
> user. Is there a default first user, or did I miss a step somewhere
> along the way?
>
> --
> Regards,
>
> Dick Steffens
>

As I remember, the user that's allowed to use vmware is the one who
installed it.

If you were someone else when you installed it, then you can always go
and fiddle with the ownership of the files.  You might have to change
the file(s) that contain the virtual disk.  You definately must change
the config (.vmx) file.

If you want to give a bunch of people permission to access the vm,
then your best bet is probably to create a group for them, and chgrp
the files.

Ali


From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Fri Oct  6 12:36:20 2006

When I say "supported", I am strictly speaking in the corporate sense.
If I do anything that voids the service agreement, that is unsupported.
It matters not a bit that I will never use the service agreement, ever.

"Supported" also means that there is someone to sue if something goes
wrong.  A corporate palace like my workplace would never use a product
that is made by a non-corporate entity because there is no one to sue if
the product does not work.  This litigational chain provides a sense of
security for those of a coprorate mindset.  The finger must always have
somewhewre to point.  Tux is too cuddly; he makes a poor scapegoat.

Carlos Konstanski

Also, there may be a need to add all vmware users to a "vmware" group,
depending on your vmware installation.  On gentoo, for instance, I need
to add myself to the vmware group, else I cannot even run the vmware
executable.  I confess I have never installed the product from a VMWare
CD or tarball downloaded from their site.  I always use the portage
packages.  Perhaps this group constraint is applied by the ebuild.

Carlos Konstanski

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Oct  6 14:01:42 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 06 Oct 2006 14:01:42 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] VMware or IDE Drive problem?
Message-ID: <4526C436.8070106@comcast.net>

I've created my first virtual machine. When I try to install Win98SE the 
mother board starts beeping -- the same one-second-on, one-second-off 
beep I got with the other mother board when I was trying to install SuSE 
10.1.

I can shut everything down cleanly, and when I power off the machine, 
the beeping goes away. When I power back on, the machine acts normally.

I tried changing the drive the virtual machine looks for from hdd, my 
DVD reader, to hdc, my CD-RW burner, but got the same results.

I haven't had a reason to use either drive since the SuSE 10.1 
installation, so I put a CD in the DVD drive to see what would happen. 
SuSE popped up a window asking me if I wanted to open the CD in a new 
window, and when I said yes, it opened a Konqueror file browser window 
that displayed the correct contents of the CD. I get the same results 
with the CD-RW drive.

So now I'm wondering if there's something odd about my drives, and there 
really wasn't anything wrong with the other mother board.

But, the bigger question is, how do I trouble shoot this thing, or find 
a work around. If I can read the CD's, I can copy the contents as an ISO 
file to the hard drive and point VMware there, right?

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens



From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Fri Oct  6 14:05:58 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Fri, 6 Oct 2006 15:05:58 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] VMware or IDE Drive problem?
In-Reply-To: <4526C436.8070106@comcast.net>
References: <4526C436.8070106@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610061504200.14048@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

You can dd the contents of a cd or dvd to an iso file, and tell vmware
that this iso file is your "cd drive".  In fact, this is preferable to
using an actual cdrom drive, as it is much faster to install an OS from.

Carlos Konstanski

Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> You can dd the contents of a cd or dvd to an iso file, and tell vmware
> that this iso file is your "cd drive".  In fact, this is preferable to
> using an actual cdrom drive, as it is much faster to install an OS from.

Worked like a charm. Thanks!


-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

It's probably moot at this point, but there are a number of good free 
third-party CD diagnostic tools available for Windows.  There are 
probably also Linux equivalents, but I haven't had occasion to look 
around for any.  In days gone by, though, I used CDCheck on problematic 
CD-Rs and it worked well.  Thing is, your problem disc might be a 
pressed CD rather than a CD-R, so I don't know if that particular tool 
would be the best bet.  And, of course, PSU might not take kindly to you 
trying to install software on their computers.  I'd be surprised, 
though, if there aren't similar utilities for Linux.

> drive open.

Heh, I had the same problem when I first encountered my mom's iMac.  I 
had all but turned the thing upside down looking for a button to push, 
when she pointed out that the drive opens from a key on the keyboard.  
Sneaky.  Also, I panicked when discovering that the key no longer worked 
after I installed Debian on it -- until I learned about the "eject" command.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Fri Oct  6 20:04:12 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Fri, 6 Oct 2006 20:04:12 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Partition analysis
In-Reply-To: <45270EE2.5080407@comcast.net>
References: <45270EE2.5080407@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610061957310.6657@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Fri, 6 Oct 2006, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> I'm not sure why hda2 is as big as it is.

Dick,

   What do you see in /etc/fstab? That relates the partition to the
filesystem mounted on it. It will tell you what is assigned where.

   Even better is to type 'df -h'. This shows the partition, mount point, and
size. For example, my server/workstation is set up this way:

Filesystem            Size  Used Avail Use% Mounted on
/dev/hda1             479M  177M  302M  37% /
/dev/hda10           1004M  265M  740M  27% /tmp
/dev/hda11            3.0G  1.9G  1.1G  65% /vm
/dev/hda12             60G  9.3G   50G  16% /data1
/dev/hda13             22G  7.5G   14G  36% /data2
/dev/hda2              55M   40M   16M  72% /boot
/dev/hda5              15G  5.9G  8.9G  40% /home
/dev/hda6             7.9G  3.4G  4.5G  43% /usr
/dev/hda7              20G  1.8G   18G   9% /usr/local
/dev/hda8              20G  1.1G   19G   6% /opt
/dev/hda9            1004M  482M  523M  48% /var

   Note that /boot takes only 16M (of 55 available); but that's for
Slackware. The /vm partition is where VMware lives.

> So, before I go messing up the partition table, what's a reasonable size
> for the boot partition?

   Depending on the number of kernels you want available, and their
compression, you could probably be well servered with a 25M /boot.

HTH,

Rich

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Oct  6 23:30:41 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 6 Oct 2006 23:30:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Suse 10.1
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610061328001.14048@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <20061006071212.17710.qmail@web50613.mail.yahoo.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610061328001.14048@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <20061007063041.GB32346@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Fri, Oct 06, 2006 at 01:36:20PM -0600, Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> ...
> "Supported" also means that there is someone to sue if something goes
> wrong.  A corporate palace like my workplace would never use a product
> that is made by a non-corporate entity because there is no one to sue if
> the product does not work.  This litigational chain provides a sense of
> security for those of a coprorate mindset.  The finger must always have
> somewhewre to point.  Tux is too cuddly; he makes a poor scapegoat.

Have you ever actually *read* a EULA?

There is no software the market that you can use according to its
contract and retain the right to sue.  Not Microsoft, not Macintosh,
not Solaris, not Linux.  Everything comes with a "not warranted to
function" license.  So your suits are smoking rope if they think
they can sue Microsoft if Excel eats the annual report.  They will
get laughed out of court, and possibly countersued when the jackals
of Redmond sics the Business Software Alliance on them.

That said, they can buy better support from Redhat or Novell or
most especially IBM than they can ever get from Microsoft.  They
can ignore that, and watch their more savvy competitors eat their
lunch.  If they are Linux-phobic (and many B-player suits are,
Microsoft paid good money to keep them that way) and feel the
need to waste money, they can buy their support from Sun.  But
IBM can feed them Linux at a high price and give them the highest
reputation service organization in the world.  So the "supported"
excuse for not using Linux is dead in the water.  IBM and Novell
are making the transition to Linux *precisely because* it allows
them to improve their service offering.

Not that you can sue IBM, either.  The IBM legal team is referred
to as the "Nazghul" (the evil demons of Lord of the Rings) amongst
the savvy legal community.  Which is why you want to be on IBM's
side, not on SCO's side.  The hills of Utah will ring with the
shrill screams of Darl McBride for a long, long time.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

available on all currently mounted file systems is shown."  So I would 
guess that /dev/hda2 doesn't show up because it isn't mounted.  I have 
two partitions (created recently for an alternate installation) that I 
haven't added to my fstab and have not mounted, and they don't show up 
when I do "df -h".

Seems odd, though, that it wouldn't be in your fstab.

  I think you need to install the perl-modules package

The Pathologically Eclectic Rubbish Lister
A scripting language with delusions of full language-hood, Perl is used
in many system scripts and utilities.

This is a stripped down Perl with only essential libraries.  To make
full use of Perl, you'll want to install the `perl', `perl-modules' and
optionally `perl-doc' packages which supplement this one.

queue_directory = /var/spool/relay_spool

    debug_peer_list smtpd_access_maps

# I don't understand smtpd_delay_reject, help.

# I don't understand what verify is about at all.

# and blacklisting rules that follow as a sort of default policy.



smtpd_helo_restrictions= check_helo_access hash:/etc/postfix/access,
                         check_helo_access hash:/etc/postfix/invalid_helo,
                         reject_invalid_hostname,
                         reject_non_fqdn_hostname,
                         reject_unknown_hostname,
                         permit_mynetworks

# Again, is any standard followed on the Internet for the helo address?  The 
# above rules are recommended in a lot of postfix documentation, but these 
# rules appear to be for a standard that isn't adhered to.  The invalid_helo 
# file is just a way to prevent mail coming in on my server that claims to be 
# from the server itself.  Is a requirement that a fully qualified domain 
# name match the helo string not adhered to?  How about the unknown hostname
# and invalid hostname rules, are they RFC and standard practices compatible?



smtpd_recipient_restrictions =
            check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/whitelist,
                                     reject_non_fqdn_sender,
                                  reject_non_fqdn_recipient,
                               reject_unknown_sender_domain,
                            reject_unknown_recipient_domain,
                                          permit_mynetworks,
                                  reject_unauth_destination,
               check_policy_service unix:private/policy-spf,
        check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/sender_access,
                                  reject_unlisted_recipient,
          check_policy_service unix:private/policy-greylist,
                           reject_rbl_client dnsbl.ahbl.org,
                          reject_rbl_client opm.blitzed.org,
                            reject_rbl_client list.dsbl.org,
                           reject_rbl_client hil.habeas.com,
                           reject_rbl_client bl.spamcop.net,
                         reject_rbl_client psbl.surriel.com,
                          reject_rbl_client cbl.abuseat.org,
                          reject_rbl_client dnsbl.sorbs.net,
               reject_rhsbl_sender bogusmx.rfc-ignorant.com,
                reject_rhsbl_sender bulk.rhs.mailpolice.com,
                reject_rhsbl_sender porn.rhs.mailpolice.com,
                   reject_rhsbl_sender dsn.rfc-ignorant.org,
            reject_rhsbl_sender postmaster.rfc-ignorant.org,
                 reject_rhsbl_sender abuse.rfc-ignorant.org,
                 reject_rhsbl_sender whois.rfc-ignorant.org,
                                                     permit

# In this section, I created a whitelist of email source addresses
# that I want to short circuit the greylist and SPF checks for because
# letting email in from certain legit sources is more important than 
# blocking someone trying to spoof those sources.  The rfc-ignorant
# lines have been a problem at times.  A lot of sites seem to
# have abandoned: postmaster, abuse, and whois.  Another question, is
# every legit email server on the Internet going to be fully qualified
# so that I can get past reject_non_fqdn_sender, etc.?
#
# With the inability to control your own PTR zone if you have a subnet
# and a shortage of IP blocks, reverse lookups on a hostname can fail
# to match a hostname.  Worse, not everyone is in whois.  PTR records
# should be 1 to many mappings and PTR zones should be able to cover
# subnets of any size in my opinion.  The maximum number of names that 
# can be mapped to an ip address should be rigidly defined.  Why when 
# dns was set up were PTR zones not supported in the same fashion that 
# forward zones are?  Why can I point forward lookups but not reverse 
# lookups to my own subnet for a domain I control?



policy_time_limit = 60 

# Is this how long I greylist for???



smtpd_sender_restrictions = reject_unknown_sender_domain

# What part of the mail header does the following deal with?
 


smtpd_client_restrictions = reject_unauth_pipelining,
                            permit_mynetworks

# What is pipelining???



smtpd_restriction_classes = local_only
local_only =
    check_recipient_access hash:/etc/postfix/local_domains, reject

# Do the latter rules mean that only local sources can send email or do
# they mean that only local sources can receive email via my server???



sendmail_path = /usr/sbin/sendmail.postfix
newaliases_path = /usr/bin/newaliases.postfix
mailq_path = /usr/bin/mailq.postfix
setgid_group = postdrop
manpage_directory = /usr/share/man

This is an excerpt out of vmware-config.pl that isn't working right...

  #
  # Check that the running kernel matches the set of header files
  #

  if (not (-r $answer . '/linux/version.h')) {
    if ($source eq 'user') {
      print wrap('The path "' . $answer . '" is a kernel header file '
                 . 'directory, but it does not contain the file '
                 . '"linux/version.h" as expected.  This can happen if the '
                 . 'kernel has never been built, or if you have invoked the '
                 . '"make mrproper" command in your kernel directory.  In any '
                 . 'case, you may want to rebuild your kernel.' . "\n\n", 0);
    }
    return '';
  }
  $header_version_uts = direct_command(
    shell_string($gHelper{'echo'}) . ' '
    . shell_string('#include <linux/version.h>' . "\n" . $pattern
                   . ' UTS_RELEASE') . ' | ' . shell_string($gHelper{'gcc'})
    . ' ' . shell_string('-I' . $answer) . ' -E - | '
    . shell_string($gHelper{'grep'}) . ' ' . shell_string($pattern));
  chomp($header_version_uts);
  $header_version_uts =~ s/^$pattern \"([^\"]*)\".*$/$1/;
  if (not ($header_version_uts eq $gSystem{'uts_release'})) {
    if ($source eq 'user') {
      print wrap('The directory of kernel headers (version '
                 . $header_version_uts . ') does not match your running '
                 . 'kernel (version ' . $gSystem{'uts_release'} . ').  Even '
                 . 'if the module were to compile successfully, it would not '
                 . 'load into the running kernel.' . "\n\n", 0);

     print "$header_version_uts\n";
     print "$gSystem{'uts_release'}\n";
    }
    return '';
  }

It says my kernel headers don't match my running kernel, but they do.

it is too cryptic for me and naturally under commented.

the last two print statements, compilation failed.

> 
> <blatant-boosterism>
>   In pine:
>     1. Press 'h' to show all headers
>     2. Press 'f' to forward messages
>     3. Press 'y' if you want to forward message as attachment
>     4. Address message and send
> </blatant-boosterism>

<blatant-boosterism>
  In mutt:
    0. add "set mime_forword" to .muttrc
    1. press 'f' to forward messages
    2. address message and send

  If you set mime_forward to either ask-yes or ask-no, it will ask you
  (a la the pine example above) whether to forward as a mime attachment,
  the -yes or -no is the default answer.
</blatant-boosterism>

	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610091353290.24422@blackbox.fnordora.org>
	<452AB9DF.3050001@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0610091428490.11589@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Mon, 9 Oct 2006, Charlie Schluting wrote:

> > Now if the UW people would just fix the ^%&%* license agreement...
> 
> What, are you trying to resell it? Why in the world do you care?

Redistributing, not reselling, is the problem. Binary packagers (Red 
Hat, Debian, and a gazillion other FOSS packagers, big and small 
alike) can't distribute patched binaries.

Want to fix a security flaw that hasn't been fixed in the official UW 
tarball and distribute patched binaries? You're in violation of the UW 
license.

Want to add a cool patch that adds NextGreatWidget to your nifty pine 
binary package? Same story.

So you either have to distribute a completely vanilla binary *or* 
distribute the source code, your custom patchset, and a script that 
patches, compiles, and installs it.

Of course, that means that anyone who wants to use your source 
distribution has to have a machine with all the development headers 
and libraries needed to build a full-function pine: ncurses, OpenLDAP, 
OpenSSL, &c.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

Brent Rieck wrote:
> Brent Rieck wrote:
>> Yes, and no it doesn't work, it really appears to be a bash issue:
> 
> Feh.  Google tells me that it's actually a kernel issue and to make 
> things work as intended I must recompile my kernel.  What year is this 
> again?

I'm unsure why you would consider it an 'issue'. Arguments passed to a program are 
stored in the kernel so limiting them to some sane number sounds reasonable, if only to 
reduce the chance of something trying to pass 10 million arguments of 65k, quickly 
consuming more than half a gig of memory.

there are plenty of ways around this limit too, even much more efficient in doing large 
numbers of arguments:

blunt bash-only compatible code below:

while read FOO; do
	something $FOO
done < <(ls)


or just use `find . -type f -exec grep "bar" {} \;`

Cheers,

Auke

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Mon Oct  9 15:42:24 2006

# from Brent Rieck
# on Monday 09 October 2006 02:50 pm:

>Google tells me that it's actually a kernel issue and to make
>things work as intended I must recompile my kernel.

Yes, it is a kernel issue.  And, not just your command-line, but also 
your environment variables.

From the kernel source:
  $ grep ARG_MAX include/linux/limits.h
  #define ARG_MAX   131072	/* # bytes of args + environ for exec() */

If you're regularly dealing with 30k files, you should consider doing 
something like readdir() in Perl.

If you insist on recompiling your kernel, I would like to see some 
benchmarks, because I believe this memory is statically allocated for 
each new process :-D  If that's the case, it is not so much a 
memory-conservation issue as a speed optimization.  I, for one, would 
rather be able to start many small commands quickly than a few large 
ones slowly.

>What year is this again?

  $ date +%s -d 'Jan 01, 2006'
  1136102400

--Eric

>> sounds reasonable, if only to reduce the chance of something trying to 
>> pass 10 million arguments of 65k, quickly consuming more than half a 
>> gig of memory.
> 
> I consider it an issue because I have to type different commands to 
> process different numbers of the same kind of file, it really seems like 
> something that the shell or kernel should accommodate without bothering 
> me with it in this day and age.  And in this day and age half a gig of 
> memory to process 10 million arguments seems pretty sane to me.

of course, but can you please tell me how long you have been using linux fine without 
hitting this restriction?

The argument that everything should be possible obviously does not accomodate any sense 
of security at all. Do you open 2000000 files simultaneously often? Keep 2TB large log 
files? use more than 32 processors? How about running 65k processes at the same time 
sleeping?

At one point the Operating System has to decide that resources (which are limited on any 
system) and that anyone trying to use all of them might be doing bad things. Setting 
limits to resources is always a smart thing, even when they seem ridiculously low or 
restrictive - most people will benefit from them.

Your computer can often do much more than your OS allows, but that may often not be the 
smartest thing, if you want to actually get some work done ;)

Cheers,

Auke

>  $ grep ARG_MAX include/linux/limits.h
>  #define ARG_MAX   131072	/* # bytes of args + environ for exec() */

ulimit does handle a few things that you used to have to recompile for, 
such as filehandles.  If it handles arg_max they have not updated the man 
pages to cover it.

On Mon, 9 Oct 2006, Brent Rieck wrote:

> Pete Lancashire wrote:
>> PS Don't know how well ext3 handles lots of files
>> in one directory, but the old days it was not a
>> good idea to go over oh .. like a 1000 files per.
>
> Yeah, in the bad old days having so many files in a directory would be 
> painfully slow, today it doesn't seem to be any problem.

It can be, it is not as easy a limit to hit.

I remember someone who wrote a web cgi script that dumped one entry into a 
directory for every hit on the script.  It had a HUGE number of entries. 
Trying to even "ls" the directory would bring the system to its knees.  I 
had to use a number of tricks to drain the file swamp.

I never did find out how many files were there.  I know it was at least 
250,000.  Maybe more.

mh and nmh are mail readers that keep every message as a seperate file. 
It used to be painful to use when you have a huge number of messages in 
one folder.

>> files.

> mkfs.ext3/mke2fs and tune2fs:

I am pretty sure you have to set this by hand.  It is not backwards 
compatible to older kernels and it requires running "e2fsck -D" on the 
partition afterwards.  Not something you do on a whim.

The Pathologically Eclectic Rubbish Lister
A scripting language with delusions of full language-hood, Perl is used
in many system scripts and utilities.

This is a stripped down Perl with only essential libraries.  To make
full use of Perl, you'll want to install the `perl', `perl-modules' and
optionally `perl-doc' packages which supplement this one.

> say "supported", I am strictly speaking in the corporate sense.
>> If I do anything that voids the service agreement, that is unsupported.
>> It matters not a bit that I will never use the service agreement, ever.
>>
>> "Supported" also means that there is someone to sue if something goes
>> wrong.
>
> This is repeated so often I'm starting to think it's a myth. Has anyone ever
> actually sued a tech vendor? You'd think Microsoft would be the leader in
> lawsuits from unhappy customers.

It is the excuse used by management.  It does not mean it is true.  They 
like the idea of being able to hold someone "accountable", even if it is a 
delusional fantasy.

>From a programmer perspective, having source code is a much better 
assurance than to have someone I can point at and blame.

"Support" means I have someone who can answer questions if I get stuck and 
provide patches if it breaks.  I have both with Open Source.  In fact, I 
get MUCH better answers from the Open Source community than I have ever 
gotten from a proprietary vendor.

An example:

Back in the Windows 3.1 days, I was working on a DOS program written in C 
and real mode x86 assembly.  I was trying to port the code to the current 
Microsoft compiler toolchain.  I found a set of assembly language macros 
with a Microsoft copyright in them.  I called Microsoft support with what 
I thought was a simple question.  "Where was this macro documented and 
where did it come from?"

No one at Microsoft support could tell me.

I found it later on.  The macros were from a Windows SDK.  They were fully 
documented, but their "support" had no knowlege of their existence.

In my experience, support from proprietary companies is next to worthless.

On the other hand, Open Source allows me to know who wrote a program and 
who to talk to if it breaks or I find a bug.  I can talk to the 
programmer, not just some Stream employee with a standard sub-standard 
level of training on the product.  Not only that, I can get it fixed. 
When was the last time you could say that about a Microsoft product?

It is much more practical to use Open Source for critical applications. 
Anyone who says otherwise is trying to sell you something.

Rogan Creswick wrote:
> Obviously, try it before you leave the country, but they should just
> work.  That said, it sounds like you'll want one that's relatively
> easy to get in/out of, and one that supports usb2.

I wish "just work" was true in my experience, but unfortunately it's 
not.  One of my friends in Australia had a generic external hard disk 
enclosure that was USB 2.0 compatible; they bought it in Britain.  The 
enclosure worked fine on my computer but refused to work on his or 
another laptop.  The problem?  It would get recognized and work okay at 
first but after a little while it would just give itself up and die.  It 
would only do this on VIA chipsets.  The enclosure worked fine on every 
computer I tried (about 10), but anything VIA just died.  It didn't 
matter whether it was Linux or Windows.  It's probably not much of a 
problem anymore with the enclosure market mature, but I would warn to do 
some fairly robust tests (reading and/or writing a whole disk) just to 
be sure.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Oct 10 17:52:25 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 10 Oct 2006 18:52:25 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] K3B vs Command Line vs Anything Else
In-Reply-To: <452C3435.7040503@comcast.net>
References: <452C3435.7040503@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610101842580.9270@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

cdrecord is the command-line utility that most, if not all, GUI
frontends use to do the dirty work of burning a CD.  You can use
cdrecord from a shell very easily yourself.  To burn an .iso directly to
a disk:

cdrecord -v -eject dev=<cd-device> speed=<something-reasonable> -tao <filename.iso>

dev used to be a SCSI bus identifier (like "1,0,0").  with a 2.6 kernel,
it's far more likely to be an IDE-looking device (like "/dev/hdc").  Do
an "eject /dev/hdc").  If it ejects the CD tray, it'll probably work in
the cdrecord command.

For duping audio CDs, you need to use a combo of cdda2wav and cdrecord.
Here is a sample script:



#!/bin/bash

rip()
{
     rm -f *
     cdda2wav dev=/dev/cdrom -vall cddb=1 -B -Owav
}

wait()
{
     eject /dev/cdrom
     echo Place the blank CD in the drive and wait 30 seconds.
     echo
     sleep 25
     echo 5 seconds left...
     echo
     sleep 5
}

burn()
{
     cdrecord -eject -v dev=/dev/cdrom speed=16 -useinfo -text *.wav
}

cd /var/tmp/xcdroast

case "$1" in
     "rip")
         rip
         ;;
     "burn")
         burn
         ;;
     "all")
         rip
         wait
         burn
         ;;
esac

echo "Done!"
exit 0



Carlos Konstanski

> reading material on how to construct which command to achieve the same 
> results? I realize that's a big question, which is why I'm asking what manual 
> to read instead of what command to type. :-)
>
> Alternatively, or in addition, does anyone have a recommendation for a GUI 
> tool for burning CDs? I need to burn both data and audio CDs.
>
> And while on the subject of audio CDs, my recollection is that 700MB CD-Rs 
> work for audio. Do I remember correctly, or am I mistaken?
>
> Thanks.

General News

***2.6 Million Lines of Code
Announcing O'Reilly Code Search, where you can enter search terms to
find relevant sample code from nearly 700 O'Reilly books. The database
currently contains over 123,000 individual examples, comprises 2.6
million lines of code, all edited and ready to use. 
<http://labs.oreilly.com/code/>


***Smalltalk for Everyone Else
The Pragmatic Programmers advise learning a new language every year.
Instead of yet another Algol derivative, why not master object
orientation with perhaps the purest OO language ever devised? Smalltalk
and the Squeak environment offer a powerful and usable learning
experience; Keith Fieldhouse provides an introduction.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/onlamp/2006/09/21/learning_smalltalk.html>


***User Group Members Receive a Special 30% Discount on 
O'Reilly Learning Courses

<https://oreillylearning.com/enroll/>

---------------------
Open Source
---------------------
***Sharing Internet Connections
Protecting your computer when you're online is good. If you have
multiple computers in your home or small business, protecting all of
them is also good--especially if you can share your internet connection.
Dru Lavigne demonstrates how to allow other computers to use the network
safely with a FreeBSD or similar Unix system and fwbuilder.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/bsd/2006/10/05/FreeBSD_Basics.html>


***Scaling Dynamic Websites with Apache Modules
Yogesh Makwana and Syvum Technologies had a problem: their application,
hosted on a shared server, used too much CPU time and had grown
unresponsive. Moving to a virtual private server and using the
appropriate Apache modules and architecture brought their costs and
response times and load averages under control. This case study explores
their technique.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/apache/2006/09/28/httpd_scaling.html>

---------------------
Digital Media
---------------------
***Two Slick QuickTime Tricks
Turn your video on its ear! Our hands-on tutorial shows you how to work
with vertical movies and then embed them in a web page with a friendly
new kind of trigger.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/oreilly/digitalmedia/2006/09/29/two-slick-quicktime-tricks.html>


***Web Domination Using Photoshop
The time has come for designers to execute their plans to dominate the
World Wide Web with stunning graphics! Here, Corrie provides a full
arsenal of fundamental techniques, before launching you on a stellar
trajectory that takes in buttons in all their forms. With her
step-by-step instructions, handy hints, and secret tricks, you'll have
everything you need to bring the Web to its knees...in awe!
<http://www.sitepoint.com/article/web-domination-photoshop>

---------------------
Mac
---------------------
***Your New iTunes Movies to DVD (and Protect Your Other iTunes
Purchases)
How much money have you spent at the iTunes store? And what do you have
to show for it? An iPod full of music? TV shows? Those new games and
movies? Too many people forget how much cash they've put into their
digital media. And when bad things happen--house fires, computer theft,
iPod loss--they're not always prepared to be able to recover those
digital assets. Erica Sadun shows you archiving techniques to preserve
your (hefty) iTunes investment.
<http://www.macdevcenter.com/pub/a/mac/2006/09/19/burn-itunes-to-dvd.html>

---------------------
Windows/.NET
---------------------
***Better Registry Searching
Finding what you need in the registry is harder than trying to find a
needle in a haystack. Mitch Tulloch shows you how to find what you
need--fast!
<http://www.windowsdevcenter.com/pub/a/windows/2006/09/26/better-registry-searching.html>


***Windows Mobile Weekly Roundup 
Here's the weekly summary of a mix of Windows Mobile and general mobile
tech related items from Todd Ogasawara's blog.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/windows/blog/2006/10/windows_mobile_weekly_roundup_5.html>

---------------------
Java
---------------------
***What is Java Content Repository
You might have heard of JSR-170, but what is a content repository, and
what can you do with it? Well, do you want to manage documents with
versioning, search, access control, and more? Content repositories offer
these features, and JSR-170 codifies them into a single API. Sunil Patil
shows how to use the reference implementation--Apache Jackrabbit--to
create a blogging application.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/onjava/2006/10/04/what-is-java-content-repository.html>

---------------------
Sys Admin/Web
---------------------
***Managing a Honeypot
If intruders are always scanning the Internet for potential victims--and
they are--can you find the intruders and their exploits by putting up
fake networks that only a deliberate scan could find? That's the theory
behind honeypots. Peter Mikhalenko discusses the implementation, theory,
and legality of using a honeypot to protect your network.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/sysadmin/2006/09/28/honeypots.html>


***Survey Exposes The Four Web Dev Stereotypes--Which are You?
In what may be the largest survey of web developers ever, The State of
Web Development 2006/2007 exposes four key web professional stereotypes.
In this review, Nick explains how the stats were collated, and what they
revealed about the industry and those who work in it.
<http://www.sitepoint.com/article/survey-web-dev-stereotypes>

---------------------
MAKE
---------------------
***Enter the MAKE & CRAFT Halloween Contests
Makers and Crafters, it's time to enter our ghoulishly fun Halloween
contests! Anyone, anywhere can enter. Depending on what type of maker or
crafter you are, enter one or all of the following contests:
Hack-o-Lantern, Costume, Decorations, Gadgets and More, and
Treat-or-Treat.

Have your entries in before Saturday, Nov. 4, 2006, at 1:59 pm EST.
We're giving you a little time to take photos and get them up
post-Halloween, but you can enter any time starting now. Contest is open
to anyone on planet Earth or the surrounding orbit. Remember you can
enter any time just by tagging your photos on Flickr (flickr.com) with
"MAKECRAFTHALLOWEEN" and adding them to the MAKE and/or CRAFT photo
pools. To enter Trick-or-Treat just post about MAKE/CRAFT or the contest
on your blog.

For complete contest information, rules, and prizes, go to:
<http://www.makezine.com/blog/archive/2006/10make_craft_halloween_contests.html>


For More MAKE Projects and info, go to:
<http://makezine.com/>

---------------------
Podcasts
---------------------
***The Way of a Digital Photography Pioneer
In "Stephen Johnson on Digital Photography," the renowned photographer
writes about his journey on the "bleeding edge of digital photography's
evolution." In this podcast, Johnson talks to Sara Peyton about his
career, photo techniques, photo ethics in a digital age, and why, for
him, taking landscape photos became synonymous with caring about the
wild places he wandered in. (20 minutes 27 seconds), Stephen Johnson, a
digital photography pioneer and author of Stephen Johnson on Digital
Photography, discusses photo techniques, photo ethics in a digital age,
and why, for him, taking landscape photos became synonymous with caring
about the wild places he wandered in.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2006/09/29/digital-photography-pioneer.html>


***Listen to DerEngel(No Starch's "Hacking The Cable Modem") on 
NYC's WBAI-FM
DerEngel talks about how he collected the information for the book, his
background as a hardware hacker, the EFF's participation in reviewing
the book from a legal standpoint, and the importance of supporting free
speech in a corporate world. The cable companies may not be happy with
this book, but DerEngel and the WBAI host agree that "Hacking The Cable
Modem" contains useful info for understanding how Internet
broadbandworks and that there are curious tinkerers who just like to
learn new stuff, not commit malicious acts.
<http://www.2600.com/offthehook/2006/1006.html>


***TechTalkRadio Interviews David Pogue
Find out what to expect with the subject of one of David's upcoming
titles on the anticipated Microsoft Windows Vista and some other ways to
get info on tech from David Pogue. We'll also find out why David loves
the latest Tivo!
<http://www.techtalkradio.com/books/poque.htm>


**Creating Beautiful Digital Photos the Eddie Tapp Way
Eddie Tapp, 2006 Photoshop Hall of Famer, award-winning photographer,
and much sought after educator, discusses his new book from O'Reilly,
"Photoshop Workflow Setups." In this interview by new O'Reilly staffer
Sara Peyton, Eddie talks about his photography roots, the art of taking
pictures, his new book...and why he lives by the rule of 300. (15
minutes 33 seconds)
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2006/09/15/eddie-tapp-podcast.html>

From sofar at foo-projects.org  Wed Oct 11 08:10:21 2006
From: sofar at foo-projects.org (Auke Kok)
Date: Wed, 11 Oct 2006 08:10:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Unix Tip: TOO MANY FILES AROUND
In-Reply-To: <452D0681.30200@acm.org>
References: <200610111347.k9BDloLX012558@ugu.com> <452D0681.30200@acm.org>
Message-ID: <452D095D.5060702@foo-projects.org>

Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> This just came through today.  It appears it isn't just a Linux "Issue". 
> :-)
> 
> 
> Rod
 >
 >
 > $grep "ABC" *
 >
 > And it fails saying with
 >
 > ksh: /usr/bin/find: 0403-027 The parameter list is too long.
 >
 > what would you do?
 >
 > Well you can do the following:
 >
 > $ls |xargs grep "ABC"

That's rather funny - effectively it should do the same, except that xargs doesn't call 
the shell to pass the arguments. Apparently on that platform `ksh` is limiting the 
amount of arguments more than the platform itself, and I would definately consider that 
a ksh bug :)

It also exposes that ksh uses /usr/bin/find internally in a rather stupid way :)

Anyway, if you're really doing something like above, you should have used:

grep -r "ABC" .

which doesn't care how many files there are :)


Cheers,

Auke

Starting to scan for devices...
[ATA:1,0,0] SAMSUNG  CD-R/RW SW-252S  R951
[ATA:1,1,0] SAMSUNG  DVD-ROM SD-604   1.0
[ATAPI:0,0,0] SAMSUNG  CD-R/RW SW-252S  R951
[ATAPI:0,1,0] SAMSUNG  DVD-ROM SD-604   1.0
Scan finished.

I guess that means that it found what it needed. I'll have to read up on

From rsteff at comcast.net  Wed Oct 11 08:28:55 2006

	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610101842580.9270@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <452D0DB7.9000307@comcast.net>

Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> cdrecord is the command-line utility that most, if not all, GUI
> frontends use to do the dirty work of burning a CD.  You can use
> cdrecord from a shell very easily yourself.  To burn an .iso directly to
> a disk:
> 
> cdrecord -v -eject dev=<cd-device> speed=<something-reasonable> -tao 
> <filename.iso>

Thanks. That's the answer I was looking for. I'll go read up on cdrecord.

> dev used to be a SCSI bus identifier (like "1,0,0").  with a 2.6 kernel,
> it's far more likely to be an IDE-looking device (like "/dev/hdc").  Do
> an "eject /dev/hdc").  If it ejects the CD tray, it'll probably work in
> the cdrecord command.

SuSE 10.1 (and 9.x, too) use the 2.6 kernel, so my burner is at /dev/hdc.

> For duping audio CDs, you need to use a combo of cdda2wav and cdrecord.

Thanks, but I won't be duping CDs, I'll be recording LPs with gramofile 
and burning the resultant .wav files in audio format to CDs.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Wed Oct 11 11:40:07 2006
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Wed, 11 Oct 2006 11:40:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] External USB hard drive "cage" under linux,
	reccomendations?
In-Reply-To: <2e3ee48f0610101401q45139510j25d2800448b4f91@mail.gmail.com>
References: <2e3ee48f0610101401q45139510j25d2800448b4f91@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <ca5165530610111140r14fa953eu70e223254937d5a6@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/10/06, John pdx <john.erich at gmail.com> wrote:
> Any recommendations? I've found some Vantec options, does anyone have
> experience working with such setups under linux? I'm curious because I'm
> leaving for the Dominican Republic and am only taking my laptop, and to do
> work on low end desktop systems, I think such a gadget would come in handy
> for pulling drives in order to work on them locally instead of across the
> network or using a liveCD (banking on them having CD drives)
>

The only recommendation would be to make sure that you can provide external
power.  Most USB2 connections can provide enough power but if you happen
to plug it into a port that uses too small of a cable to allow current
to flow without
a significant voltage drop, the drive will not function and the whole
setup will be
worthless.

Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> cdrecord is the command-line utility that most, if not all, GUI
> frontends use to do the dirty work of burning a CD.  You can use
> cdrecord from a shell very easily yourself.  To burn an .iso directly to
> a disk:
>
>


Isn't cdrecord on its way out?  Debian has already replaced it (in fact, 
my update this morning to Etch removed the cdrecord package), and from 
what I understand, SuSE, Red Hat and others will follow suit.  Most 
distros have already been patching cdrecord heavily because of 
incompatibilities with upstream and the 2.6 series kernel.  Now I think 
they are giving up on it altogether.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Mon Oct  9 17:26:39 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Mon,  9 Oct 2006 17:26:39 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] NUTS, PERL, NFS incompatibilities...
Message-ID: <1160439999.452ae8bf1cccf@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Fedora Core 3 and Redhat 7.x seem to be largely incompatible...

I try to mount from Redhat 7.3 box an nfs partition exported on
a Fedora Core 3 box and end up getting a mount version error.

Slackware 10.1's perl isn't compatible with Redhat 7.3's...

I try to run an EasyTCP socket program between SW10.1 and RH7.3
box only to get a perl-Storable version mismatch error.

Centos 4.3 to Fedora Core 3 also gives me NFS trouble.  
CentOS 4.3 as the client produces a mount version error.
So much for netbooting Redhat 7.x boxes into CentOS to
back them up to a FC3 NFS share.  I thought FC3 and 
Centos 4.3 Final were essentially supposed to be the 
same distribution.

For people who run mixed enterprise and bleeding edge
Linux distros, don't they need to talk to each other?
Is there any simple way to ensure compatibly between
FC3 and other Redhat variants new and old?

What if I have an old system running Redhat 6.2 and I 
want it to be able to mount shares off of a FC3 box?

Michael C. Robinson

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Oct 11 15:08:40 2006
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 11 Oct 2006 15:08:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Unix Tip: TOO MANY FILES AROUND
In-Reply-To: <452D095D.5060702@foo-projects.org>
References: <200610111347.k9BDloLX012558@ugu.com> <452D0681.30200@acm.org>
	<452D095D.5060702@foo-projects.org>
Message-ID: <c834ec80610111508y5fbd82e3w37de8dac62f8a293@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/11/06, Auke Kok <sofar at foo-projects.org> wrote:
> Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
>  > Well you can do the following:
>  >
>  > $ls |xargs grep "ABC"
>
> That's rather funny - effectively it should do the same, except that xargs doesn't call
> the shell to pass the arguments. Apparently on that platform `ksh` is limiting the
> amount of arguments more than the platform itself, and I would definately consider that
> a ksh bug :)

As I understand it, using the pipe turns the list of command line args
into a fifo pipe from ls to xargs -- the pipe can then be any size (as
long as it can hold one file name).  xargs is then responsible for
managing storage of the entries, and (probably) processes them as they
come in.  It does the same thing, but in a very different way -- it
allows lazy evaluation of the arg list, in effect.

One should note that the above command will fail without passing "-0"
to xargs -- it will break on files / directories with spaces
otherwise.

ls | xargs -0 grep "ABC"

--Rogan

>
> It also exposes that ksh uses /usr/bin/find internally in a rather stupid way :)
>
> Anyway, if you're really doing something like above, you should have used:
>
> grep -r "ABC" .
>
> which doesn't care how many files there are :)
>
>
> Cheers,
>
> Auke

Why? Well, for starters, it insisted that I launch it first as root to
configure it. I did so, and there was a button to start it so that any
user could launch it. I did so. Then there was a button to go ahead and
launch it. I clicked on it and it launched it, but insisted that there
was no configuration file. After a while I found the config settings
and told it to save them. Then I exited and restarted, whereupon it
repeated that there was no config file. After three attempts I finally
got it to launch without that error message.

told me that it could not find any CD drives. I told it to rescan and
it still couldn't find the CD drive. Finally I added it manually. It
took a lot more fiddling around, but eventually I came to the
conclusion that it is not able to see my CD-DVD R+W drive. Oddly, when

empty. It shows an icon for the drive and everything, it just can't
read it. Also the Eject button doesn't work, more evidence that there
is a fundamental problem.

So, back to K3b and Gnomebaker. They read/write to the drive just fine.

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Wed Oct 11 15:43:03 2006
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Wed, 11 Oct 2006 15:43:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] NUTS, PERL, NFS incompatibilities...
In-Reply-To: <1160439999.452ae8bf1cccf@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1160439999.452ae8bf1cccf@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0610111543qb3f513uf69c538b4b31cb69@mail.gmail.com>

RH7.3 is "ancient", seriously consider upgrading.  If you can't,
prepare to build a new kernel and replace some core binaries so it is
at least _somewhat_ bugfixed, secure, and interoperable.

Warnings on doing an NFS mount between versions is "normal", as long
as the mount still takes place you're OK.  RPC/mountd/lockd/statd
versions change, versions of nfsutils/mount change, etc and generate
warnings.

I wouldn't say you're running bleeding edge there... RH6/7 and FC3 are
pretty much outdated.  At the very least, they're full of known issues
that have been fixed in recent distros/kernels/packages, since they
haven't had updates for a while.

Get some packet captures and strace output of the client when you
attempt these mounts, it should shed significant light on what's going
on.

Cheers,

/eli

|Laptop 1| <--1--> |Metrix A| <--2--> |Metrix B| <--3--> |Laptop 2|
