From daniel at digitree.org  Wed Oct 11 19:19:03 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Wed, 11 Oct 2006 19:19:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Seeking a Metrix Mark II / Pyramid Linux guru
In-Reply-To: <334c4450610111905u3c4890ch577239f9fddc5d6c@mail.gmail.com>
References: <334c4450610111905u3c4890ch577239f9fddc5d6c@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <452DA617.4020405@digitree.org>

Ben,

Benjamin Watson wrote:
> #ifup eth0
> udhcpc (v0.9.9-pre) started
> Sending discover...
> Sending discover...
> Sending discover...
> No lease, failing.
> Failed to bring up eth0.
> 
> I know my cables and networking gear are OK as my Windoze boxen can 
> obtain a
> DHCP address fine.

Have you considered the possibility of it being an issue with the 
firewall not letting DHCP packets through?  I'm not running Pyramid 
Linux, but the following might work to open a hole in the firewall for DHCP:
iptables -A INPUT -p udp --sport 67 --dport 68 -j ACCEPT
iptables -A OUTPUT -p udp --sport 68 --dport 67 -j ACCEPT

or you could try flusing your entire firewall:
iptables -F
iptables -P INPUT ACCEPT
iptables -P OUTPUT ACCEPT
iptables -P FORWARD ACCEPT

The above might not work...just depends if there is anything 
custom/special about how the distro has set up the firewall.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

>    pool-71-127-201-38.nwrknj.east.verizon.net[71.127.201.38] : Helo command
> rejected (need fully-qualified hostname) : 1 Time(s)
>    pool-72-64-88-9.dllstx.fios.verizon.net[72.64.88.9] : Helo command rejected
> (need fully-qualified hostname) : 1 Time(s)
>    pool-72-70-96-172.bstnma.east.verizon.net[72.70.96.172] : Helo command
> rejected (need fully-qualified hostname) : 1 Time(s)
>    pool-72-93-204-6.bstnma.fios.verizon.net[72.93.204.6] : Helo command rejected
> (need fully-qualified hostname) : 1 Time(s)
> 
> 
> 
> What rules describe what constitutes a fully qualified hostname?

The sending mail server needs to be able to identify itself using a real DNS name, 
otherwise we cannot return bounces and other problems to it. In the above case, I think 
that they didn't provide a proper name at all (HELO\n) or something useless (HELO 
localhost.localdomain\n).

> I am
> running a postfix-2.1.1 server.  Aren't A records supposed to be free of
> ip addresses?

An A record resolves a name into an IP. If you look at it that way, the name part is a 
required part in that ;)

> Are these DNS records most likely being put out by verizon 
> or are they complete duds?

Since the host didn't identify it properly postfix resolved the PTR record for that IP 
address, which gave something like pool-72-93-204-6.bstnma.fios.verizon.net. That's 
convenient since now you know that a verizon customer was trying to reach you and you 
can call verizon about abuse, etc.

> Which RFCs deal with HELO addresses and what they have to be?

don't know by heart, sorry :)

> Will IPv6 improve the dns infrastructure across the Internet at all?

unlikely :)

> Are these failed connection attempts most likely zombied Windows boxes?

of course! did you really think you had that many friends? ;)

> What are the most accurate ways to detect zombie hosts without breaking
> things?

ah! now that's a really good question.

I'm afraid there's no such thing as 'most acurate' as almost all methods have flaws in 
them. Being a bastard at smtp level by dropping bad HELO names, things like rfc-ignorant 
etc. all help, and some dnsbl lists seem to work ok (no false positives), most of them 
are still not good enough to my taste (spamassassin, bogofilter) to deploy on larger 
systems. SPF? not widely used and paranoia-driven if you ask me.

since kernel.org switched filters I'm getting 3-10 spams a day :/ (they switched to 
bogofilter afaik)

I prefer dropping and blocking people during the smtp session itself when badness occurs 
(I have a custom syslog daemon that drops IPs on certain messages). That does make some 
spam trickle through but prevents my servers being targeted by zombies directly, and 
gets rid of a lot of abuse quickly.

Cheers,

Auke

>easy to get in/out of, and one that supports usb2.

On Tue, Oct 10, 2006 at 03:16:00PM -0700, Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> I wish "just work" was true in my experience, but unfortunately it's 
> not.  One of my friends in Australia had a generic external hard disk 
> enclosure that was USB 2.0 compatible; they bought it in Britain.  The 
> enclosure worked fine on my computer but refused to work on his or 
> another laptop.  The problem?  It would get recognized and work okay at 
> first but after a little while it would just give itself up and die.  It 
> would only do this on VIA chipsets.  The enclosure worked fine on every 
> computer I tried (about 10), but anything VIA just died.  It didn't 
> matter whether it was Linux or Windows.  It's probably not much of a 
> problem anymore with the enclosure market mature, but I would warn to do 
> some fairly robust tests (reading and/or writing a whole disk) just to 
> be sure.

I had some issues with external USB cages and Linux, which could be
similar to the problems Daniel mentions.  It was a while ago, on 2.4
kernels, and may be resolved by now.  See:

     http://www.keithl.com/usb2bug.html

My approach for on-the-road backup for my IBM T30 is to use a hard
drive ultrabay adapter.  I power down the machine, unlatch the CD and
latch in the hard drive adapter. I reboot the machine in single user,
unmount all the partitions but /, and do a:

     sync ; dd bs=1M if=/dev/hda of=/dev/hdc

This copies the complete 80GB disk image at about 1GB/minute to an 
identical drive.  Since the second hard drive is easily removable
from the ultrabay adapter, it is easy to swap into the primary slot
if I lose the main drive while on the road.  I keep the spare drive
wrapped up in a ziploc bag in my dirty laundry for padding - I just
have to be careful not to wash it!  The drive plus the adapter is
very light compared to most external USB drives.

Keith

From daniel at digitree.org  Thu Oct 12 12:57:00 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Thu, 12 Oct 2006 12:57:00 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] PLUG Knoppix Problem with NTFS
In-Reply-To: <20061012193800.80167.qmail@web509.biz.mail.mud.yahoo.com>
References: <20061012193800.80167.qmail@web509.biz.mail.mud.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <452E9E0C.3020700@digitree.org>

Pomeroy Lab wrote:
> Hey I got a problem recognizing NTFS with Knoppix or any other Linux distro for that matter. It sees the drive but does not allow me to make changes, overwrite, or paste to the drive. I can copy from it.
> 
> 2 reasons I need to use NTFS.
> 
> 1. I want to work on XP computers from Live CDs.
> 2. I want to build a Linux Distro that can import or access existing files from Windows to share them (of course it would be nice if Windows could recognise Linux partitions too but lets stop dreaming).
> 
> If anyone has any advice on how to do this I would appreciate it.

NTFS write support is usually not allowed by default on most Linux 
distributions because it has the potential of damaging your Windows 
partition.  There is write support

Have a look at the following FAQ entry on Knoppix's website:
http://www.knoppix.net/wiki/Using_FAQ#Q:_How_can_I_write_data_on_NTFS_partitions.3F

The FAQ entry just above it is also useful as it explains how to make 
the partition writable with Knoppix 5.x.

As far as Windows detecting Linux partitions, it depends on the 
filesystem you are using for Linux.  EXT2/3 and ReiserFS can be accessed 
within Windows.  For ReiserFS, have a look at the external links at the 
bottom of http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ReiserFS .  For EXT2/3, have a 
look at the external links at the bottom of 
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ext2

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Thu Oct 12 14:25:26 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Thu, 12 Oct 2006 14:25:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: debugging hyperthreading problems
In-Reply-To: <e2aeccd60610121053n48145977g1fb79b77cfd36e0c@mail.gmail.com>
References: <e2aeccd60609211127r29cc48c2hceacb914db55898@mail.gmail.com>
	<e2aeccd60610121053n48145977g1fb79b77cfd36e0c@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1160688326.2580.26.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Thu, 2006-10-12 at 10:53 -0700, drew wymore wrote:
> Bringing this thread back to life. Still trying to figure this one out. I
> have ruled out SSH as using Telnet causes the same behavior with the second
> CPU enabled. However with both CPU's going and logging in locally the issue
> doesn't happen. So its something with sockets/bash or some other weirdness
> but I'm at a loss now. Someone at work suggested maybe a glibc problem but
> I'm running a recent version that supports NPTL and bash is using those libs
> and when I force standard linux thread libs it still has the same problem.
> 
> Anyone ideas?

Something funny is going on with interrupts, probably.

 o Have you tried a different NIC, preferably not on-board?
 o Have you tried pinging it, preferably more than the default 4 times 
   Windows ping does? You might find some odd variances in ping times 
   where there should be none.
 o If you can do a flood ping or otherwise send other traffic to the 
   host while making the connection and see if that causes it to not 
   happen--it sounds like a buffer somewhere isn't getting flushed as 
   often as expected and pumping data through might cause it to be 
   flushed.
 o Run Wireshark (nee Ethereal) while logging in and compare packet 
   timings, look for broken packets, etc.
 o Check /etc/profile, /etc/profile.d, /etc/bashrc to see if something 
   gets run or set on login.  You might temporarily enable tracing by 
   putting 'set -x' at the top of the /etc/profile and see if you can 
   pinpoint where it stops, if it actually stops.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc

From admin at mindblowingidea.com  Thu Oct 12 15:03:13 2006

I read this but I don't understand how to access, use, or turn on libntfs and fuse.
Do I also need to change the mode of read write to NTFS also? I believe I would. hmmm. Oh another thought. Does this work with SATA drives. I don't get the same icons for a HD as the ones shown on the FAQ page for a Hard Drive instead I get a USB device icon. Is this just a bug? I am suing Knopix 5.0 and 4 neither one seem to help me. I don't want the risks involved with 4. 5 sounds safer.


A: Knoppix 5 supports write access for NTFS (http://www.wikipedia.org/wiki/NTFS) partitions using libntfs and fuse. While preliminary results show generally good stability and a low likelihood of data corruption on the NTFS partition, these libraries are still beta and there is risk of data loss and disk corruption (back up your data first). Knoppix (and the Linux kernel) can read NTFS partitions just fine, the danger is only present when attempting to write to it. 
Older versions of Knoppix can be forced to write to NTFS partitions, but at great risk of scrambling the underlying NTFS filesystem (Captive NTFS (http://www.jankratochvil.net/project/captive/) used the existing NTFS drivers from a Windows system to acesss the NTFS drive, but was slow and unstable.)

plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
> Fedora Core 3 and Redhat 7.x seem to be largely incompatible...

We have a mixture of these OSes in our lab:

Redhat	7.2 through RHEL4 latest update
Mandrake/iva 8.0 through 2007
SuSE 8.2 through 10.x
FreeBSD 4.1 through 6.1
Solaris 2.6 through 10 on Sparc
Solaris 10 on x86
AIX 4.2 through 5.3
HP-UX 10.2 through 11.23

All of these above mount nfs partitions between each other, as long as 
you honor the nfs versions that each support. Some of the systems don't 
support nfs version 3 or 4. Some support nfs over tcp/ip, while others 
support udp, and others support both.

> I try to mount from Redhat 7.3 box an nfs partition exported on
> a Fedora Core 3 box and end up getting a mount version error.

I suspect the Redhat box is just asking, and the Fedora box is trying to 
serve a newer version than the Redhat box supports. But that's just a 
guess. Without more information, it's impossible to say.

> Slackware 10.1's perl isn't compatible with Redhat 7.3's...

I'd expect some incompatibilities when writing a perl script on a 10.1 
box, since the perl on slack 10.1 is probably 5.8.x, while on 7.3 it's 
5.004 or something... Lot's of changes in there. New features and stuff.

> I try to run an EasyTCP socket program between SW10.1 and RH7.3
> box only to get a perl-Storable version mismatch error.

Again, if perl-Storable has been updated in the last 5 years, then it 
probably has changes that your script doesn't take into account.

You have to write to the lowest common denominator. So if you must run 
the script on both system, write it on the oldest system. That doesn't 
guarantee anything, but I bet you'll have fewer issues to resolve.

> Centos 4.3 to Fedora Core 3 also gives me NFS trouble.  
> CentOS 4.3 as the client produces a mount version error.
> So much for netbooting Redhat 7.x boxes into CentOS to
> back them up to a FC3 NFS share.  I thought FC3 and 
> Centos 4.3 Final were essentially supposed to be the 
> same distribution.

Then why would they have two different distros? I use a knoppix CD to 
boot my x86 boxes and dd the drive to a RHEL4 nfs share. It works great.

http://www.dimstar.net

For laptop backups I use a usb laptop hard drive adapter.  It holds a 2.5"
laptop drive.  It is small enough to carry around.  It is made of aluminum,
so it does not add huge amounts of weight.  It also supports firewire on the
same enclosure. You can get laptop drives of at least 100 gigs these days.
(Just depends how much you want to spend.)

And it has a REAL bright light on it.

I picked up mine at Pacific Solutions for about $35.

> 
> 2 reasons I need to use NTFS.
>

The support is variable, but you could also try UDF. Linux can read and
write UDF (but fsck for UDF isn't in all distributions). I'm lead to
believe that W should also be able to do so (due to use on larger USB
keyfobs?).

> -cost

> experience,
> so I'd appreciate some wisdom from wizened I mean wise old Unix geeks.

Other things that come to mind:
-Open code means more security review
-Open code means more F/OSS applications that use deep-ish system hooks 
are available
-Open code means that if you run into a really nasty bug, you can edit 
the thing yourself (or hire someone to) and have a different, fixed, OS 
for an entire enterprise within hours. Just try that with HP/UX, SCO, 
etc.
-Choice and variation: Just try to find 30+ versions of any closed *nix 
that will cater to your needed languages, equipment style, desktops, 
etc., or allow you to make your own version.

-Bop

On 10/12/06, Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc> wrote:
>
> On Thu, 2006-10-12 at 10:53 -0700, drew wymore wrote:
> > Bringing this thread back to life. Still trying to figure this one out.
> I
> > have ruled out SSH as using Telnet causes the same behavior with the
> second
> > CPU enabled. However with both CPU's going and logging in locally the
> issue
> > doesn't happen. So its something with sockets/bash or some other
> weirdness
> > but I'm at a loss now. Someone at work suggested maybe a glibc problem
> but
> > I'm running a recent version that supports NPTL and bash is using those
> libs
> > and when I force standard linux thread libs it still has the same
> problem.
> >
> > Anyone ideas?
>
> Something funny is going on with interrupts, probably.
>
> o Have you tried a different NIC, preferably not on-board?
> o Have you tried pinging it, preferably more than the default 4 times
>    Windows ping does? You might find some odd variances in ping times
>    where there should be none.
> o If you can do a flood ping or otherwise send other traffic to the
>    host while making the connection and see if that causes it to not
>    happen--it sounds like a buffer somewhere isn't getting flushed as
>    often as expected and pumping data through might cause it to be
>    flushed.
> o Run Wireshark (nee Ethereal) while logging in and compare packet
>    timings, look for broken packets, etc.
> o Check /etc/profile, /etc/profile.d, /etc/bashrc to see if something
>    gets run or set on login.  You might temporarily enable tracing by
>    putting 'set -x' at the top of the /etc/profile and see if you can
>    pinpoint where it stops, if it actually stops.
>

From aaron at bavariati.org  Fri Oct 13 10:09:45 2006
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Fri, 13 Oct 2006 10:09:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] dumping unix for linux
In-Reply-To: <200610122027.49536.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200610122027.49536.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <20061013170945.GN9025@syrinxpc.com>

On Thu, Oct 12, 2006 at 08:27:49PM -0700, Carla Schroder wrote:
> I'm looking at reasons to migrate from Unix to Linux. So far I've come up 
> with:
> 
> -cost
> -flexibility
> -better feature set
> -dependency-resolving installers like apt and yum
> -runs on lower-cost hardware
> -not moldy

Dependency-resolving installers and packaging is *very* valuable.  At
OSDL we had a policy that we could rebuild a production system from
OS install onwards, automatically.  If there was software on the system,
it came from a package that was checked-in to CVS and listed in CFEngine.  
When you can clone or rebuild a prod. system from bare-metal in less
than an hour, it makes problems *much* less stressful.

I'm sure these are mostly explications of "cost", "flexibility" and "not
moldy", but here's mine: 
* Runs on a wide variety of hardware, incl. some old Unix boxes.  
* You can run the exact same OS & software on servers, dev-boxes and
  sandboxes.  There's usually no extra licensing charge, and the devs
  can actually work on usable desktop systems.  
* Runs on commodity (which means "available in standard form from a
  variety of vendors", not "cheap") hardware so you can comparison-shop,
  run competitive bids and buy precisely the hardware you need.
* Open Source means no End-Of-Life, ever, if you're willing to pay.
  Recent versions of popular packages & distros cost less to support, of
  course.
* You can rent generic linux boxes (or even VMs) in hosting centers
  at competitive prices instead of running your own datacenter.
* Linux is now the standard platform for most opensource applications;
  other platforms are less well-supported and often lagging.
* If you make a major conversion, you can throw out your overpriced
  monitoring and management software and substitute OSS stuff.
* Sysadmins and techs are *much* easier to hire.

And as some bearded older guy whose site I can't find anymore said,
Would you rather have proprietary vendors dragging you down, or dragging
down your competitor?

or Enderle, when we say something in public? No-one!" -- Danny O'Brien


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Oct 13 10:09:34 2006

On Thu, Oct 12, 2006 at 08:27:49PM -0700, Carla Schroder wrote:
> I'm looking at reasons to migrate from Unix to Linux. So far I've come up 
> with:
> 
> -cost
> -flexibility
> -better feature set
> -dependency-resolving installers like apt and yum
> -runs on lower-cost hardware
> -not moldy
> 
> *bsd or OpenSolaris are also possibilities. I don't have any Unix experience, 
> so I'd appreciate some wisdom from wizened I mean wise old Unix geeks.

As a wizened old Unix user (1975-1999), my main reasons for switching
to Linux were:

1) Rapidly developing and adaptive ( "not moldy?")

2) Books and online information and community.  Far more information on
   how most things actually work.

3) Open source.  Sometimes the code needs tweaking.  Sometimes you have
   to look at the source to see why things do what they do.

4) Control.  I am master of my own systems and software.  I choose upgrades
   and fixes on my own schedule and for my own needs.

5) Opportunities to give back to the community.  Open source users help
   each other, and helping others builds character.

These features were not available for Solaris, closed BSDs, and SVR4
variants when I was using them.  The deciding moment for me was when
I was unable to patch or replace some of the applications that were
Y2K noncompliant on a proprietary *nix.  So here I am...

Keith

Just don't rely on UDF for anything other than short-term 
storage/transfer.  The beginning of the end of my trust in proprietary 
software solutions was when I discovered I'd been hoodwinked by 
Adaptec/Roxio's fantabulous marketing claims for packet-writing to 
CD/RW.  "Use your CD-ROM drive just like a floppy drive and get 500X the 
storage capacity!"  Yeah ... how about, "Lose 500X the amount of data 
you can with a floppy!"

But then, I've never claimed that I've never been naive.


-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

On 10/12/06, Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> wrote:
> I'm looking at reasons to migrate from Unix to Linux.

  I'm an old Solaris admin, with a lot of time on HP-UX, Apollo
Domain, and Xenix systems.  I also served an extended sentence as a
System Programmer in an IBM mainframe shop.

  The biggest difference I see with OSS is that you are free from
vendor lock-in.  You don't have some sales vampire with their hand in
your back pocket.  I could carry on all night with horror stories from
my encounters with SUN, HP, and IBM sales staff.  It's been a decade,
but I *still* carry a cold place in my heart for HP-UX because of my
last encounters with their sales staff.

  To paraphrase a comment made by a Wall Street CIO.  He didn't give
damn what OS his server farm was running.  He switched from Solaris to
Linux because when he put out an RFP for server chassis, he had Dell,
Compaq, and IBM tripping over each other to give him the best quote.

<html>
<head>
<title> Sending mail </title>

<?php
echo "<p>Thank you, <b>".$_POST["name"]."</b>, for your message!</p>";
echo "<p>Your e-mail address is: <b>".$_POST["email"]."</b>.</p>";
echo "<p>Your message was:<br />";

$msg = "Name:    ".$_POST["name"]."\n";

$msg .= "Message: ".$_POST["message"]."\n";
//set up the mail
$recipient = "ted at charter.net";
$subject = "Form Submission Results";
$mailheaders = "From: My Web Site <defaultaddress at yourdomain.com> \n";
$mailheaders .= "Reply-To: ".$_POST["email"];
//send the mail
mail($recipient, $subject, $msg, $mailheaders);
?>
</body>
</html>

gotchas in K3B and xcdroast.

isn't something I have the energy to deal with. If I have something that
works that I paid for, like a laptop that plays DVDs using Windows XP
Home, I use it and I'm happy. When someone comes along like Sony did and
messes with that, I'm unhappy.


From daniel at digitree.org  Sat Oct 14 15:44:20 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Sat, 14 Oct 2006 15:44:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Spotty DNS Resolution
Message-ID: <45316844.6000507@digitree.org>

I am trying to track down the cause of some spotty DNS service, which 
has recently started causing me to lose emails from some people.

My DNS server seems to work fine in itself.  I'm using BIND 9.3.2.  I 
can use nslookup to query every single hostname in my zone file when I 
point the queries at my nameserver.  I can do this locally on the box 
that has BIND, from any other machine on my local network and from 
computers on the "outside".  I haven't tested enough from the outside 
yet to know for certain.  My DNS configuration seems fine and it is the 
same configuration files I used to use a year ago without any problems 
and the service responds as I would expect.

The problem then is that some DNS servers on the Internet get the 
correct DNS information from my server while others don't.  Sometimes 
the DNS server will get some of my DNS records but not all of them.  I 
have not updated my DNS records in a couple of months so it's not an 
issue with propagation time.

I'm using Dotster as my registrar and they require that I provide two 
fully qualified hostnames for my DNS.  I only have one DNS server so I 
provide the registrar with a hostname that doesn't have a DNS server on 
it, using another static IP address I own, but not on the same network.

I'm using DSL-Only as my ISP with a static IP address.  I have a Linksys 
WRT54G router that is set to forward all DNS requests to my nameserver; 
I am not using OpenWRT on this box, but  rather just the standard software.

To do some testing, I contacted two of DSL-Only's DNS servers, 
sulfur.dsl-only.net (63.105.16.4) and silver.dsl-only.net (63.105.16.2). 
  sulfur is able to correctly provide me with information for all of my 
DNS entries.  silver, on the other hand, can only provide me with 4 of 
about 13 records and returns failure on the other 9, including failure 
for my MX record.

Throughout my period of DNS woes, I have been receiving email from all 
of my mailing lists without any major hiccups.  I have been able to 
communicate with many companies in a timely fashion who have their own 
mail servers and have not noticed any missed communications (no 4 hour 
delays, etc).  Rich Shepard and one other individual written to me about 
problems DNS resolution problems from their end using public nameservers 
(Rich, care to elaborate?).  Google's Gmail used to work but now doesn't 
work for sending email to my address.  I'm still waiting for Gmail to 
report back an error message.  I haven't tested Yahoo or Hotmail.

My DNS server is authoritative and is the first entry in my list of DNS 
servers with my registrar.  It's not something silly like using a 
possibly unresolvable hostname, as I have entered a hostname that is not 
managed by my DNS server itself (ie. subxx-xxx.member.dsl-only.net.

I'm wondering if my Linksys router is to blame.  I might put OpenWRT on 
it in the middle of the night tonight if nobody else can offer up any 
suggestions (I have my configuration settings all ready to go from 
another Linksys box I had).

Any thoughts on where to start?

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From aaron at madebyai.com  Sat Oct 14 16:12:08 2006
From: aaron at madebyai.com (Aaron Ten Clay)
Date: Sat, 14 Oct 2006 16:12:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Spotty DNS Resolution
In-Reply-To: <45316844.6000507@digitree.org>
References: <45316844.6000507@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <200610141612.14676.aaron@madebyai.com>

On Saturday 14 October 2006 15:44, Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> I am trying to track down the cause of some spotty DNS service, which
> has recently started causing me to lose emails from some people.
<snip>
> I'm using Dotster as my registrar and they require that I provide two
> fully qualified hostnames for my DNS.  I only have one DNS server so I
> provide the registrar with a hostname that doesn't have a DNS server on
> it, using another static IP address I own, but not on the same network.

This is likely your problem. Most DNS servers these days, it seems, give up 
after about 2000ms if they can't get the info you want. Especially when just 
doing forwards. The DNS scheme is that you have multiple servers so if one is 
down/unreachable you can keep trying all the servers for a zone. Since most 
software doesn't seem to do that anymore, your listing a non-DNS server as a 
server for your domain is likely the cause of the problem. Resolvers are 
probably trying that one first, and giving up.

In cases where I've had no other options, I've run a second IP on one host and 
listed both IPs as the two nameservers for a domain. No redundancy, but at 
least all DNS requests are satisfied if the host is up (and any services on 
that host would be down if the host were down, anyway, negating any benefit 
to redundancy)

If all else fails, you could use everydns.net for a secondary server. I've 
used them for a while now without any problems. (Free/donation requested 
service)

-Aaron

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sat Oct 14 19:35:38 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 14 Oct 2006 20:35:38 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Spotty DNS Resolution
In-Reply-To: <45316844.6000507@digitree.org>
References: <45316844.6000507@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610142025470.8410@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

Instead of trying to put your IP address into the Nameservers box, you
should park the domain name on the Dotster DNS server, and then use
their Total DNS Control interface to edit THEIR DNS records to forward
all requests to your IP address, where you DNS server is waiting.  The
following two A records are all you need (substitute your own IP
address):

HOST    Points To       TTL
----------------------------
@       70.168.39.139   3600
*       70.168.39.139   3600

The only users who can put meaningful informatin into the Nameservers
boxes are those who own large blocks of IP space handed out by ICANN.
Someone, somewhere, has to resolve your domain name to an IP address.
If ICANN isn't doing it, then you need to have Dotster do it.

I'm not a Dotster user, so I do not know what their web interface looks
like.  I have seen only 1PlanHost and DirectNic, whose interfaces are
identical to one another.  One key difference between the two: 1PlanHost
supplies the Total DNS Control feature for no added cost, while
DirectNic charges an extra 5 bucks a year.  I had to hunt around for it,
and haul out my credit card.

Carlos Konstanski

From ehem at m5p.com  Sat Oct 14 20:30:08 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sat, 14 Oct 2006 20:30:08 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] K3B vs Command Line vs Anything Else
In-Reply-To: <20061014190005.6B2B64629@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200610150330.k9F3U84p057281@m5p.com>

> From: Paul Mullen <pm at nellump.net>
> On Fri, Oct 13, 2006 at 02:38:55PM -0700, Michael M. wrote:
> > The beginning of the end of my trust in proprietary software
> > solutions was when I discovered I'd been hoodwinked by
> > Adaptec/Roxio's fantabulous marketing claims for packet-writing to
> > CD/RW.  "Use your CD-ROM drive just like a floppy drive and get 500X
> > the storage capacity!"  Yeah ... how about, "Lose 500X the amount of
> > data you can with a floppy!"
> 
> Another sucker^H^H^H^H^H^Hproud owner of several useless DirectCD
> discs, eh? I feel your pain. And now that I think about it, I'm pretty
> sure I just gave away the last computer I had that could read those
> coasters. I hope there was nothing valuable on them. :-(

I certainly wouldn't use RW media and UDF for long term archival storage,
but for short term they appear to work well. UDF has sparing tables to
compensate for sectors that die so you shouldn't lose files. UDF also has
the advantage of being very widely supportted so various OSes can read
the filesystems (useful for a removable hard disk).


> From: Carlos Konstanski <ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com>
> Doesn't the linux kernel support packet writing?  I've never used it
> (for reasons like those mentioned already), but I see the option in the
> kernel config.  Perhaps someone out there can actually explain how to
> make use of it.

Yes. If you're lucky and have a drive with good firmware, you won't even
need that option (the +RW drives are supposed to simulate 2K granularity
rewritable sectors, but not all drives actually do). For the majority,
enable that option.

For CD-RWs and DVD-RWs:
cdrwtool -d /dev/cdrw -q (burns the entire media so several minutes)

For DVD+RW:
dvd+rw-format (takes 30 seconds, the + group did do neat things with RW)


modprobe pktcdvd (assuming it is a module)
pktsetup foo /dev/dvdrw (early 2.6 foo needs to be a number, later foo is
an arbitrary string)
mount -t udf /dev/pktcdvd/foo /mnt/mnt -o noatime
(have fun)
umount /mnt/mnt
pktsetup -d foo (no "/dev/pktcdvd/" on the begining!)


For good drives the use of `pktsetup` isn't even needed, directly mount
the device. I'm unsure whether the last step is supposed to be required,
but the drive tends to get locked if you don't do it.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Oct 14 22:44:49 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sat, 14 Oct 2006 22:44:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Syntax to connect to network drive
Message-ID: <20061014224449.4b110996.johnxj@comcast.net>

This has bugged me for a long, and I've not been able to learn how to
do it.

At home I have ethernet, on which I connect my Windows 2000 desktop and
my Ubuntu Dapper laptop. Both computers also go through a switch and
router to the cable modem. It all works just fine (normally).

However, I also have a 250 GB USB disk hanging on the Windows desktop.
I created two partitions on it, which the desktop sees as F and G. I
formatted F as NTFS and have been using it as backup media for the
Windows desktop. That part works fine. I formatted the other partition
as FAT32 and I want to use it as backup media for the laptop. 

I can create an rsync or tar command to do the backup from the laptop.
But on the command line I cannot get the syntax right for rsync to see
and write to the USB disk. Note: Nautilus sees it fine; SAMBA is
working perfectly. The problem is the command line syntax. Lots of //
and :: and stuff that I can't get right. I've read documention on this
for a long time and I can't figure out how many /'s to use and
where to put them. Also, when I access the drive with Nautilus I always
have to pop in my username and password for the Windows desktop, so I
suppose that must be handled as well. Or maybe that will automatically
pop up once I finally figure out how to tell rsync where to copy the
files to. It's the syntax for specifying a network partition that I
can't get right.

The laptop is Devil5 and the desktop is Devil4. So the destination
should be something like //Devil4/G:/Backups/ -- except that doesn't
work, so I must have it wrong. (/Backups is a folder I created on the
G:/ partition on the USB disk.)

I'm sure those of you who work in networking environments can't believe
that someone could not just automatically understand this, but I can't.
Any help is welcome. 


From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sat Oct 14 22:53:34 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 14 Oct 2006 23:53:34 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Syntax to connect to network drive
In-Reply-To: <20061014224449.4b110996.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20061014224449.4b110996.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610142347520.30396@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

First, mount the USB volume:

     mkdir /mnt/smb
     smbmount "//<windows-box-netbios-name>/g$" /mnt/smb

Then rsync to /mnt/smb/Backups.  If ubuntu does not have a smbmount
command, look for mount.smbfs or the like.

Note the g$ notation.  Winblows uses $ for hidden shares.  By default,
every volume can be accessed this way.  Try it on your co-workers'
machines!

Carlos Konstanski

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sun Oct 15 09:42:04 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sun, 15 Oct 2006 10:42:04 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Syntax to connect to network drive
In-Reply-To: <20061014235534.51c061b2.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20061014224449.4b110996.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610142347520.30396@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
	<20061014235534.51c061b2.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610151021030.30396@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

Uh, yeah, sorry about forgetting to mention you need to be root.  My
bad.

I'd look for smbmount or mount.smbfs with apt-cache search.  You really
want to use one of these wrapper programs, rather than going the "mount
-t smbfs" route.

Can you access the share with smbclient?  That would be a good
troubleshooting measure to take.  Maybe also try konqueror or nautilus,
if you're a GUI guy.  Using these tools will help you identify share and
NTFS permission issues.  Remember, BOTH share AND NTFS permissions come
into play with winblows shares.  You get the more restrictive of the
two.

Can you access the share from another winblows box on your network?  If
not, time to haul out ethereal (now called wireshark).  Filter for TCP
ports 138 and 139, and UDP ports 137 and 138.  Let a packet trace run
for quite a while (a half hour).  Then look for all packets whose
protocol is "BROWSER".  See if anything looks fishy.  For instance, are
two or more machines battling for master browser?

The kernel config needs to be checked for CIFS support.  In "make
menuconfig":

File systems  --->
     Network File Systems  --->
         <*> SMB file system support (to mount Windows shares etc.)
             [*]   Use a default NLS
             (cp437) Default Remote NLS Option
             <*> CIFS support (advanced network filesystem for Samba, Window and other CIFS compliant servers)
             [*]   CIFS statistics
             [*]     CIFS extended statistics
             [*]   CIFS extended attributes
             [*]     CIFS POSIX Extensions
             [ ]   CIFS Experimental Features (EXPERIMENTAL)

This is how I have my CIFS options set, and it works (even in an AD
domain).  Some settings may be optional.

You are using an appliance router, correct?  In this case, it is highly
unlikely that your machines are getting weird netbios settings from
DHCP.  But it might be worth a check.  For instance, DHCP can tell
clients where to find the wins server, and also whether they should
be allowed to broadcast or not.

The above steps are not in proper order.  I would check the kernel
config first, as that is quick and easy to do, and you'll get nowhere if
it isn't right.  It may be that smbclient (and its GUI companions,
konqueror and nautilus) don't require kernel support.  These may be
tried first as well.  Calling in another winblows box would be dead last
on my list.

Carlos Konstanski

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sun Oct 15 10:19:52 2006

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610151118130.30396@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

I think that your situation proves that nautilus (and konqueror and
smbclient) do not mount anything; tht is, they do not employ the kernel
CIFS filesystem support.  Rather, nautilus has its own implementation of
samba-speak.

So off to the kernel config you go!

Carlos Konstanski

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Sun Oct 15 13:30:33 2006
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Sun, 15 Oct 2006 13:30:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] giftopnm et al
In-Reply-To: <1640024710.1160942502744.JavaMail.root@fepweb04>
References: <1640024710.1160942502744.JavaMail.root@fepweb04>
Message-ID: <200610151330.33722.carla@bratgrrl.com>

On Sunday 15 October 2006 13:01, tkubaska at charter.net wrote:
> How do I get the giftopnm, pnmscale and ppmtogif utilities.? They didn't 
come with my Ubuntu Dapper and it's not clear to me what I should download 
from the Synaptic Package Manager. Thanks.
>  -Ted

apt-file finds out what files belong to what package:

$ apt-file search giftopnm
netpbm: usr/bin/giftopnm
netpbm: usr/share/man/man1/giftopnm.1.gz

Before you run apt-file for the first time, set it up with this command:

$ apt-file update

Re-run this every so often to keep it up to date.

Carla

I have found that what management really cares about is cost. Cost of
migrating, cost of licensing, cost of hardware, and ongoing cost of
ownership. If you can spell things out in dollars and cents, they will go
for it. They probably don't care that penguins are cute. Although, you are
right... penguins are cute.

From alan at clueserver.org  Mon Oct 16 10:23:38 2006

On Mon, 16 Oct 2006, Robert Anderson wrote:

> I have found that what management really cares about is cost. Cost of
> migrating, cost of licensing, cost of hardware, and ongoing cost of
> ownership. If you can spell things out in dollars and cents, they will go
> for it. They probably don't care that penguins are cute. Although, you are
> right... penguins are cute.

I have found that to be false in most cases.  Otherwise why would anyone 
ever buy a Windows print server CAL?

What they want is security.  They want to go with what makes them 
comfortable, no matter how much more it costs them.  If they believe that 
Windows will give them that "warm and fuzzy experience", then they will go 
with Windows.  For some shops it is Solaris.  Others it is Oracle.

Cost is rarely the number one reason for any management choice.  They are 
managers, not accountants.  (Besides...  If they don't spend all of their 
budget, they get less next year.)

> > cents, they will go for it. They probably don't care that penguins 
> > are cute. Although, you are right... penguins are cute.
> 
> I have found that to be false in most cases.  Otherwise why would 
> anyone ever buy a Windows print server CAL?
> 
> What they want is security.  They want to go with what makes them 
> comfortable, no matter how much more it costs them.  If they believe 
> that Windows will give them that "warm and fuzzy experience", then 
> they will go with Windows.  For some shops it is Solaris.  Others it 
> is Oracle.

Sometimes the "warm and fuzzy experience" has to do with indirect (but 
real) financial expenses...

 * avoidance of official IT infrastructure is a financial expense,
   because it means that employees are having to spend time to
   develop their own solutions, and usually not very efficiently.

 * employees who stovepipe their own IT solutions often do so using
   tools that aren't designed with corporate security in mind.
   whether the problem is information leaks or inbound viruses,
   cleanup is a financial expense.

 * spending time answering questions from cranky users or addressing
   problems introduced by software that doesn't work as most
   employees think it should is a real financial cost.

...but sometimes the cost isn't financial...

 * getting berated in management meetings or losing status in an
   organization sometimes involves financial costs (less budget
   next year), but often the cost is psychological.

 * there's also a high psychological price to pay by IT staffers
   who perceive that their work is being dismissed by their
   co-workers; it's harder to work with people in a "bad mood."

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

I was speaking of IT management.

When I worked for NCD, there were cheaper, more stable solutions.  But 
because of comfort issues we went with the more expensive options.

And they paid for it too.  Most of the time was spent keeping the three 
multi-user NT boxes and the one Outlook server running.  The rest of the 
Unix boxes were managed to require little heavy lifting.

But there is always the assumption that they people making the decisions 
are doing so for rational reasons and not just what they last read in the 
management magazines or something they read while traveling.

More IT decisions are based on what an article said than it was by asking 
someone who actually understood all of the issues.

From tkubaska at charter.net  Mon Oct 16 11:58:13 2006
From: tkubaska at charter.net (tkubaska at charter.net)
Date: Mon, 16 Oct 2006 11:58:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] dumping unix for Linux
Message-ID: <1008910296.1161025094256.JavaMail.root@fepweb03>

About the UNIX/Linux issue ...

What I've seen is companies wanting to contract and not hire support staff. Reasons are I surmise that they don't want full-time people that are not directly generating revenue and it's much easier to let a contract expire or cancel one than it is to lay off employees.

Companies that used to have full-time security people and full-time building maintenance, now often contract that out.

There are support Linux companies that one can contract with, but (and please correct me if I'm wrong) they appear to be small ops and not general comp support -- that is, you contract for specific apps and configs.

This discussion about dumping UNIX for Linux brings up Microsoft and I don't think Microsoft is doing UNIX support currently, but then I don't really know.

I'm sure this is not the only issue ... many other valid ones brought up here. But it's definitely part of the mix.
 -Ted

On Mon, Oct 16, 2006 at 11:58:13AM -0700, tkubaska at charter.net wrote:
> 
> There are support Linux companies that one can contract with, but
> (and please correct me if I'm wrong) they appear to be small ops
> and not general comp support -- that is, you contract for specific
> apps and configs.

Uh ... IBM?  Novell?  Redhat?  Expensive, but quite large and
general.  If you mean there are no large local Linux support shops
like there is for M$, that is true but not really important (unless
you are looking for a job in such a shop).

There are some perfectly adequate general-purpose local Linux support
companies;  there is a list at www.possepdx.org .  Some of them will
grow quite large in time, as the need appears;  there is certainly
enough local talent to grow out of.  But one real advantage of Linux
compared to other non-Unix systems is that it can be securely supported 
from anywhere.  I don't /need/ any more than a local screwdriver jockey
to physically maintain systems.  Software/application wise, experts in
Europe or Asia can (in principle) maintain my machines as well as an
expert in Tigard can.  I contract locally because good talent is here,
and I suspect that the talent suppliers will grow faster than I can. 
The fact that I /like/ these folks as well is a pure bonus.

Keith

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Oct 16 13:12:07 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 16 Oct 2006 13:12:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Wireless Cards - (was "Linux Clinic today")
In-Reply-To: <20061015181115.bc07d0c1.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20061015162620.GA2864@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<20061015181115.bc07d0c1.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061016201207.GB18318@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sun, Oct 15, 2006 at 06:11:15PM -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> Another fine clinic! 

And John is instrumental in making that happen.  One thing, though;
we are all there to have fun, and solving small Linux problems is
fun.  Solving huge problems is work, and not fun.


One of the larger problems we have is people bringing in odd bits
of hardware they picked up cheap and expecting us to get them going.
The most common example of such hardware is cheap Frys wifi cards
for laptops.  We have good success with Atheros G chipsets and with
PRISM-II B chipsets, but most cheap cards use Windoze-only chipsets
with poor or nonexistent Linux support.  That is the nature of
Wifi cards, and it is rooted in FCC requirements supported by
proprietary vendors.  That said, some vendors "get it", and you
should support them.  You have to do some study of individual card
types, and the chipsets they use, BEFORE you buy them.  If you don't
know that a card will work, DON'T BUY IT, and DON'T expect the clinic
volunteers to compensate for your carelessness.  There is plenty
of good wireless hardware that you can buy with good manufacturer
support for Linux.  Here are two good lists of working chipsets:

  http://www.seattlewireless.net/HardwareComparison
  http://www.linux-wlan.org/docs/wlan_adapters.html.gz

And if that is too hard, just look for Senao NL-2511 (PRISM-2.5) on
the web and buy those.   Cheap and good range.  They are not G cards,
but how often does the wireless access point connect to something
faster than 11 Mbps anyway?  

If your laptop has builtin wireless, you might be able to get that
running with some work.  However, laptop builtin chipsets are often
obscure (we spent 4 hours fighting a Broadcom BCM43xx chipset), and
even if you get them running they are typically low power with limited
range.  Personally, I prefer a card that I can pull out and keep in
my pocket - then I can be sure I am offline, whether or not my software
has been damaged, and I am not wasting battery power while offline.

You can get many windows drivers to work with something called 
"ndiswrapper" - sometimes that works, often it does not.  It is just
one more thing that can go wrong.

The Linux clinic is currently being held at FreeGeek;  wireless service
there is "special".  Most of the time it works good, but if there is
someone in another part of the building using wireless then the diversity
antennas may point at them rather than you.  There are other open APs
nearby that can interfere.  In general, it is not a very good place
to debug Linux wireless, and most of the volunteers are not wireless 
experts.  Fortunately, I was able to help one fellow debug an Atheros
chipset DLINK WNA-2330 card (after we fixed yum and found a spot in
the building that would connect) but two other fellows were not so
lucky, and there was much hair pulling and gnashing of teeth.  

One way to deal with wireless cards and laptops would be to schedule
quarterly "wireless days", perhaps in conjunction with the Personal
Telco Project.  We would want a venue where we control the wireless 
environment (our own AP, no others in range), good connectivity (for
looking up things on the web, and downloading drivers) and plenty of
wireless savvy people (Tom Higgins, Don Park and Russell Senior 
spring immediately to mind, but there are plenty of others).  This
would not be a monthly thing.

Also, since the lists above are kind of old, it would be handy to
do a monthly "store scan" to update a list of currently available
wireless cards at Frys and Compusa and Bestbuy (and other stores
that carry them) and then research them for chipsets and drivers.
That does not require a rocket scientist, anyone who wants to 
contribute to the community can do that.  We can divide cards into
"easy", "difficult", and "impossible".  Impossible cards will be
taken out to the sidewalk and stomped.  You Have Been Warned.

And for built-in wifi - assume that doesn't work.  If you get it
working, that's wonderful.  Write down what you did and share it
with others.  But just because somebody somewhere got the built-in
working with their own particular setup, that doesn't mean we can
get YOURS working in just a few hours.  It is far easier to buy a
card that lots of people have gotten working with many versions of
Linux.  You paid >$500 for the laptop; be a mench and spend >$40 
for a wireless card.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From dmandel at pdxLinux.org  Mon Oct 16 14:23:44 2006
From: dmandel at pdxLinux.org (David Mandel)
Date: Mon, 16 Oct 2006 14:23:44 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] dumping unix for linux
In-Reply-To: <1160754804.524.8.camel@willow.odshp.com>
References: <200610122027.49536.carla@bratgrrl.com>
	<1160754804.524.8.camel@willow.odshp.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0610161401230.8024@PPC.DavidMandel.com>


I agree 100% with Wil.  It is the APPLICATIONS that count.
If this wasn't true I would never ever run Windows.

Linux is easier to use than most other Unix OSs, and it 
has tons and tons of ready to load applications available
for it.  Has a result, it makes a wonderful desktop for 
lots of people.  I personally prefer Linux to Windows
or Mac OS because:

    -  It is easy to administrate
       (This is also true of any good Unix.
       Some people even think Windows is easy to administrate, but I 
       have trouble.)
    -  It is easy and friendly to use 
       (at least for a power user like most PLUG members)
       Most other Unix OSs have desktops similar to Linux, but they
       aren't quite as nice.
    -  There are tons of OpenSource applications for Linux
       (These can be ported to most any Unix or even Windows, but they 
       are usually ready to run on my Linux desktop without any pain at all.)
    -  Linux networks well with any Unix machine
       This makes it easy to use specialized proprietary applications on
       proprietary Unix computers without the expense of giving everyone
       a proprietary Unix computer.
    -  Linux sort of works with Windows thanks to products like
       Samba, Wine, and VMWare.
       This isn't perfect, but it is better than many proprietary Unix 
       computers do.

On the other hand, I would never restrict myself entirely to Linux.
Many applications (mostly proprietary) are only available for Windows
or some proprietary Unix.  If you need SAS or ArcInfo you need to 
run something other than Linux.  Moreover, Linux is getting more
and more stable, but it still lacks features some people need in
enterprise computing.  At least, I think it does.  This becomes less
true everyday.

From slinkywizard at integraonline.com  Mon Oct 16 14:19:08 2006
From: slinkywizard at integraonline.com (slinkymax0r)
Date: Mon, 16 Oct 2006 14:19:08 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] dumping unix for linux
Message-ID: <12353.64.122.164.5.1161033548.squirrel@webmail.integraonline.com>



>> The biggest difference I see with OSS is that you are free from
>> vendor lock-in.

In theory yes, in practice the results are a bit more murky, at least in
present circumstances.  RedHat support isn't cheap, and the lock-in isn't
any less substantial in most cases.

It works like this:

An ERP app requires Oracle to run.

Oracle is supported on X,Y,Z (commerical, pay for support) linux distros,
Solaris and Windows.

No one in their right mind runs Oracle on Windows.

 Since the stacks are vertically integrated, it's basically a toss up
between Solaris and Redhat and/or SuSE if you want support from both
Oracle and the ERP vendor.  Regardless of the kool-aid you end up
drinking, you're probably going to end up spending roughly the same
amount;  uptime, stability and CYA still costs $.

Coincidentally, this is probably why Larry Ellison wants to create his own
Linux Distro.

Regards,
Max

If you run a windows shop, and have no expertise in linux or unix, then a
"Windows print server CAL" may be a more cost effective solution that open
source alternatives. All software has a cost, even if the licensing is free.
In some cases, it is more cost effective to buy software with a big price
tag from a vendor than it is to use an open source solution or to develop an
in-house solution. Management like to rely on vendor provided support for
the "warm fuzzy feeling" you describe. This is directly related to the cost
of ownership. Security, in the sense that you mention, is very important.
Again, I think this is related to cost of ownership. Managers like to be
able to predict costs. If they know there is a licensing and support cost
that is payable to a vendor, it becomes a line item in the budget. If you
decide to use an open source solution, your cost is a big question mark. How
much will it cost to implement, support, fix bugs, resolve issues? Sure, you
can estimate these cost, but what if you're wrong? How much variation
between the estimated cost and the actual cost can be tollorated. These
things play a huge deciding factor when management chooses a solution.

Rob Anderson
riznob at gmail.com

Only if one considers IBM and HP as "small ops."  Debian was originally
supported primarily by Pixar Animation Studios of all places, and when
Software in the Public Interest took over the Debian Project, HP became the
big sponsor and was Debian's primary financial sponsor for almost a decade.

> This discussion about dumping UNIX for Linux brings up Microsoft and I
> don't think Microsoft is doing UNIX support currently, but then I don't
> really know.

Microsoft forked XENIX off AT&T UNIX System V in 1979, support ended in 1987
when Microsoft sold XENIX to the orignal SCO (now known as Tarantella, Inc)
so they could focus on OS/2 and Windows.

The nice thing is Microsoft announced two months back that Vista will be
Microsoft's last operating system so they can focus on virtualization and
their console products.  Good riddance!

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Mon Oct 16 16:37:12 2006
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Mon, 16 Oct 2006 16:37:12 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Spotty DNS Resolution
In-Reply-To: <453177B6.8030902@digitree.org>
References: <45316844.6000507@digitree.org>
	<200610141612.14676.aaron@madebyai.com>
	<453177B6.8030902@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0610161637yff3c934k73e198a847a7deef@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/14/06, Daniel Hedlund <daniel at digitree.org> wrote:
> Aaron Ten Clay wrote:
> > This is likely your problem. Most DNS servers these days, it seems, give up
> > after about 2000ms if they can't get the info you want. Especially when just
> > doing forwards. The DNS scheme is that you have multiple servers so if one is
> > down/unreachable you can keep trying all the servers for a zone. Since most
> > software doesn't seem to do that anymore, your listing a non-DNS server as a
> > server for your domain is likely the cause of the problem. Resolvers are
> > probably trying that one first, and giving up.
>
> Timeouts could be a very likely issue and may be my main problem.  There
> is always a possibility on the server that the DNS response might lag by
> a couple of seconds.  This is because I don't have very much memory on
> the machine and some other services on the box might cause BIND to swap
> out of memory at the wrong time.  Good suggestion; not much I can do
> about it besides get more memory or move it to another box (ie. OpenWRT
> router or something).
>

Your main problem is that you listed a non-nameserver as nameserver
for your domain.  Resolvers will randomly choose one of the
nameservers listed for the domain.  They do not try the first and
fallback to the second. If they choose the non-responsive one, they
will wait for the timeout (I think it is 2 sec) until they try the
other one.  Resolvers may be smart enough to cache the fact that one
of the nameservers was non-responsive and use the other one for later
queries.  But the first query for the domain has a 50% chance of being
really slow.

 - Ian

your corporate products, you'll find that it's down to redhat or suse.
Oracle may support redhat, suse and asianux, while vmware supports all
these plus a few more, but IBM and Dell only support redhat and suse,
period.  The support number you're most likley to call is the one for
your hardware vendor.

From ckonstanski at sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com  Sat Oct 14 19:12:15 2006
From: ckonstanski at sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 14 Oct 2006 20:12:15 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Spotty DNS Resolution
In-Reply-To: <45316844.6000507@digitree.org>
References: <45316844.6000507@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <17713.39167.782788.60299@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

I think you are using the Dotster admin tool incorrectly.  You should
not use the 2 hostname text boxes to enter your DNS server location.
These are meant for those customers who own large blocks of IP address
space, given out by ICANN.  A private individual like you or me will
never be able to play ball in this league.  If I am mistaken, and you
are a company who has a block of IP space doled out by ICANN, then my
bad.

Instead, you should look for a feature caled "Total DNS Control" or
something similar.  I use 1PlanHost (Windows servers, I know, but I
started using them years ago and now I'm stuck).  A friend of mine who
uses DirectNic has the identical-looking admin interface.  This makes
me think that the interface is common to many domain registrars.
Anyway, the idea is this: instead of telling the internet that your
DNS server is the primary nameserver for your domain, you should
instead let Dotster be the primary DNS server, and use the tools
provided to customize their DNS records to simply point all requests
to your IP address.

I have ten domain names set up with 1PlanHost, and they all have DNS
records that look exactly like this:

A RECORDS:
@  	70.168.39.139  	3600
* 	70.168.39.139 	3600

Notice the lack of CNAMEs and MX records.  These two A records suffice
to send all requests directly to my IP address, into the arms of my
DNS server.  Every request for one of my domain names goes to
1PlanHost first, and then to me.

Where you attempted to put your DNS server information, you should
instead click the "Default Parked Nameservers" button, thereby letting
Dotster be the "real" DNS server.  Once you park your domain name(s)
on Dotster's DNS server, you can edit their records as described
above.

I get the Total DNS Control feature for no extra charge with
1PlanHost, but my DirectNic buddy had to pay an extra $5 to get the
feature, even though the web interface is identical.  I had to hunt
around a bit to find where I could purchase the feature for him.

would like some, I can send an email directly to you (provided you can
receive it).

Carlos Konstanski

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sun Oct 15 14:18:50 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Unknown)
Date: Sun, 15 Oct 2006 14:18:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Version creep and patching...
Message-ID: <1160947131.28430.50.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

What are the major problems being found in perl that perl 5.008
is not very compatible with perl 5.8.0?  Isn't a newer version of 
a program generally supposed to be compatible with older versions?

I often hear, "you have to upgrade or you are insecure."  Well, some
hardware won't run the newest version of Linux.  The Linux community
needs to reign in the bloat and realize that increasing complexity in
the kernel is starting to become a major problem for people trying to
use it effectively.  I'd say that inadequate documentation for the 2.4
kernel is a problem that has carried over to 2.6 in spades.  How many
people wonder if they have something when attempting to configure the
2.6.18 kernel and don't know what decision to make?  If that which is
not in the kernel tarball to explain the choices is on the net, what
good does that do anyone?  With sufficient bloat, not everyone can go 
to the Net to download everything they need.  Oddly enough, most 
Americans are still limited to dial-up connections magnifying the 
bloat issue greatly.

I don't know of any series of standard places one can look if some
portion of the kernel documentation is wrong about where to go for 
a particular option.  I don't know how to follow library changes, I
am not a highly skilled programmer.  Heck, most people aren't
programmers at all.

Upgrading is not always a viable solution.  Sometimes you don't know
what you are breaking.  My Maple 9.0.2 for Linux does not seem to run
on any Linuce that's newer than Fedora Core 1.  How often is it
reasonable to do a major upgrade?  It seems that Fedora supports more
than it's Enterprise cousins, but it turns over so rapidly.  The
enterprise variants are very limited in new hardware support, so one
loses on the equipment side of the equation.  I think dropping 386
support in Slackware 10.1 was the wrong move.  For Linux to be better
then the alternatives, it needs to be the best choice for the hardware
that exists already and the hardware of tomorrow.  Fragmentation after
Windows 2000 evidently upset many Windows advocates and is potentially
lowering consumer and corporate demand for it.  Is the Linux community
making the same mistake?

Should I remind everyone, within the past three months the evaluation
download for VMWare Workstation 5.5 was still distributed for Fedora
Core 3?  If FC3 is so ancient and off official support, what is with
the producers of VMware?  On the topic of VMWare, why is sound support
only partial under Fedora Core 3?  Nothing like hearing only one 
channel of a stereo connection.  I still haven't gotten VMWare
Workstation 5.5 to run on Fedora Core 5.

Anyone starting with Linux today will probably run into upgrade 
issues sooner than later.  Even if you stick to purchased 
"Enterprise" distributions, the problem isn't completely avoided.

Internet speeds are not keeping up with growth.  The Internet which made
Linux both possible and successful is becoming rapidly overloaded.  If
the Internet falls will Linux fall with it?

     --  Michael C. Robinson

> 
> Your main problem is that you listed a non-nameserver as nameserver
> for your domain.  Resolvers will randomly choose one of the
> nameservers listed for the domain.  They do not try the first and
> fallback to the second. If they choose the non-responsive one, they
> will wait for the timeout (I think it is 2 sec) until they try the
> other one.  Resolvers may be smart enough to cache the fact that one
> of the nameservers was non-responsive and use the other one for later
> queries.  But the first query for the domain has a 50% chance of being
> really slow.
> 
>  - Ian
Isn't putting up a non nameserver as your last name server a standard
anti abuse tactic?  Don't the weights put on nameservers mean anything
anymore?  Isn't querying a site's last nameserver first non standard
behavior?  The whole point of having two nameservers is that one of them,
either one, could be down.

On Sat, 14 Oct 2006, Carlos Konstanski wrote:

> Date: Sat, 14 Oct 2006 20:12:15 -0600
> From: Carlos Konstanski <ckonstanski at sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
> Reply-To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;	civil and on-topic"

>     <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> Subject: [PLUG] Spotty DNS Resolution
> 
> I think you are using the Dotster admin tool incorrectly.  You should
> not use the 2 hostname text boxes to enter your DNS server location.
> These are meant for those customers who own large blocks of IP address
> space, given out by ICANN.  A private individual like you or me will
> never be able to play ball in this league.  If I am mistaken, and you
> are a company who has a block of IP space doled out by ICANN, then my
> bad.
>
> Instead, you should look for a feature caled "Total DNS Control" or
> something similar.  I use 1PlanHost (Windows servers, I know, but I
> started using them years ago and now I'm stuck).  A friend of mine who
> uses DirectNic has the identical-looking admin interface.  This makes
> me think that the interface is common to many domain registrars.
> Anyway, the idea is this: instead of telling the internet that your
> DNS server is the primary nameserver for your domain, you should
> instead let Dotster be the primary DNS server, and use the tools
> provided to customize their DNS records to simply point all requests
> to your IP address.
>
> I have ten domain names set up with 1PlanHost, and they all have DNS
> records that look exactly like this:
>
> A RECORDS:
> @  	70.168.39.139  	3600
> * 	70.168.39.139 	3600
>
> Notice the lack of CNAMEs and MX records.  These two A records suffice
> to send all requests directly to my IP address, into the arms of my
> DNS server.  Every request for one of my domain names goes to
> 1PlanHost first, and then to me.
>
> Where you attempted to put your DNS server information, you should
> instead click the "Default Parked Nameservers" button, thereby letting
> Dotster be the "real" DNS server.  Once you park your domain name(s)
> on Dotster's DNS server, you can edit their records as described
> above.
>
> I get the Total DNS Control feature for no extra charge with
> 1PlanHost, but my DirectNic buddy had to pay an extra $5 to get the
> feature, even though the web interface is identical.  I had to hunt
> around a bit to find where I could purchase the feature for him.
>
> I wish I could put screenshots in this post, but I cannot.  If you
> would like some, I can send an email directly to you (provided you can
> receive it).
>
> Carlos Konstanski
>

On 10/16/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> >
> > Your main problem is that you listed a non-nameserver as nameserver
> > for your domain.  Resolvers will randomly choose one of the
> > nameservers listed for the domain.  They do not try the first and
> > fallback to the second. If they choose the non-responsive one, they
> > will wait for the timeout (I think it is 2 sec) until they try the
> > other one.  Resolvers may be smart enough to cache the fact that one
> > of the nameservers was non-responsive and use the other one for later
> > queries.  But the first query for the domain has a 50% chance of being
> > really slow.
> >
>
> Isn't putting up a non nameserver as your last name server a standard
> anti abuse tactic?  Don't the weights put on nameservers mean anything
> anymore?  Isn't querying a site's last nameserver first non standard
> behavior?  The whole point of having two nameservers is that one of them,
> either one, could be down.
>

What weights?  You may be thinking aobut MX records for mail servers
which do have weights.  And putting in a bogus (or honeypot)
high-weight MX record is a tatic for reducing spam.  NS records don't
have weights or ordering.  Like A records, the resolver randomly
orders the list to load balance access to the servers.  The point of
having two nameservers is that if one of them is down, the other will
be evenutally accessed.

 - Ian

if people would only fix problems and never implement new features, that

by new features, thus backwards compatibility is always an issue, and
never sure.

> I often hear, "you have to upgrade or you are insecure."  Well, some
> hardware won't run the newest version of Linux.

almost no hardware has been 'defeatured' and removed support for in the
kernel. Plenty of new additions in recent 2.6 kernels allow even smaller
systems to run this kernel too!

The kernel process works the other way: new hardware is not supported in
*old* kernels, not the other way around.

> The Linux community
> needs to reign in the bloat and realize that increasing complexity in
> the kernel is starting to become a major problem for people trying to
> use it effectively.

everlasting discussion, can't be solved: today you want things to be
more stable, tomorrow you want your new system to be supported. You
can never make up your mind as a customer: you want both, and at the
same time.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Tue Oct 17 11:59:15 2006
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Tue, 17 Oct 2006 11:59:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Version creep and patching...
In-Reply-To: <1160947131.28430.50.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1160947131.28430.50.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0610171159l6b53a1e5w92a1b2a64b1fbdff@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/15/06, Unknown <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> What are the major problems being found in perl that perl 5.008
> is not very compatible with perl 5.8.0?  Isn't a newer version of
> a program generally supposed to be compatible with older versions?
>

Perl 5.008 and 5.8.0 are the same version.  The older way of
representing Perl version numbers is as a floating point number.  So
5.8.8 is 5.008008.

Fedora is not the best distribution is you want compatibility and
stability.  They have been aggressive in introducing new technologies
and in deprecating old ones.  For example, FC5 provides glibc 2.4
which removes the old LinuxThreads support.    Fedora tends to
introduce new versions of packages instead of backporting fixes.  RHEL
is much better about providing compatibility packages and they
backport fixes instead of releasing new updates.    Backporting fixes
and providing compatibility is a lot of work which is why Redhat
charges for their support.

 - Ian

From ehem at m5p.com  Tue Oct 17 23:33:22 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Tue, 17 Oct 2006 23:33:22 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Version creep and patching...
In-Reply-To: <20061017180531.E2C354760@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200610180633.k9I6XM0q000788@m5p.com>

>From: Unknown <plug_0 at robinson-west.com>
> I often hear, "you have to upgrade or you are insecure."  Well, some
> hardware won't run the newest version of Linux.  The Linux community

Well, as long as the release you're dealing is supportted, you can patch.
Patches tend to be very small changes, hopefully the absolute minimum
needed to patch the security problem. Once the version you're dealing
with is no longer supportted by the distribution, you do have to upgrade
or be insecure (or roll your own, but that is a PITA). If you don't like
upgrades, you go with RHEL, SuSE, Debian or one based on those, enjoy the
long upgrade cycles, but endure software that is older. If you need to be
on the bleeding edge, you go with Fedora, Debian-unstable or another
such distribution, but have to bleed (upgrade frequently). There is a
whole spectrum here, depending on how often you're willing to deal with
upgrades and how close to the edge you wish to be.

> needs to reign in the bloat and realize that increasing complexity in
> the kernel is starting to become a major problem for people trying to
> use it effectively.  I'd say that inadequate documentation for the 2.4
> kernel is a problem that has carried over to 2.6 in spades.  How many
> people wonder if they have something when attempting to configure the
> 2.6.18 kernel and don't know what decision to make?  If that which is
> not in the kernel tarball to explain the choices is on the net, what
> good does that do anyone?  With sufficient bloat, not everyone can go 
> to the Net to download everything they need.  Oddly enough, most 

Well, as already mentioned, if you can't deal with sparse documentation,
*don't*! Leave it to the distribution to make choices that aren't
optimal, but cover 90% of the cases. If neither of these is acceptable,
then hire a professional system administrator, they're expensive, but
that is because they will deal with these problems.

As far as bloat, well some distributions do go for the eye candy. If you
need a minimal system, that is quite doable too. On occasion I deal with
a PPro 200 system. Obviously Gentoo is hopeless for that system, but that
is why it doesn't use Gentoo. Does surprisingly well given its age. What
were you commenting about older hardware not working with recent
distributions/software? If you can't handle it, there are plenty of
people who you can hire to handle it for you. This is also trivial
compared to what companies like a certain Redmond company *requires*.

> Americans are still limited to dial-up connections magnifying the 
> bloat issue greatly.

Again, if bandwidth is an issue, use a smaller install. Also avoid full
upgrades which suck the bandwidth.

> I don't know of any series of standard places one can look if some
> portion of the kernel documentation is wrong about where to go for 
> a particular option.  I don't know how to follow library changes, I
> am not a highly skilled programmer.  Heck, most people aren't
> programmers at all.

Yes, and that is why you go with a stable distribution that doesn't
require you to be a programmer. You choose to either deal with
difficulties on the bleeding edge, or you go for something where you
don't have to, but don't get the latest thing. You don't get both, so
make your choice.

> Upgrading is not always a viable solution.  Sometimes you don't know
> what you are breaking.  My Maple 9.0.2 for Linux does not seem to run
> on any Linuce that's newer than Fedora Core 1.  How often is it
> reasonable to do a major upgrade?  It seems that Fedora supports more
> than it's Enterprise cousins, but it turns over so rapidly.  The

Again, it is the same choice. Bleeding edge or support. You don't get
both unless someone puts in a *lot* of time. This can be you, or someone
paid by you. Make the choice.

> enterprise variants are very limited in new hardware support, so one
> loses on the equipment side of the equation.  I think dropping 386
> support in Slackware 10.1 was the wrong move.  For Linux to be better

How many 80386 class machines are still out there? Sorry, but they're
dying. If you're really that desperate to use an old machine, then the
time has to be spent. The distribution folks rightfully are moving beyond
because it is too much time for them for a trivial number of machines.
Even a WRT54GL is many times faster than an 80386 machine! The cost to
replace one with a faster machine is pennies! Far cheaper to replace them
than spend the hours of time for support.

Why is this decision such a bad one?

> Should I remind everyone, within the past three months the evaluation
> download for VMWare Workstation 5.5 was still distributed for Fedora
> Core 3?  If FC3 is so ancient and off official support, what is with
> the producers of VMware?  On the topic of VMWare, why is sound support
> only partial under Fedora Core 3?  Nothing like hearing only one 
> channel of a stereo connection.  I still haven't gotten VMWare
> Workstation 5.5 to run on Fedora Core 5.

Again, make your bloody choices and live with them! You cannot have the
whole world bowing at your feet to suit your whims unless you're willing
to pay the cost either directly in time, or indirectly with money.

Fedora is a *bleeding* edge distribution! They say very clearly that
their support cycle is a grand total of 2 years, *period* (and even that
is far longer than they originally planned on). If you do not understand
that, then what point would there be in additional documentation in the
kernel for obviously you would not bother to read it?

If you want support then choose a different bloody distribution!

> Anyone starting with Linux today will probably run into upgrade 
> issues sooner than later.  Even if you stick to purchased 
> "Enterprise" distributions, the problem isn't completely avoided.

Yeah, enterprise systems say 5 years, though often it extends a little
bit beyond that. That is what companies demand, and they are willing to
pay for that. They also get distributions that have older software. They
just plan ahead and a software upgrade once every 5 years isn't that big
a deal.

Make your choice and live with it. Don't go ranting about something
you're not willing to pay for in time or money.

in the silly "Unknown" as real name, or "plug_00" as username doesn't
fool us. Please give us some credit for a minimal amount of intelect.
You are after all asking us questions that require far more intelect
than identifying you requires.

From Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu  Wed Oct 18 13:18:10 2006
From: Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu (Jeff Moore)
Date: Wed, 18 Oct 2006 13:18:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: PLUG Digest, Vol 25, Issue 47
In-Reply-To: <20061017184712.9286E476A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20061017184712.9286E476A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <1161202690.31225.76.camel@localhost>

Hi all,

   Just checking to see if you all have any standard practices or tips
when it comes to server forensics.
   We had an instance where an administrator installed an FC4 box
outside our firewall and while testing the machine he stopped iptables.
DOH! Wouldn't you know it, in no time a pesky little hermit crab(not to
offend hermit crab lovers) scrambled along and crawled in. This
particular crab used the server as an IRC server and for some attacks
and reconnaissance(this is how the administrator was alerted to the
intrusion). Immediately the admin unplugged the network cable and
shutdown the machine(we try to teach them not to shut the machine down
but those darn knee jerk reactions take over sometimes). 
   I have currently booted into knoppix mounted the lvm and studied it a
little. I am also scp'ing the contents of the lvm to another machine for
a more intense look. It appears that this particular hermit crab
connected consistently from one IP address in Romania. By the looks of
it I wouldn't doubt if this was his IP. He did nothing to conceal his
work on the server. We have the complete history file all his scripts
and links to where he houses his scripts(a bank of scripts on a
geocities site and an irc server camouflaged as a pdf on an undisclosed
server etc.). Pretty interesting really.
   Thanks in advance for the help and tips.

Jeff M
