       "Detect drives done: not any drive found".

Then the "Start or Install Ubuntu" screen comes up and when I click this 
I get the following BIOS error message:
    
    MP-BIOS bug: 8254 timer not connected to IO_APIC
    Kernal Panic - not syncing: IO-APIC + timer doesn't work!
    Boot with apic=debug and send a report. Then try booting with the 
"noapic" option.

I was able to format the drive using a GParted Live CD so it found the 
HD OK and the problem appears to be strictly in the bios setup for the 
motherboard. When I called Cool Tech about the problem they suggested 
that I Google the net or bring the computer back and they would install 
Ubuntu for $40. Perhaps this is the best solution but I thought I would 
first see if any of you good people could offer any suggestions and save 
myself another trip to Vancouver. If they regularly install Ubuntu then 
they must have known that I would run into this problem when I tried to 
install. Here is a summary of the system - motherboard et al:
    
Asus M2N-SLI Deluxe Motherboard
AMD Athlon 64 AM2 X2 Dual Core 3800 CPU
2GB ( two 1GB ) PC-5300 Kingston memory
56K V.92 PCI Hardware modem
Microsoft BLACK Keyboard/Mouse Combo
Samsung Quiet 160GB SATA2 HDD HD160JJ
No Operating System
Nexus 500W NX5000 Power Supply
MSI NX6200TC 256MB PCIE (Fanless)
Antec P180 BLACK Case
Plextor (Beige or Black) PX-760A DVDRW
Zalman CNPS9500AM2 CPU Cooler

The bottom line message may be -- don't try to buy a Linux system from a 
Windows shop.

Any help will be greatly appreciated.

On 10/18/06, Jack Hoke <jwhoke at bctonline.com> wrote:
> The bottom line message may be -- don't try to buy a Linux system from a
> Windows shop.

Or at least take something with to test the system out first.  A live
CD of some kind, like Knoppix, or in your case just take the Ubuntu
live CD with when you go to make the purchase, and leave the computer
there (taking your money with you) until it works.  There's no greater
incentive to a vendor than money literally walking out the door.

You can try the following BIOS test kit:
http://www.linuxfirmwarekit.org/

or Oracle. People *did* get fired for buying PeopleSoft :).


From znmeb at cesmail.net  Thu Oct 19 07:24:10 2006
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Thu, 19 Oct 2006 07:24:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] dumping unix for linux
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610161555360.1044@blackbox.fnordora.org>
References: <12353.64.122.164.5.1161033548.squirrel@webmail.integraonline.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610161555360.1044@blackbox.fnordora.org>
Message-ID: <45378A8A.4060509@cesmail.net>

alan wrote:
> But there are free versions that give you exactly the same thing as the
> supported version without the support. (i.e. Centos.)

CentOS is "binary compatible" with RHEL but it is absolutely, positively
not "exactly the same thing" as RHEL. I've used CentOS, and I like it
for what it is ... if you like boring, stable Linux platforms, it's
fine, but then, so is Debian "Sarge", and if all goes well, "Etch" in
December. :)
> 
> It may cost you time and work, but the GPL does keep you from getting
> locked in to a specific vendor.
> 
> You did remember to save all those Source RPMS?

Why bother to save the source RPMs? Red Hat posts them on their FTP
server, as required by the GPL. :)

When you come right down to it, a Linux distro is composed of:

1. A Linux kernel,
2. The GNU utilities and tool chain, compilers, editors, etc.,
3. Networking and associated tools,
4. An X windows implementation,
5. A package management system, and
6. Office/desktop, workstation and server applications.

Layered on top of this will be a community, a corporation, or both.

Along these lines, one of the things that never ceases to amaze me is
that businesses run production servers and desktops on Fedora rather
than CentOS. In other words, they believe a community distro "blessed by
Red Hat" is better in some sense than something that's binary compatible
with RHEL.

I just finished an EE degree so I could get out, but I'd recommend an
MCSE[1] cert to anyone who wants to break into the exciting world of
systems administration[2].  Not because you'll actually use whatever you
memorized to pass the tests, but because you're much more likely to get
hired by the e-ignoramuses who make such decisions.

Add a CCNA and you're a High-Dollar Network Engineer.  Combine with a
Linux cert and you're Cross-Platform.  RHCE is the best-known, but LPI
is way cheap through Free Geek and studying for it is amazingly educational.

Oh, and don't forget to deduct training costs off your taxes.

It's all about the keywords on the resume,
  Aaron

[1] MCSE: Microsoft Certified Shutdown Engineer
[2] http://www.adminspotting.org/


From heinlein at madboa.com  Thu Oct 19 10:56:53 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)

Subject: [PLUG] dumping unix for linux

	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610161555360.1044@blackbox.fnordora.org>

Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0610191043020.16932@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Thu, 19 Oct 2006, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:

> Along these lines, one of the things that never ceases to amaze me 
> is that businesses run production servers and desktops on Fedora 
> rather than CentOS. In other words, they believe a community distro 
> "blessed by Red Hat" is better in some sense than something that's 
> binary compatible with RHEL.

It doesn't amaze me at all, really. It all depends on what you want, 
and the time you're willing to invest.

While I was at OGI, I relied on Fedora for login servers and desktop 
workstations. Fedora provided tools of recent vintage that tended to 
work better with newer hardware. Prior to each Fall term, I'd nuke and 
repave all the Fedora machines. It took some time to figure out what 
configuration files needed tweaking, but getting the newer tools was 
worth the investment in time.

The amazing thing is that people install Fedora while desiring 
long-term stability in the face of Internet-borne threats...

CentOS or Red Hat were better choices for machines that offer stable, 
always-on network services: DNS, DHCP, RADIUS, web, e-mail, database, 
calendaring, file services, and what-not. Living with increasingly 
fossilized (albeit maintained) versions of service software was worth 
the stability they offered.

The amazing thing is that people install RHEL/CentOS while desiring a 
bleeding-edge toolset...

Both approaches have strengths and weaknesses -- and I've always found 
myself in situations where I need both, because they solve different 
problems.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

Wow. First, everyone knows that MCSE especially, and CCNA's to a lesser
degree, don't know much. PHB's don't normally make the hiring decisions,
it's the people who already do the work. After you get the interview,
it's all about technical knowledge rather than certs. Do feel free to
state "instructor-level knowledge for CCNP and RHCE," that works well
for getting past HR :)

I do agree that getting certs (shady business, as the cert industry is)
is a good way to get entry-level jobs. Afterward, experience and
networking (the people kind) are what's required. It's really scary to
explain technical things to MCSE's sometimes.. and yes they do make
decent money. But if you ever want to get out of the rage they're in, it
won't happen with only cert knowledge.

And finally, CCNA holders don't get high-dollar wages. Many people
looking for mid-level network engineers won't even consider CCNA's. They
might consider a CCNP holder, if you have tons of experience too, but
you're really better off not going the cert route.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Thu Oct 19 13:44:29 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Thu, 19 Oct 2006 13:44:29 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Windows firewall
In-Reply-To: <4537CFF1.9040709@alcpress.com>
References: <4537CFF1.9040709@alcpress.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0610191322040.16932@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Thu, 19 Oct 2006, Ed Sawicki wrote:

> I've had my head buried in writing a book for the past few months 
> and I've not kept up with the latest Windows stuff. Does the latest 
> version of Windows server (what's it called now? Is it shipping 
> yet?) have a built-in firewall whose functionality comes anywhere 
> close to Linux Netfilter/iptables?

Afaict, you have three choices with Windows Server 2003:

1. Simple TCP/IP filtering on a per-interface basis:

   Network Connections -> $INTERFACE -> Local Area Connection
   Properties -> Internet Protocol (TCP/IP) Properties ->
   Advanced TCP/IP Settings -> TCP/IP filtering

   The default is to let all traffic pass; exceptions are blocked.

2. Stateful packet analysis/filtering using Internet Connection
   Firewall (ICF). When enabled, ICF's default setting is to
   deny unsolicited inbound traffic. Exceptions must be made
   explicit. There's a logging module, but it's not as configurable
   as netfilter.

   Note that ICF can't do masquerading; that's handled by the
   Internet Connection Sharing (ICS) application.

   Both ICF and ICS are included with the most popular versions of
   Windows Server 2003.

3. For extra $$, you can purchase the Microsoft Internet Security
   and Acceleration (ISA) Server, a software suite that application-
   level filtering and proxy capabilities.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

> and get use to fairly major roads that are N Thisroad,

You will also discover that the greater Portland area is divided into 
six quadrants: N, NE, NW, SW, SE, and S. Somebody else chose to call 
them quadrants -- probably one of the folks who thinks there are three 
kinds of people in the world: those who can count and those who can't.

The East and West are divided by the Willamette River. North and South 
are divided by Burnside Street. The North and South designations are a 
bit fuzzier. South starts south of the Clackamas River. North is west of 
N. Williams Ave., and north of the Steel Bridge. In spite of all that, 
or perhaps because of it, finding your way around isn't too hard.

On Thu, 19 Oct 2006, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> You will also discover that the greater Portland area is divided into six 
> quadrants: N, NE, NW, SW, SE, and S.

Huh?!?  Who ever refers to "South Portland"?!?

There are five quadrants and that makes sense because the city is divided 
by the river east from west and Burnside north from south.  The river then 
curves west and so the NE quadrant's zero divider runs due(ish) north 
while the NW designation is picked up only on the west side of the river. 
Hence, there is a North Portland (which, historically, was a separate city 
pre-1890).

> The East and West are divided by the Willamette River. North and South 
> are divided by Burnside Street. The North and South designations are a 
> bit fuzzier. South starts south of the Clackamas River.

Huh (again)?!?  If you're south of the Clackamas, then you're also east of 
the Willamette.  So that's SE Portland.  Once you're outside the city 
limits, nobody cares.

The streets are labelled to designate which of the five quadrants they 
occupy.

So what streets are labelled "S Somethingorother" and are within the city?

> North is west of N. Williams Ave., and north of the Steel Bridge.

Williams doesn't exist south of there because that's where the river bends 
West.  So, west of N Williams is de facto north of the Steel Bridge.

Note that the only street that is labelled W or E without a north or south 
designation is Burnside, the north/south divider.

J.

      Jeme A Brelin
     jeme at brelin.net

associated groups in Portland like Biznix, Personal Telco, PASA,
Perl Mongers, the Python and Ruby groups, a Postgres group, and 
so on.

    Welcome to Portland.

    Also, you will have to let us know what is happening in
Montana.  I got a degree from the University of Montana 
in Missoula once upon a time - long, long ago.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Oct 19 17:39:39 2006

On Thu, Oct 19, 2006 at 02:43:32PM -0600, Skip Evans wrote:
> Hey all,
> 
> I will soon be relocating to Portland and was 
> wondering if the PLUG meetings are still active, 
> and if anyone has directions to the meeting place.

> I'll be living down in the SE part of the city (I

> Butte, Montana 59701


We are quite active.  You can look at the website for time and place,
www.pdxlinux,org.

We typically have 30-50 at the general meeting, 20 at advanced topics,
and 12 or so at the Linux Clinic.  There are also meetings for Perl,
Ruby, Plone, Open Source Entrepreneurs, Personal Telco (community 
wifi) and lunch meetings at the Open Technology Business Center. 
Occasionally special events at Portland State University or elsewhere; 
we've recently had Richard Stallman, Jeff Waugh and Mark Shuttleworth,
and we will have Cory Doctorow on 11/16 .   Plan on being immersed in
Penguin Luv.

Unfortunately, there is no Glisan street in southeast.

2104 *North West* Glisan is in the neighborhood sometimes called
Nob Hill and more often just the Northwest, close to the Cinema
21 theater.  However, I think that location is a one story shop
building, with a pharmacy at 2100 NW Glisan if I recall.  Perhaps
there are upstairs apartments that I haven't seen.  There is also

I am guessing that you are talking 2014 Glisan, the Biltmore
Apartments.  You will be near two busy streets and a couple of
busy taverns, so I hope a Montana boy can get used to the city
noises!  The park across the street from 2104 is nice on a summer
day, and there is a free wifi node nearby, but there are sometimes
evil doings there at night.  

And remember, no discharging of firearms in the city, unless you
are a police officer and the suspect is unarmed :-/

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

  The Flash Player 9 Beta is available for download.  It seems to work
fine on my FC5 system with Firefox version 1.5.0.6.  It's worked with
all the web sites I had tagged as Flash 9 only.

Flash Player 9 Update
http://labs.adobe.com/technologies/flashplayer9/

  I just downloaded the "Installer for Linux" version and unarchived
the file.  There isn't any installation program.  Just move
"libflashplayer.so" into the "/usr/lib/mozilla/plugins" directory,
chmod 755 the file, and then restart Firefox.

Jeme A Brelin wrote:

> On Thu, 19 Oct 2006, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> 
>> You will also discover that the greater Portland area is divided into 
>> six quadrants: N, NE, NW, SW, SE, and S.
> 
> 
> Huh?!?  Who ever refers to "South Portland"?!?
<...>
> Huh (again)?!?  If you're south of the Clackamas, then you're also east 
> of the Willamette.  So that's SE Portland.  Once you're outside the city 
> limits, nobody cares.

You've missed a significant word in my statement: greater. I never said 
that there was a South Portland, or that there were streets named S. 
something in the city of Portland. For confirmation of my description, 
have a look at The Thomas Guide or GoogleMaps.

The "great renaming" (I don't recall when it happened, or if it was 
really called the "great renaming") involved the cooperation of most of 
the communities that make up the metropolitan Portland area (in Oregon), 
which includes parts of Washington and Clackamas counties in addition to 
Multnomah. The cooperation got a little fuzzy in Gresham. They kept 
their name and numbering system, as did Oregon City. But the counties 
around them switched. I live outside Oregon City, and have a S. street 
name. SE 242nd Ave changes its name to SE Hogan Road when it crosses the 
county line, and enters Gresham, changes to NE Hogan Drive when it 
crosses Powell, and then becomes NE 242nd Dr when it crosses Stark.

(Note to Skip: As you can see, we sometimes get carried away with off 
topic stuff.)

--
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From blitters at gmail.com  Thu Oct 19 23:02:22 2006
From: blitters at gmail.com (Steve D...)
Date: Thu, 19 Oct 2006 23:02:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Flash Player 9
In-Reply-To: <ff01ea180610192221t14b231cby2d2d8a6f843476eb@mail.gmail.com>
References: <ff01ea180610192031t11907561hb8be98b6a3a3d3d2@mail.gmail.com>
	<ff01ea180610192221t14b231cby2d2d8a6f843476eb@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <ff01ea180610192302g30545ef2r13ca0ed2a9f183bc@mail.gmail.com>

  It looks like it's known bug.  You need to delete *all* the symbolic
links to the Flash 7 player.  Having both versions of the plug-ins
installed causes bad things to happen.

  Enter, "about:plugins" into Firefox.  Remove the symbolic links
until you don't see Flash installed.  I found extra symbolic links in
"/usr/lib/firefox-1.5.0.6/plugins/" and "~/.mozilla/plugins/".

  It seems to be working OK since I removed all the links to the older
version of the plugin.

Steve D...

On 10/19/06, Steve D... <blitters at gmail.com> wrote:

From skip at bigskypenguin.com  Fri Oct 20 12:13:03 2006

Subject: [PLUG] PLUG and BSD?

Hey all,

I get the impression from looking over the web 
site that PLUG also has members up on or 
interested in the BSD troika, and other *nix 
flavors as well?

That would be great in my book, since we use both 
Penguins and Devils around here.

The small PHP/MySQL shop we will be moving to 
Portland in a couple of weeks uses Debian Sarge 
running Fluxbox WM for workstations, but I've been 
using FreeBSD on servers for years and have 
carried that on through, even though I have no 
complaints whatsoever about Linux servers.

Just figured I could keep up on BSD too, and we're 
currently migrating our two 1U MBX jobbies to 
FreeBSD 6.1.

Workstation-wise, we also keep a Windows machine 
on the network, mostly for checking PHP apps out 
on IE, but I've not installed Samba or anything 
that allows it to mount the servers, just thinking 
if it's all loaded up with viruses maybe that will 
slow them down a bit from spreading around.

To paraphrase Bart Simpson, "Why would anyone want 
to touch a Windows box? Everybody knows that's 
where cooties come from!"

(Cite the original Bart quote and I'll buy you a 
beer when I get to Portland.)

PS. Just in case I've already annoyed some Windows 
users, no harm meant. I know it has its place. I 
was just joshing.

From dmandel at pdxLinux.org  Fri Oct 20 12:55:28 2006
From: dmandel at pdxLinux.org (David Mandel)
Date: Fri, 20 Oct 2006 12:55:28 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] PLUG and BSD?
In-Reply-To: <45391FBF.3010101@bigskypenguin.com>
References: <45391FBF.3010101@bigskypenguin.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.58.0610201242530.8024@PPC.DavidMandel.com>


> Hey all,
>
> I get the impression from looking over the web
> site that PLUG also has members up on or
> interested in the BSD troika, and other *nix
> flavors as well?
>
> That would be great in my book, since we use both
> Penguins and Devils around here.
> ...

Yes, PLUG doesn't restrict itself to Linux and never ever has.
Indeed, a number of years ago we changed our name from
          The Portland Linux Users Group (abbreviated PLUG)
       to
          The Portland Linux/Unix Group (still abbreviated PLUG)

Basically, we are simply a group of people interested in OpenSource
software.  This includes Linux and the open BSDs; but also includes
open like systems (proprietary Unix) and OpenSource applications
on proprietary OSs.  We are even interested in open stuff on
Windows.  One of these days I want to have Microsoft speak about
it's move toward open systems.  (Is it real or is it marketing?)

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Oct 20 13:14:18 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)

Subject: [PLUG] PLUG and BSD?
In-Reply-To: <45391FBF.3010101@bigskypenguin.com>

On Fri, 20 Oct 2006, Skip Evans wrote:

> Hey all,
> 
> I get the impression from looking over the web site that PLUG also 
> has members up on or interested in the BSD troika, and other *nix 
> flavors as well?

At the risk of oversimplification, PLUG members

* know and love Linux
* know and love core Free software (GNU utils, LAMP stack, ...)
* are aware of and respect the BSDs
* tolerate Windows as a necessary evil

BSD-esque questions are treated as first-class citizens, but I'm not 
sure this is the forum to which I'd turn for widespread, hard-core BSD 
knowledge.

Back in the 90s, however, we had a discussion that led us to expand 
the PLUG acronym as "Portland Linux/UNIX Group" (contra "Portland 
Linux Users Group").

Of course, the usual blithe quote around here is, "If you say you're a 
PLUG member, you *are* a PLUG member." I guess the first corollary is 
"If you say PLUG is concerned about BSD, it *is* concerned about BSD." 
:-)

> PS. Just in case I've already annoyed some Windows users, no harm 
> meant. I know it has its place. I was just joshing.

Windows users are way more annoyed with the OS than they'll ever be 
with comments like that. :-)

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From skip at bigskypenguin.com  Fri Oct 20 15:42:07 2006
From: skip at bigskypenguin.com (Skip Evans)
Date: Fri, 20 Oct 2006 16:42:07 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] PLUG and BSD?
In-Reply-To: <20061020221744.GS9025@syrinxpc.com>
References: <45391FBF.3010101@bigskypenguin.com>
	<20061020221744.GS9025@syrinxpc.com>
Message-ID: <453950BF.4050000@bigskypenguin.com>

I'm not a professional sys admin by any stretch. 
I've been a developer for 20+ years. Grew up on 
C/C++ in industrial apps, so the OS was Unix or 
*nix flavored. Then telecom where HP-UX was pretty 
ubiquitous.

But I've always been around the servers in the 
smaller shops, so as I moved out on my own 
recently I was more comfortable having servers at 
my own site where I could get at the console than 
who knows where at some hosting facility. I know, 
old school, but I like saying things like "Why 
back in my day, by cracky...!"

Also, as an educational experience I find being 
able to get my hands on them beneficial.

So in our shop here we keep our own servers for 
development, and I maintain them, but occasionally 
go for outside help if the need arises (which is 
less and less).

BSD's ports collection is pretty easy to build 
stuff with, and I've had good success with FreeBSD 
as servers, so I don't plan on changing that any 
time in the foreseeable future.

Love Linux for workstations though. Plenty of good 
applications and 'apt-get install' is a breeze. We 
tried Kubuntu, bit I'm partial to light WMs, so 
Fluxbox got the nod.

One of my pet projects I'd like to get going when 
I get to Portland is to try to set up workstations 
to run as thin clients, booting from a server, 
etc, and all that stuff. No real strong reason to 
do it other than to see how it's done, how it 
work, etc.

Skip

While cleaning out old cupboards and such, I ran across a 15-year-old

is still in its original box and is complete with manual and floppies

a whopping 105MB to your PC.  I suspect it is an MSM disk.

so, let me know and I'll see about getting it to you.  Otherwise, it
will get trashed.

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Fri Oct 20 20:36:19 2006
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Fri, 20 Oct 2006 20:36:19 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] thunderbird sending plaintext passwords when talking to
	dovecot
Message-ID: <20061021033624.5C9DD8F00@tinman.seitzassoc.com>


I'm seeing a problem where thunderbird sends passwords in the clear
while talking to a dovecot imap server.  Thunderbird is configured to
use imap without SSL, TLS, or secure authentication.  This was done
intentionally just as a test.  Dovecot is configured to disallow
plaintext authentication.  I was expecting thunderbird to not even
attempt to login, but it does anyway.  Is this normal?


Dovecot sends the following in response to thunderbird's capability
request.

  * CAPABILITY IMAP4rev1 SORT THREAD=REFERENCES MULTIAPPEND UNSELECT IDLE CHILDREN LISTEXT LIST-SUBSCRIBED NAMESPACE STARTTLS LOGINDISABLED\r\n

Since it says LOGINDISABLED, I expected thunderbird to just give up, but
instead it prompts for a password anyway.  After entering a password and
hitting return, I see the username and password in the clear on the
wire.

  2 login "user" "notapassword"\r\n

Thunderbird then pops up an alert message from dovecot.

  * BAD [ALERT] Plaintext authentication is disabled, but your client sent password in plaintext anyway.If anyone was listening, the password was exposed.\r\n


Dovecot is dovecot-0.99.11-4.EL4 running on Centos 4.3.
Thunderbird is thunderbird-1.0.8-1.1.fc4 running on Fedora 4.

My goal is to use imap when connecting via localhost and imaps for
everything else.  I suppose I can block imap using iptables or possibly
tcpwrappers, but I didn't think that would be necessary.


galen




From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Fri Oct 20 21:02:39 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Fri, 20 Oct 2006 22:02:39 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] thunderbird sending plaintext passwords when talking to
	ecot
In-Reply-To: <20061021033624.5C9DD8F00@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
References: <20061021033624.5C9DD8F00@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610202152290.30396@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

On Fri, 20 Oct 2006, Galen Seitz wrote:

> Date: Fri, 20 Oct 2006 20:36:19 -0700
> From: Galen Seitz <galens at seitzassoc.com>
> Reply-To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;	civil and on-topic"
>     <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;	civil and on-topic"
>     <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> Subject: [PLUG] thunderbird sending plaintext passwords when talking to
>     	dovecot
> 
>
> I'm seeing a problem where thunderbird sends passwords in the clear
> while talking to a dovecot imap server.  Thunderbird is configured to
> use imap without SSL, TLS, or secure authentication.  This was done
> intentionally just as a test.  Dovecot is configured to disallow
> plaintext authentication.  I was expecting thunderbird to not even
> attempt to login, but it does anyway.  Is this normal?

Here's one solution to throw into the bucket: stunnel.  With vm for
emacs, for example, using an external SSL wrapper is the easy (or only)
way to encrypt those pesky packets.  A .vm config file might start like
this:

(setq vm-stunnel-program "/usr/sbin/stunnel")
(setq vm-imap-server-list `("imap-ssl:mail.pippiandcarlos.com:993:INBOX:login:ME:PASS"
                             "imap-ssl:mail.wrlug.org:993:INBOX:login:ME at wrlug.org:PASS"
                             "imap-ssl:mail.viralvideo.ws:993:INBOX:login:ME at viralvideo.ws:PASS"))

Sorry I have no Thunderbird-specific tips.  Also, this does not further
your goal of allowing non-encrypted local traffic.

Carlos Konstanski

>  dj is ready and printing
>  Rank    Owner   Job     File(s)                         Total Size
>  active  russell 235     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>  1st     russell 236     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>  2nd     russell 237     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>  3rd     russell 238     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>  4th     russell 239     test.ps                         687104 bytes

Try cupsdoprint to print from a client to a remote CUPS server.  My
command looks like this:

cupsdoprint -H ns.pippiandcarlos.com:631 -P HPDeskJet812C <filename>.ps

where ns.pippiandcarlos.com is the machine where my cups server is
running (and the printer is attached), and HPDeskjet12C is the name of
my printer as I named it in the CUPS server config.

Carlos Konstanski


From danielmyoung at gmail.com  Fri Oct 20 21:49:07 2006
From: danielmyoung at gmail.com (Dan Young)
Date: Fri, 20 Oct 2006 21:49:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] cups silliness
In-Reply-To: <86ac3qz3to.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86ac3qz3to.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <2e42bd9b0610202149x615092a3sc5e8960388d7c484@mail.gmail.com>

On 20 Oct 2006 21:32:35 -0700, Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com> wrote:
>
> I am a cups newbie, so naturally it doesn't take much to make me
> confused.  I have cups running on the various hosts on my network, I
> can print local (to the printer).  I can also submit print jobs from
> the clients, but for some reason the remotely-submitted jobs just sit
> in the printer-local host's queue.  Any hints on how to debug this?
>
>   $ lpq -P dj
>   dj is ready and printing
>   Rank    Owner   Job     File(s)                         Total Size
>   active  russell 235     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>   1st     russell 236     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>   2nd     russell 237     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>   3rd     russell 238     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>   4th     russell 239     test.ps                         687104 bytes

Anything interesting at http://localhost:631/jobs ?

CUPS uses Apache-style ACLs (i.e. <Location /> \n Order Deny,Allow \n
Deny from All \n Allow from localhost \n </Location>) and allows most
things only for localhost out of the box, so you might want to check
/etc/cups/cupsd.conf

Tailing /var/log/cups/* can be informative, possibly more so after
setting "LogLevel debug" in cupsd.conf.

-- 
Dan

On Friday 20 October 2006 21:32, Russell Senior wrote:

> 
>   $ lpq -P dj
>   dj is ready and printing
>   Rank    Owner   Job     File(s)                         Total Size
>   active  russell 235     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>   1st     russell 236     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>   2nd     russell 237     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>   3rd     russell 238     test.ps                         687104 bytes
>   4th     russell 239     test.ps                         687104 bytes
> 

What do your logfiles (/var/log/cups) say? The default loglevel is info, so 
you might need to kick it up to debug to get useful information (cupsd.conf). 
Clear your print queue with lprm, restart cups, and try it again.

It could be that network sharing is not enabled. This is a basic barebones 
cupsd.conf that should work:

LogLevel debug
Port 631
<Location />
Order Deny,Allow
Deny From All
Allow From 127.0.0.1
Allow From 192.168.1.*
</Location>
BrowseAddress 192.168.1.255

this free- yes, free free free!- book chapter should help:
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/chapter/

?and sysadmin's guide! http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/

From rsteff at comcast.net  Sat Oct 21 13:19:43 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Sat, 21 Oct 2006 14:19:43 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] SuSE 10.1 Install Experiences
Message-ID: <453A80DF.2020103@comcast.net>

Over the last month or so, I've been setting up a new (to me) machine
built of parts from multiple sources. Many of you helped me through a
number of problems. As it turns out, it appears that all of those
problems are related to marginal CD/DVD drives.

During the 'overheating' episode, I assumed overheating was the problem
because of the beep codes I was getting. Switching to a different mother
board seemed to make the alarm go away. Alas, it did not, and that was
when it became obvious that the CD/RW drive was the problem. Someone
suggested that it might be marginal, and recommended purchasing a new
DVD/RW drive from Pacific Solutions. That was where I bought the CD/RW
drive a few years ago. Some day I will probably get another one, but
maybe somewhere else this time.

The next time the problem came up was when I was trying to install
software on a VMware Win98 virtual machine. When I put the CD into the
drive to copy it to an ISO file, the alarm returned. I removed the CD/RW
drive and set the DVD reader to be the only drive on the second IDE
controller. The alarm went away.

For a variety of reasons, I decided to re-install SuSE 10.1. I
downloaded the DVD ISO, copied it to my XP laptop, and burned the DVD.
The install progressed to the point where it was trying to install
Firefox, and then died, claiming that the software was not present. From
this, and another experience related below, I'm assuming that the DVD
drive is also marginal. That one came from an old e-machines box I got
from my sister.

I reverted to a network install and carried on. During the install
configuration stage, a list is presented that describes what the
installation program will do. The proposed partition scheme is
described. Before I started the DVD install, I deleted all the
partitions on the 60GB drive, and when the proposed partitioning scheme
was listed I saw three partitions #1 for swap at about 1.5 GB, #2 for
root at about 20 GB, and #3 for home at about 35 GB. When I started the
network install, there were extended partitions (5 and 6, IIRC) with
sizes identical to #2 & #3, above. By editing the install configuration
I was able to tell it to use the whole drive, at which point it said
that it would delete the old ones and create 1, 2, and 3, as described
above. It probably didn't matter as far as day to day operation is
concerned, but now I understand it better.

Finally, I reinstalled VMware Server, this time from the RPM. It worked
fine, but now I have a problem getting ISOs for it to use. I copied the
Win98SE install CD to an ISO, but the winders install program thinks
there is dirt on the ISO. It's kind of hard to wipe off. I'll try
cleaning the CD and making a new ISO, but adding this experience to the
one with the DVD install of SuSE 10.1, I suspect that drive to be
marginal, too. I think I have another couple of CD drives laying around,
so I'll try swapping them in.

Meanwhile, I've successfully configured T-bird to use the profile file
I've been copying back and forth to whichever machine is working, and
I'm mostly back in business.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

a couple of potential problems.  1. Was the mic connected to the wrong 
input(line versus mic)?  2. Does your laptop even have a mic input?  If 
not, output level of a normal microphone is likely to be too low.

>
I have had good luck with most microphones on both Windows and Linux on 
my laptop. I'm guessing either you plugged it in the wrong port as 
suggested here, or your software is somehow misconfigured.

Some things to check:

1. Open a terminal in "root" mode and do "lspci". That will tell you 
what sort of sound card you have.

2. Make sure the module for that sound card is compiled and loaded. Do 
an "lsmod" to see if it's there.

3. Use the "alsamixer" console app. Make sure the little "M" is turned 
off on the microphone. The volume settings should be out of the red 
zone; I generally bring them up until they turn red, then down again. On 
my sound card that registers about 71.

4. Install a graphic recording package and use the VU meters in it to 
see if you're getting a signal. I've forgotten which ones are the best 
-- what little mike work I do these days is Skype with a gamers USB 
headset with built in mike and on Windows. :)

From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon Oct 23 09:17:29 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Mon, 23 Oct 2006 09:17:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Microphones
In-Reply-To: <453C643F.9070504@cesmail.net>
References: <20061022191312.0ccb06d6.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<453C56DD.1010702@seitzassoc.com> <453C643F.9070504@cesmail.net>
Message-ID: <20061023091729.f0125a51.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Sun, 22 Oct 2006 23:42:07 -0700

> > John Jason Jordan wrote:
> >> I need a microphone to record speech clips in Phonetics class. 
> >> I bought one today at Radio Shack, but will have to take it back
> >> because it doesn't put out enough juice to make a loud enough recording
> >> on my Compaq R3240 notebook with Ubuntu amd-64 Dapper. I cranked all
> >> the sliders to the max, but it was just not loud enough. 

> Galen Seitz wrote:
> > I don't know much about laptop sound systems, but I wanted to point 
> > out a couple of potential problems.  1. Was the mic connected to the 
> > wrong input(line versus mic)?  2. Does your laptop even have a mic 
> > input?  If not, output level of a normal microphone is likely to be 
> > too low.

"M. Edward (Ed) Borasky" <znmeb at cesmail.net> dijo:
> Some things to check:
> 1. Open a terminal in "root" mode and do "lspci". That will tell you 
> what sort of sound card you have.

It says:
0000:00:06.0 Multimedia audio controller: nVidia Corporation nForce3
Audio (rev a2)

> 2. Make sure the module for that sound card is compiled and loaded. Do 
> an "lsmod" to see if it's there.

Well, I already knew it was NVidia nforce 3 from the GUI Device
Manager, but neither Device Manager nor lspci tells me what the name of
the module is. Just "lsmod" gives me a long list, several of which
start with "snd-." But I don't think this is the problem -- the sound
*does* work. Playback is fine. It even records. It's just that the
microphones I have tried are apparently not putting out enough juice.

> 3. Use the "alsamixer" console app. Make sure the little "M" is turned 
> off on the microphone. The volume settings should be out of the red 
> zone; I generally bring them up until they turn red, then down again. On 
> my sound card that registers about 71.

I opened alsamixer, but a lot of it is mysterious, and the man page
doesn't always help. The first problem is that I don't have any idea
what some of the controls are, that is, what they are supposed to do.
For example, the first item is Master and underneath it shows 100<>100,
and the color bar above is white, green and red, in approximately equal
proportions. The man page doesn't tell me what the function of master
is, or what the colors mean, or what the 100<>100 means. The next item
is Master Mono, which appears to be Off, but again, I have no idea how
it is different from Master, or even what Master does. Moving along,
Line Jack Sense appears to be Off also, but I remember turning it on in
the Gnome panel applet, and it definitely is on, that is, the speakers
do go silent when I plug in headphones.

But in spite of my kvetching above, alsamixer does seem to have done
something for me. Arrow-keying along the different controls I got to
Mic and Mic Boost. Mic was already set to 100<>100 (which I am guessing
means the max input from left and right channels), but Mic Boost was
"MM." I changed "MM" to "OO" and then tried the two microphones I have
(one crappy old ancient thing and one brand new high quality Radio
Shack one), and both now record at acceptable levels. Well, "almost
acceptable," because I still need the Mic setting in Audacity set to
the max. I worry that setting all these controls to the max is inducing
distortion. Plus, I had to hold the Radio Shack microphone right in
front of my mouth, which is not really suitable for recording a
linguistics informant.

> 4. Install a graphic recording package and use the VU meters in it to 
> see if you're getting a signal. I've forgotten which ones are the best 
> -- what little mike work I do these days is Skype with a gamers USB 
> headset with built in mike and on Windows. :)

As noted, I'm definitely getting a signal. Just not enough signal. And
Audacity does have VU meters, which are dancing back and forth as I
speak into the microphone. Plus, Audacity displays the waveform, which
also shows the sound level.

I think I am going to try a microphone designed for computers. After
googling a bit, the Labtec 524 sounds like a good one to try. I think
the Radio Shack microphone is designed for a PA system, which is
expecting a different level. Like I'm plugging a magnetic phono
cartridge into the Aux input on a stereo. I just wish I knew what the
microphone input on this computer was expecting so I could buy a
microphone matching those specs.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Oct 23 10:07:37 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 23 Oct 2006 10:07:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Microphones
In-Reply-To: <20061022191312.0ccb06d6.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20061022191312.0ccb06d6.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061023170737.GB26642@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sun, Oct 22, 2006 at 07:13:12PM -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> I need a microphone to record speech clips in Phonetics class. The
> usual telephone quality microphones are not high enough quality, but I
> don't need the kind of microphone they'd use to record a symphony.
> Should be unidirectional and noise-cancelling. 
> 
> I bought one today at Radio Shack, but will have to take it back
> because it doesn't put out enough juice to make a loud enough recording
> on my Compaq R3240 notebook with Ubuntu amd-64 Dapper. I cranked all
> the sliders to the max, but it was just not loud enough. For the
> record, the specs on the Radio Shack microphone are:
> 
> 	60-16KHz frequency response (a sheet inside 
> 		had a graph which was fairly flat) 
> 	Impedance 500 ohms + or - 30%
> 	Sensitivity -75 dB + or - 3 dB
...

This may be an electrical, not a software problem.  Most laptop
microphone inputs to laptops are designed to work with "electret"
microphones - these mimic the old-style capacitance microphones,
and have an internal amplifier requiring power and produce far
more signal than an unamplified microphone does.  You can check
my hypothesis by plugging your soon-to-be-returned microphone
into another laptop, perhaps at Radio Shack.  

What to do?  Perhaps a microphone pre-amplifier before the signal
goes into the laptop - this allows you to use the best microphones.
Otherwise, look around (perhaps on the web) for microphones designed
to work with laptops.  I think, but I'm not sure, that microphones
designed for cassette recorders have similar behavior.  

There are people in PLUG that know a lot more about microphones
than I do.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

I doubt that I know more about microphones than Keith, but I do know
that in addition to what he mentioned above, I have noticed the
built-in audio capabilities of several laptops I have owned have a
relatively high level of noise.  If you care dearly about the quality
of the recording, you might wish to use an external sound device, such
that the signal is converted to digital before getting anywhere near
you hard drive/processor/memory/whatever causes all that noise.

Jason

Rich Shepard quoted Jeme:
>> Why sell what you can give away?

And replied:
>    Er, ... for the money?

Yeah, it's what motiviates all of us.  Since we can't get enough we charge for
everything.

Once livelihood is covered everything else _can_ be given away.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Mon Oct 23 18:47:03 2006

Subject: [PLUG] Sound in SuSE 10.1
Message-ID: <453D7097.4090602@comcast.net>

Sound in general works on my recent installation of SuSE 10.1. I get the
little melody when the system starts up. I've successfully installed
RealPlayer and can connect to and listen to KBPS (allclassical.org). I
can successfully record and playback using gramofile.

Other programs that should produce sound, don't. NoteEdit, a MIDI
sequencer that has a graphical input for writing music on a staff can
successfully generate .mid files, but doesn't produce a sound when I
click on the "play" icon. I can see the notes being played (the black
note change to red as the song progresses), but I don't hear anything.
I've tried various settings on KMix, but nothing changes for this
program -- it does correctly control the line input used by gramofile.
Amarok plays .wav files, and claims to play .mid files, but I get no
sound when it does.

I'm guessing there's some connection missing between MIDI files and the
sound code. Any recommendations on what to look for?

Dick Steffens



From ehem at m5p.com  Mon Oct 23 21:12:02 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)

Subject: [PLUG] tutoring rates?
In-Reply-To: <20061023190008.587B64700@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200610240412.k9O4C22B066037@m5p.com>

>From: tonyr <tonyr at hevanet.com>
> A friend of a friend wants to get up to speed on Linux relatively
> quickly and asked me to tutor him.  I've used Linux for
> several years (Red Hat, Mandrake/Mandriva, now (K)ubuntu) and can
> do what he is asking.  I'm not sure what rate to charge, if any.  I've
> tutored before in the public schools on a volunteer basis, but never
> Linux, except on an informal basis.  The only references I have found
> on the net with rates (one in Canada, one in NC) quote $15/hr as a
> minimum.  Is that good? Bad? Indifferent?  Anybody doing anything
> like this now?

Unless you're exchanging it for something else, ridiculously low. You
might get some at Universities at that rate as a service to the students,
in exchange for volunteer work or something else. Otherwise, $15/hr is
someone who can tell you what the book says, but has never actually done
anything vaguely related.

Figure a decent system admin in a real position is going to be $20/hr
full-time, with benefits. The above situation is more like a contracting
situation, so more like $40/hr. *If* this is pay-only not a favor to a
friend. The other way would be a teacher in front of a class, in which
case the students might pay merely that amount.

From ali.corbin at gmail.com  Mon Oct 23 21:44:06 2006
From: ali.corbin at gmail.com (Ali Corbin)
Date: Mon, 23 Oct 2006 21:44:06 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Sound in SuSE 10.1
In-Reply-To: <453D7097.4090602@comcast.net>
References: <453D7097.4090602@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <965f90c20610232144h7474e898s21f1acc0416a5fad@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/23/06, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
> Sound in general works on my recent installation of SuSE 10.1. I get the
> little melody when the system starts up. I've successfully installed
> RealPlayer and can connect to and listen to KBPS (allclassical.org39). I
> can successfully record and playback using gramofile.
>
> Other programs that should produce sound, don't. NoteEdit, a MIDI
> sequencer that has a graphical input for writing music on a staff can
> successfully generate .mid files, but doesn't produce a sound when I
> click on the "play" icon. I can see the notes being played (the black
> note change to red as the song progresses), but I don't hear anything.
> I've tried various settings on KMix, but nothing changes for this
> program -- it does correctly control the line input used by gramofile.
> Amarok plays .wav files, and claims to play .mid files, but I get no
> sound when it does.
>
> I'm guessing there's some connection missing between MIDI files and the
> sound code. Any recommendations on what to look for?
>
> --
These days, most sound cards aren't midi-capable.  You're expected to run
some software to convert the midi file into a wav file and push it out to the
sound card.

The most commonly used software is probably timidity.  See if you already
have it.  If you do, try 'timidity some.mid' to see if that works.  If
it does, then
you may be able to configure noteedit to use it to play the midis, or you might
be able to set up timidity as a demon.

If you don't already have timidity, then suse might well have an rpm for it.
If not, you can get the source at sourceforge.  (You may also need to find
some patches - They didn't used to be included in the source tarball.)

Ali

Richard,

You might want to look at conflicts between OSS and ALSA under SuSE.  I
run SuSE 10.0 and have seen som eissues getting sound working consistently
with my TV/Radio Card and playing MP3s.

I don't have more advice other than to see if both OSS (I think it's the
OSS - Open Sound System or something like that) and ALSA are conflicting
or causing problems with regards to the software playing the sound.

I've used command line tools like mpg123 (and mpg321) to help with some of
my troubleshooting.

Tim / TJ

From tonyr at hevanet.com  Tue Oct 24 11:10:46 2006
From: tonyr at hevanet.com (tonyr)
Date: Tue, 24 Oct 2006 11:10:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] PC Purchase
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0610240954370.2130@shaft.bitmine.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0610240954370.2130@shaft.bitmine.net>
Message-ID: <453E5726.3060106@hevanet.com>

I'm no expert, and others will no doubt have other opinions. I usually
go to http://www.pricewatch.com/ and look under Computers/Notebooks
to see waht the spread is. Low end w/winXP seems to be about $450-700, 
which gets you 2+GHz (single core), 512-1Gb mem, 60-250GB HD, nice 
graphics with dedicated memory, but no monitor. LCD monitors seem to be 
about $150-$250. There are also PCs with No OS, if you're looking for
something unspoiled on to which to install Linux. The CPUs are
comparable, but the mem/HD configurations tend to start out smaller.
On the upside, the prices start at about $200 (still no monitor). And 
there are outfits like System76 <http://system76.com/> that sell PCs 
with Linux installed.

This is just a starting point. Dual core/64bit architectures are taking
over, at least from the advertising standpoint, and there doesn't seem
to be much of a price hit, if any.

Good Luck.

- tony

>> I'm guessing there's some connection missing between MIDI files and the

>>
>> -- 
> These days, most sound cards aren't midi-capable.  You're expected to run
> some software to convert the midi file into a wav file and push it out
> to the sound card.

That's the foundational piece of information I didn't have.

> The most commonly used software is probably timidity.  See if you already
> have it.  If you do, try 'timidity some.mid' to see if that works.  If
> it does, then
> you may be able to configure noteedit to use it to play the midis, or
> you might
> be able to set up timidity as a demon.

More digging will be needed for setting up timidity to handle the MIDI
work of other programs, but...

> If you don't already have timidity, then suse might well have an rpm for
> it.

YaST does have an rpm for timidity, and I successfully installed it.

> If not, you can get the source at sourceforge.  (You may also need to find
> some patches - They didn't used to be included in the source tarball.)

The reference I found on the SuSE forums page:

http://nfs-uxsup.csx.cam.ac.uk/pub/doc/suse/suse9.2/suselinux-userguide_en/ch15s10.html

had a dead link, but Googling located another source:

http://alleg.sourceforge.net/digmid.html

The first link they provide downloads eawpats12.tar.bz2. This is not the
same file name as the on in the instructions (listed two links up), but
the content seems to be the same and, after taking that into account, I
successfully installed timidity and its patches.

I also successfully used timidity to play .mid files, and have it as the
default action when clicking on a .mid file in the Konqueror file browser.

That will be satisfactory for the time being. Later on, when time
permits, I'll look into setting up timidity as a daemon to handle the
MIDI needs of other programs.

Thanks very much for the needed insight.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Fri Oct 20 10:54:45 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Fri, 20 Oct 2006 10:54:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Version creep and patching...
In-Reply-To: <45352495.2000106@foo-projects.org>
References: <1160947131.28430.50.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
	<45352495.2000106@foo-projects.org>
Message-ID: <1161366885.45390d65dc70a@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

> > I don't know of any series of standard places one can look if some
> > portion of the kernel documentation is wrong about where to go for 
> > a particular option.  I don't know how to follow library changes, I
> > am not a highly skilled programmer.  Heck, most people aren't
> > programmers at all.
> 
> You shouldn't be trying to compile/run your own kernel then, but really use
> a mainstream distribution targeted at you :) ["and just shell out the
> bucks too, just like you take the 3-year SEARS service plan for the
> fridge!"]

The premise that ordinary people will be put in an increasingly difficult
position over time if they want to compile their own Linux kernel 
from current vanilla sources, is that an ideal premise that most OSS folks 
want to see come true?  The comment, "if you don't know how to do something,
don't do it," isn't the most inviting attitude one can communicate.  
Fedora Core 5 is a mainstream disrtribution even if it's a more bleeding 
edge variant of Linux that is upstream from an enterprise distribution.

> Upgrading is not always a viable solution.  Sometimes you don't know
> what you are breaking.  My Maple 9.0.2 for Linux does not seem to run
> on any Linuce that's newer than Fedora Core 1.  How often is it
> reasonable to do a major upgrade?  It seems that Fedora supports more
> than it's Enterprise cousins, but it turns over so rapidly.  The
> enterprise variants are very limited in new hardware support, so one
> loses on the equipment side of the equation.
> 
> Companies like RH will support old installations for up to 7 years on the
> original installation with fixes only. Perhaps that's what you need?

It's too expensive for me to buy enterprise Redhat and so far I've 
had bad luck trying to use a free enterprise variant.  It looks
like I need to pick a free enterprise Linux variant and move all
my servers to it.
 
> I think dropping 386 support in Slackware 10.1 was the wrong move.

> Why? are you certain that the small slackware community could test the
> new software and features on this hardware properly? If they decided to
> keep maintaining i386, they might waste so many resources on it that
> they effectively kill progress in the rest of the project. If you value
> your project, you keep it alive, but not in suspended animation!

There is nothing new on the 386 these days.  Up until Itanium came out, 
the PC's architecture was simply a superset of the 386's architecture.
I don't think that even Pentium architecture is something that students
are taught about in most computer architecture classes yet.  I've heard
that 386's are slow, why support them?  Well, what is wrong with 
reimplementing the 386 in a low power configuration for embedded 
tasks such as implementing a smart phone?  Some major complaints 
against the modern PC are: it is too loud, it runs really hot, it
consumes too much electricity, it is too patched and complex.  I 
see nothing wrong with a market that focuses on yesterdays hardware
unless that hardware becomes really rare.  There is more old hardware 
than there is new, especially with the rate at which Microsoft has been
demanding more processing power and memory to even install Windows.  I 
bet there is a larger market for an operating system that is current, 
patched, and will run on older equipment than there is for an OS that
requires the latest and greatest dual core system.

As far as companies focusing on rare hardware is concerned: 
http://www.cloud9tech.com/ 
is a good example.  What this company is not doing though is
providing itself a clear migration path when the last color 
computer disappears.  I don't see any attempt to clone it 
with a more powerful and featureful version or even produce
a machine that is 100% compatible using modern electronics.  

In the software arena, I think ReactOS is in trouble because
it focuses on supporting proprietary Windows software when it
could become a proving ground for popular new applications that 
vendors etc. aren't willing or able to write for Linux, open 
source or otherwise.  The level of proprietary software support 
ReactOS has achieved is minimal and unfortunately it isn't 
even stable yet.
http://www.reactos.org/

Concerning the premise that there aren't enough people to work on 
developing the most secure and most patched OS for yesterdays 
hardware, what are the limiting factors?  They're not: an 
inavailability of aging computer systems, a universal lack of 
interest in retro computing, total lack of interest in recycling 
old computers.  If yesterdays computer is one that was even bought
as recently as a year ago new, that means there are a ton of old 
computers around.

     --  Michael C. Robinson

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Fri Oct 20 18:28:56 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Fri, 20 Oct 2006 18:28:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Bash functions...
Message-ID: <1161394136.453977d86841e@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

I am trying to write a bash script to break a postfix mail log down.
I want to seperate the greylist errors from the blacklist errors, etc.
and when I'm done build a useful web page to inform my users.

I'm at the beginning of a project to provide a way to allow users
to see who should be getting through my mail relays that isn't.  I am 
not really familiar with a method to make postfix more fine grained on 
whether an email server that exhibits problems should be blocked or not
directly, so I'm taking an establish a universal whitelist approach.  
First though, I want to get an informative web page up built on 
information culled from /var/log/maillog.

In bash if I make variable bar local to function foo, is bar global to any
function called from within foo?  Another approach is for me to use an array
to sort multiple strings such as "Service is unavailable;", but I want to
avoid that for now.  In C++ there is a concept called namespaces, but this 
is for object oriented programming.  Yes an array is a common solution
to store a collection of strings uniquely, but you have to know how many
strings you are going to need ahead of time and possibly how long they will
all be.  So far, I use these identifying strings with grep, tail, and head
to search for a particular class of errors and effectively sort it out from
everything else dumping it to a file.  If there is a syslog or postfix
configuration trick to cause my mail server to log in such a way that the
different types of errors are seperated...

Michael C. Robinson

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sat Oct 21 11:00:35 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Sat, 21 Oct 2006 11:00:35 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] The helo address...
Message-ID: <1161453635.453a6043e65a3@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

First off, why do simple mail transfer protocol based systems have this
helo address?  There is a sender address and a recipient address, that
seems to be enough to cover where a message is from and where it needs
to go.

I'm running into a common issue with some sites sending a helo that is
a fully-qualified hostname and others sending a helo that is at best:
some sort of text message, complete garbage, or a non resolvable
fully-qualified domain name.

My ISP Opus gets through okay with a valid hostname for a helo, but 
other sites don't use one.  Why use something else?  If sites
don't use somehing I can look up, aren't they inviting anyone on the
Internet to pretend they are them?  Makes me wonder if some junk 
email claiming to be from hotmail, etcetera, really isn't. 

With Postfix 2.1.1, is there a simple way to test the helo compliance
in a weighted fashion so that something else has to be wrong such as:
recipient is invalid, source is blacklisted, user not in relay recipient
table... before a message will get blocked?  Skipping all tests for 
sites that aren't set up correctly is not the most inviting solution.

ISP's seem to be willing to program exceptions for strange helo addresses,
but shouldn't they be cracking down?

      --  Michael Christopher Robinson

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Sat Oct 21 12:47:10 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Sat, 21 Oct 2006 12:47:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] HELO checking, a closer look...
Message-ID: <1161460030.453a793e7ea1a@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

http://www.mhonarc.org/archive/html/spf-discuss/2005-11/msg00239.html

...
"RFC2821 is very explicit in section 3.6:

   -  The domain name given in the EHLO command MUST BE either a primary
      host name (a domain name that resolves to an A RR) or, if the host
      has no name, an address literal as described in section 4.1.1.1.

I.e., the EHLO/HELO name "MUST BE" the (primary) *host* name (or an address
literal).  That a host name resolves to an A RR is so fundamental that it's
included as part of the definition of host name.

It's actually the address literal part of that paragraph that is its
purpose.  Section 3.6 begins by saying

    Only resolvable, fully-qualified, domain names (FQDNs) are
    permitted when domain names are used in SMTP.
    
It then goes on to allow only two exceptions, address literals in EHLO names
and "postmaster" recipients with no domain name at all.

As in most RFCs, "domain name" means "name within a domain", not "name of a
domain", which is made evident in section 2.3.5:

    The domain name, as described in this document and in [22], is the
    entire, fully-qualified name (often referred to as an "FQDN").

("[22]" is STD 13 - RFCs 1034/1035, the DNS standard.)

That makes this worth quoting again:

    Only resolvable, fully-qualified, domain names (FQDNs) are
    permitted when domain names are used in SMTP.

Any domain-based name used in SMTP must be (forward) resolvable.

A client EHLO/HELO name that's the name of a domain - especially any
well-known domain - is itself a high-probability spam indicator, unless the
domain is hotmail.com.  Hotmail uses the name of their domain as the EHLO
name, in violation of RFC2821 (and earlier RFCs)."



Quoted by Michael C. Robinson
-----------------------------
 
Okay, why is someone upset with hotmail in this post???
What is the difference between EHLO and HELO???

I've run into some comments on google that attempting to 
check a helo address is an act of espionage.  Any thoughts?

Is it true that there is no RFC requirement for a HELO
address to be a standardized and useful piece of information?

I've seen "friend," among other things for helo addresses.  I
get "localhost" and "localhost.localdomain" a lot as well.

If what the helo is going to be is so ambiguous, why in postfix
is the standard anti spam approach to try and resolve it?

and when I'm done build a useful web page to inform my users.

should be getting through my mail relays that isn't.  I am not really
familiar with a method to make postfix more fine grained on whether an email
server that exhibits problems should be blocked or not directly, so I'm
taking an establish a universal whitelist approach.  
First though, I want to get an informative web page up built on information
culled from /var/log/maillog.

In bash if I make variable bar local to function foo, is bar global to any
function called from within foo?  Another approach is for me to use an array
to sort multiple strings such as "Service is unavailable;", but I want to
avoid that for now.  In C++ there is a concept called namespaces, but this
is for object oriented programming.  Yes an array is a common solution to
store a collection of strings uniquely, but you have to know how many
strings you are going to need ahead of time and possibly how long they will
all be.  So far, I use these identifying strings with grep, tail, and head
to search for a particular class of errors and effectively sort it out from
everything else dumping it to a file.  If there is a syslog or postfix
configuration trick to cause my mail server to log in such a way that the
different types of errors are seperated...

> information culled from /var/log/maillog.
> 
> In bash if I make variable bar local to function foo, is bar global to any
> function called from within foo?

yes:

$ foo() { local bar; bar=1; echo $bar; baz; echo $bar; }
$ baz() { echo " $bar"; bar=2; echo " $bar"; }
$ foo
1
  1
  2
2

> Another approach is for me to use an array
> to sort multiple strings such as "Service is unavailable;", but I want to
> avoid that for now.  In C++ there is a concept called namespaces, but this 
> is for object oriented programming.  Yes an array is a common solution
> to store a collection of strings uniquely, but you have to know how many
> strings you are going to need ahead of time and possibly how long they will
> all be.  So far, I use these identifying strings with grep, tail, and head
> to search for a particular class of errors and effectively sort it out from
> everything else dumping it to a file.  If there is a syslog or postfix
> configuration trick to cause my mail server to log in such a way that the
> different types of errors are seperated...

<spam>
http://lard.foo-projects.org/
</spam>

wrote that for just this type of thing - it's basically a full syslog replacement that 
can match regexes, spawn commands on certain log messages, does remote logging (so you 
can stack it on top of a normal syslogd too). I use it to block ssh login attempts 
instantly (okay, withing half a second or so) and even have it trigger on spam attempts 
from the postfix logs (are you faking your HELO? bye bye!): (example lard configuration 
snipplet)

command "/usr/sbin/block-ip-from"
     log mail.*
     match "Service unavailable; .* blocked using"
     match "Helo command rejected"
     match "Improper use of SMTP command pipelining"

etc :)

Cheers,

Auke

Yes.

>From: Aaron Burt <aaron at bavariati.org>
> Anyone here know GTK+ well enough to give a quickstart at the next
> available AT meeting?  I can offer beer as payment...

Wrong sort of payment, but otherwise, yes. I'm a bit far away to do it
casually and I'd need to do a lot of organization.



The other method is on Debian, install the package "libgtk-2.0-doc", go
into /usr/share/doc/libgtk2.0-doc, and look at the files
"gtk-tutorial/book1.html" and "gtk/index.html". The first is a pretty
decent tutorial and a good starting place. The second is the raw
documentation that would traditionally of been in man pages.

Alternatively, look at the files "docs/tutorial/html/book1.html" and
"docs/reference/gtk/html/index.html" in the GTK tarball. I imagine most
distributions have a package that includes those, but I don't guarentee
the filesystem location on anything but Debian.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Oct 26 00:49:45 2006

Date: Thu, 26 Oct 2006 00:49:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] GPL and Nukes
Message-ID: <20061026074945.GA4907@gate.kl-ic.com>


I was out at a lunch lecture at OTBC;  the subject was allegedly
"Making Money on Open Source".  The lawyer speaking actually spent
most of his time talking about how bad the GPL was.  One of his
points was that although the FSF makes about 30 out-of-court 
settlements a year, the GPL has only been tested once in court (it
won).  He thought that was insufficient testing.

I was pretty riled by this guy, so I spoke up with "Well, nuclear
weapons have only been tested on cities twice, but I wouldn't want
to be in the next city one goes off over!"

That brought down the house.  Feel free to use it elsewhere.

Keith

What does the 64 bit refer to?  Is it: the word size on these processors, 
the memory addressing space, a clever way to shove 2 32 bit processors 
into one package?  For all the recent duo core hype, is it really 
merited?  Sounds to me like problems from IA-32 architecture are still 
causing trouble for operators of the newer Itanium architecture systems, 
if that's what it's called.  I have an Alpha system.  A miserable 
experience, everywhere I went it was hard to get the right software 
for it.  To make it run again, I need to modify a new power supply for it.
  
Linux was lagging behind on Alpha and Microsoft abandoned both their 
Windows NT and their Windows 2000 port.  What will make or break 64 
bit computing?  Alpha got killed,  what makes Intel think that their 
64 bit offerings are going to live?  What makes AMD so confident?

The computer industry has pushed for speed, speed, and more speed.  Are
the current technologies being taken as far as they can go?  Hard drives
have grown in capacity impressively fast, yet lately their reliability
seems to be dropping.  Motherboard level: APM, plug and play, and ACPI 
implementations still seem to vary a great deal causing problems even 
in Windows.  Are features still designed for Windows by chipset and
processor manufacturers without any consideration of other OS'es.  Maybe 
our computers are getting fast enough, but what good is a race car without
tires?  What good is a duo core pentium system if the $10 power supply 
dies?  People say 64 bit computing is a reality, but then I see a long
discussion about problems running flash on these systems.

Reliability needs to catch up to speed increases.  Maybe skillful use of
pelsiar devices will allow the newest processors to operate fanless and
save on power consumption.  Nothing like losing a $3000 computer because
a $20 fan failed.

     --  Michael C. Robinson

from the livna FAQ http://rpm.livna.org/rlowiki/FAQ

This is not a technical issue, the FAQ should answer your
questions

And this from Fedora

http://www.fedoraproject.org/wiki/ForbiddenItems



Can I use rpm.livna.org packages during the installation of Fedora Core?

Yes, as of Fedora Core 6 the Fedora installer, Anaconda, supports using
external repositories during installation.


> Can Fedora Core 6 support DVD playback out of the box?

My guess no ...

> why is there a livna repository

As Fedora Core and Extras is officially affiliated with Red
Hat, Inc. in the Fedora Project, Fedora Core and Extras is
effectively bound by the same legal restrictions as Red Hat,
as a US company, is bound by. This means in particular that
software encumbered with US patents cannot be included.
Also excluded is software covered by licenses which are
incompatible with Red Hat's licencing policy, e.g.
licenses which only permit non-commercial use. See
Fedoras own list of forbidden items.

From ehem at m5p.com  Thu Oct 26 17:58:33 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Thu, 26 Oct 2006 17:58:33 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] PC Purchase
In-Reply-To: <20061026164659.08B0246F5@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200610270058.k9R0wXT3025332@m5p.com>

>From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com
> What does the 64 bit refer to?  Is it: the word size on these processors, 
> the memory addressing space, a clever way to shove 2 32 bit processors 
> into one package?  For all the recent duo core hype, is it really 
> merited?  Sounds to me like problems from IA-32 architecture are still 
> causing trouble for operators of the newer Itanium architecture systems, 
> if that's what it's called.  I have an Alpha system.  A miserable 
> experience, everywhere I went it was hard to get the right software 
> for it.  To make it run again, I need to modify a new power supply for it.

Addressible memory is generally 2 to the power of the word size, 2^32 for
processors with 32-bit words, 2^64 on processors with 64-bit words. ia32
of course had the segment registers which messed this up, but in 32-bit
mode those were pretty well ignored. I guess you haven't heard all the
mentions, Alpha is dead and Itanic is pretty well DOA (Intel claims it is
still alive, but almost everyone agrees it looks pretty well like a
zombie on un-life support).

Generally they're always refering to the word size (since that is the
number that matters). The one field where the manufacturers tend to lie
was in the console market, the N64 didn't use a 64-bit word, it merely
had a 64-bit wide bus.

> Linux was lagging behind on Alpha and Microsoft abandoned both their 
> Windows NT and their Windows 2000 port.  What will make or break 64 
> bit computing?  Alpha got killed,  what makes Intel think that their 
> 64 bit offerings are going to live?  What makes AMD so confident?

Do you really think Alpha, Itanic and ia32 are the only processors ever
made? There are plenty of other processors, such as 68K, ARM, MIPS,
PA-RISC, SPARC and PowerPC. Of those the last 4 all have 64-bit modes,
PA-RISC has been terminated by HP, SPARC looks like it will be slowly
dying off, while MIPS has been consigned to the embedded space (who don't
need 64-bit words *yet*), and with Apple gone PPC64 isn't on very many
machines. Nonetheless all four do have 64-bit modes/varients.

What will make 64-bit computing common is the 4GB barrier. 4GB is
2^32, once you've got 4GB of memory there isn't too much point of adding
more memory to a machine with 32-bit addressing. Even before then, a
fair slice of a process's address space tends to get grabbed by various
things being mapped in that aren't needed. Even at 2GB of memory address
starts becoming scarce. With normal consumer desktop systems heading
towards that, 64-bit is here to stay. Professional desktops of course are
already well past that point.

Intel has two 64-bit offerings. There is their implementation of amd64
(later P4s, and Core 2), and ia64 (Itanic). Their confidence in ia64 is
insanity, that is management trying not to admit defeat even when the
reality is they're DOA. Core 2 is a decent amd64 implementation, it is
faster than AMD's current generation (but that generation is now old) and
its power consumption isn't too awful. But, we've yet to see AMD's
answer, otherwise Core 2 is expected to be reasonably successful.

AMD of course produced the amd64 architecture. Their magic card was that
it was fully backwards compatible with ia32, as opposed to ia64 which
merely placed an ia32 core on the same die as the ia64. Since they've
only got one architecture to deal with, they don't have to waste as much
effort. Given how ia64 is DOA, and amd64 is now well established, AMD is
right to be confident in amd64. OTOH they're still behind Intel in
percentage of the market, but they've been making massive inroads.

> The computer industry has pushed for speed, speed, and more speed.  Are
> the current technologies being taken as far as they can go?  Hard drives
> have grown in capacity impressively fast, yet lately their reliability
> seems to be dropping.  Motherboard level: APM, plug and play, and ACPI 
> implementations still seem to vary a great deal causing problems even 
> in Windows.  Are features still designed for Windows by chipset and
> processor manufacturers without any consideration of other OS'es.  Maybe 
> our computers are getting fast enough, but what good is a race car without
> tires?  What good is a duo core pentium system if the $10 power supply 

Yeah, you get what you pay for. Cheap garbage is, well, cheap garbage.

> dies?  People say 64 bit computing is a reality, but then I see a long
> discussion about problems running flash on these systems.

Flash doesn't have a 64-bit version. Running an ia32 version of Flash
inside a browser that uses amd64, is a little interesting (though I'm
surprised no one has taken a serious whack at it yet).

> Reliability needs to catch up to speed increases.  Maybe skillful use of
> pelsiar devices will allow the newest processors to operate fanless and
> save on power consumption.  Nothing like losing a $3000 computer because
> a $20 fan failed.

Only problem is the peltier devices use a *lot* more power than a fan.
All of the current generation of processors have a shutdown on overheat,
so that isn't too big a deal (well, they shutdown, but they don't fry).

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Oct 27 06:20:49 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 27 Oct 2006 06:20:49 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Installing a Guest OS in VMware Server
In-Reply-To: <45411213.2080004@comcast.net>
References: <45411213.2080004@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061027132048.GA24633@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Thu, Oct 26, 2006 at 01:52:51PM -0600, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Several attempts to install Win98SE as a guest OS in VMware Server have
> all failed at the same point. After "power on" the virtual screen
> reports that I have an invalid system disk.

I can guess, though I can't tell for sure without seeing your
Windows 98 install disk.  Win98SE CDs come in /at least/ two
flavors, and one flavor of CD is designed to install on a new
computer while another only contains the files suitable for
restoring to an already-configured computer.  You probably have
the second kind of less-useful CD.  In "Bill's World", installing
on a clean hard drive (or a clean VMware partition) constitutes a
new copy, and you are supposed to use a CD capable of that.  These
full installation CDs are more difficult to find at garage sales
and on Ebay than the more common re-install CDs, since in theory
the full install CDs are limited to the folks that build computers,
not the folks that merely buy complete computers.

The full install CD for Win98SE will say something like "For
distribution with a new PC only" on them.

And of course it would be wrong, very wrong, to violate the rules
of "Bill's World" and make copies of full installation CDs and
use those copies, even if it was technically possible, which no
loyal American would know about anyway.  

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

> computer while another only contains the files suitable for
> restoring to an already-configured computer.  You probably have
> the second kind of less-useful CD.

<...>

> The full install CD for Win98SE will say something like "For
> distribution with a new PC only" on them.

The CDs I have been using are the first kind. They say "For distribution
with a new PC only..." Both of the PCs that they came with also came
with "Recovery" CDs, which when inserted into the CD drive at boot time
would cause the factory installation to be written to the hard drive.
This would include all the drivers as required for that particular
computer. I'm not trying to use the recovery CDs. Also, I did
successfully use one of these "distribution with a new PC" CDs in my
first, successful attempt to use VMware a couple of weeks ago, before I
re-installed SuSE 10.1.

Regards,

Dick Steffens

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Oct 27 07:24:30 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)

Subject: [PLUG] VMWare Player and Samba and caching
Message-ID: <20061027142430.GB25100@gate.kl-ic.com>


Since VMWare is a hot topic, I will ask a few questions I have
encountered so far.  Here's the first:

I have a copy [*1] of windows 98 SE running under VMWare under
Linux, connected through VMWare's Samba to my account on the
native Linux host "t30".  I can see the files in \\T30\keithl\
and copy them into Windoze.  The problem is, when I copy a file
from the host, change it, then copy it again, I get the old 
version.  It is behaving like the copy process is reading out 
of a cache somewhere.  The workaround is to make a renamed copy
on the host, copy into the guest,  and then rename the copy back
on the guest, but this is time consuming and error prone.  Not
to mention very lame.

Is this a problem with Windows 98?  Samba?  VMWare's version of
Samba?  Or am I being smitten by an angry God, disgusted with my
profane and unnatural descent into Microsoft Hell? :-)

Note, this is part of a project to port a Windows app to Linux,
so at some point I can ditch VMWare and return to blessed purity.
Thus I discount the smiting explanation,  God should favor the
recovery of a lost program into the blessed light of Linux!  :-)

Tee hees aside, have any of you seen this apparent caching problem
with VMWare, or can you suggest some alternate keywords besides
"caching" to Google the problem with?

Keith
