From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Fri Oct 27 08:51:58 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Fri, 27 Oct 2006 08:51:58 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] VMWare Player and Samba and caching
In-Reply-To: <20061027142430.GB25100@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20061027142430.GB25100@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610270847410.21707@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Fri, 27 Oct 2006, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> Is this a problem with Windows 98?  Samba?  VMWare's version of Samba?  Or
> am I being smitten by an angry God, disgusted with my profane and
> unnatural descent into Microsoft Hell? :-)

Keith,

   Yes, to all of the above.

   How is your smb share configured? Is there a common directory readable and
writeable by both linux and win98?

   I have such a directory and have not had any problems in the almost 10 years
I've run win9x in VMware. Here's that share definition at the end of the
/etc/samba/smb.conf file:

; share name -- modified by RBS 2004-09-22
[winstuff]
    comment = Shared directory for group vmwin98
    path = /data2/winstuff
    writable = yes
    valid users  = @vmwin98
    locking = yes
    create mode = 0660
    directory mode = 0770

Rich

working right. Other than that, I have not seen anything fatal.

taking legal action against them.

same size on the media.  Extras just keeps getting bigger.

I have run vmware in FC5.  Have not tried on FC6.  You just need to get the
right set of patches.

But you pay money for that.  The holders of those patents want your money.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Oct 27 21:46:00 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 27 Oct 2006 22:46:00 -0600
Subject: Resolved: [PLUG] Installing a Guest OS in VMware Server
In-Reply-To: <4542012B.8080306@comcast.net>
References: <45411213.2080004@comcast.net>	<20061027132048.GA24633@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<4542012B.8080306@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4542E088.7070503@comcast.net>

I had a back and forth conversation with someone on the VMware Forum
site. The recommendation was to install from the CD. Here's the
instructions:

With the virtual machine virtual CD-ROM drive configured to use the
physical CD, power on the VM and when you see the VMware Logo click the
mouse in the virtual machine window to get focus of the virtual machine
and press the "Esc" key. Select the CD-ROM as the first boot device.

This was a step I did not do before, the <ESC> and selection of the
CD-ROM. Following that procedure, I successfully installed the guest OS,
and then successfully installed the VMware Tools in the guest OS.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens



From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Oct 27 21:54:54 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 27 Oct 2006 22:54:54 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] Annoying alarm -- embarrassing discovery
Message-ID: <4542E29E.8010505@comcast.net>

This probably belongs in the same category as "is the power cord plugged
in?" or similar user error stories.

During the installation of the guest OS in VMware, using the physical CD
drive, I got that annoying alarm I've been getting since I began
building this machine. I was sure the machine wasn't overheating, but I
decided to get down under the desk and double check. Sure enough, it was
not the machine. The embarrassing discovery was where the alarm was
actually coming from: the battery backup. This machine has a 450 watt
power supply. All the older ones had 300 watt or less. I have two
machines powered through this battery backup. Spinning up the CD drive
on the new machine was enough to overload the battery backup unit. I
shut down the other machine and the alarm shut off.

This means that there is probably nothing wrong with the first mother
board, or any of the CD drives. On the other hand, this one works, and
is fast enough for my current needs, so I'm leaving it alone.

So, the next time I am able to make it to the clinic, you'll recognize
me because of the paper bag I'll be wearing over my head. I suppose the
headless horseman costume I'm wearing to a party tomorrow night would
also be appropriate.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

Also, for those that are interested, there is an issue with getting VMWare 
to install on Fedora Core 6.

There is a bug in the installer that installs the 586 kernel instead of 
the 686 kernel.  The fix is to install the 686 kernel and kernel-devel via 
yum.  Should fix the problem.

From gilmanhunt at comcast.net  Sat Oct 28 21:46:11 2006

I figure it's been a good long time since I've asked a stupid
question. There's a bunch of preamble here, if you want to see the
question, skip down about 5 paragraphs.

I'm minimally in charge of a set of servers. Some of them are very old
Pentium-4 class machines, and their motherboard has a lot of integrated
electronics, but this particular one has no video card. It has 3 pci
slots and one agp slot. 

It's a 2U chassis. Currently it's refusing to boot without a
video card. I can install one, but to do that I have to remove the
motherboard, brace it with non-conductive materials, insert agp
card (it's what I have handy), and leave everything connected to it.

This won't work in a server, and I can't leave my agp card in it. It
sticks up outside the box and it generally won't go into the
motherboard; it doesn't fit right; seems to be a little short
lengthwise, and some of the chassis openings for the bracket of the
card to connect to the monitor cables are in the wrong places. It has 3
vertical and one horizontal opening in the case; perhaps the horizontal
one would come off of a riser card of some sort?

I've tried all the things I can do to change the BIOS settings et
cetera, and I honestly can't make it come up without a video card.
Which is strange, because this is a relatively new problem- this system
booted fine without a card last month.

vvv===The question===vvv
If I go to order a video card for this box, what is it called?
Do I order a "low rise video card" like the video cards on this page ?
http://www.directron.com/lowprofile.html

As a side note; if I have other cards I need for similar boxes, am I
just looking at ordering 'low profile' cards?

Russ (YAR) Gilman-Hunt wrote:

> I've tried all the things I can do to change the BIOS settings et
> cetera, and I honestly can't make it come up without a video card.
> Which is strange, because this is a relatively new problem- this system
> booted fine without a card last month.

Look at your COM port settings and see if you can find console redirect, 
or something to that effect.  Enable it to see if that makes a difference.

> vvv===The question===vvv
> If I go to order a video card for this box, what is it called?
> Do I order a "low rise video card" like the video cards on this page ?
> http://www.directron.com/lowprofile.html

This is what I started thinking you needed before I read to much.

> As a side note; if I have other cards I need for similar boxes, am I
> just looking at ordering 'low profile' cards?

What I'm confused about is you saying your AGP card is a little short. 
The physical slot and position of the slot is no different than on a 
normal desktop board.  It's nothing special outside of the low profile 
backplane to fit the chassis.

-- 
- m0gely

From eehouse at eehouse.org  Sun Oct 29 07:55:29 2006
From: eehouse at eehouse.org (eehouse)
Date: Sun, 29 Oct 2006 15:55:29 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] Need help booting M0n0wall (FreeBSD) using Grub
Message-ID: <20061029155529.Z25765@eehouse.org>

This weekend's project is a WRAP box I use as a home firewall/router.

For storage I have a 1G (Sandisk) CF card with GRUB on the MBR.  There
are three extended partitions, formatted ext2, with Grub's files in
one and a couple of LEAF releases in the others.  Everything works
that far: on boot I see the Grub menu over the serial console and can
boot either of the LEAF releases.  Here's how LEAF's fdisk sees the
card:

Command (m for help): p

Disk /dev/hda: 1024 MB, 1024966656 bytes
32 heads, 63 sectors/track, 993 cylinders
Units = cylinders of 2016 * 512 = 1032192 bytes

   Device Boot      Start         End      Blocks   Id  System
/dev/hda1               1         125      125968+  a5  FreeBSD
/dev/hda2             126         250      126000   a5  FreeBSD
/dev/hda3             251         375      126000   a5  FreeBSD
/dev/hda4             376         993      622944    5  Extended
/dev/hda5             376         377        1984+  83  Linux
/dev/hda6             378         382        5008+  83  Linux
/dev/hda7             383         387        5008+  83  Linux

I got the card (1G Sandisk CF and SD cards are $20 at Staples this
week, BTW :-) and set aside the larger primary partitions in order to
play with M0n0wall, a FreeBSD-based firewall/router distribution.  But
I can't get Grub to boot M0n0wall.

I downloaded a monowall image, and wrote it to an old but proven 8M
card (in a USB reader) just to confirm that it works:

# dd if=/tmp/wrap-1.22.img of=/dev/sda

Here's what my desktop's fdisk says about that card after dd finishes:

Command (m for help): p

Disk /dev/sda: 8 MB, 8028160 bytes
256 heads, 63 sectors/track, 0 cylinders
Units = cylinders of 16128 * 512 = 8257536 bytes

   Device Boot      Start         End      Blocks   Id  System
/dev/sda4   *           1           4       25000   a5  FreeBSD
Partition 4 has different physical/logical endings:
     phys=(1023, 255, 63) logical=(3, 25, 41)

I can boot my WRAP box off of this card, and it takes me into the
M0n0wall setup process, so I think the image is good.

But if I then write the image to the 1G card grub can't handle it.
I've tried writing the original image (the same I dd'd to /dev/sda) to
/dev/sda3, which I think was wrong since that image is an entire disk.
But I've also dd'd /dev/sda4 from the working M0n0wall 8M disk and
written that to /dev/sda3 of the 1G disk.  In either case grub can't
boot, and the failures look the same:

root (hd0,2)
 Filesystem type unknown, partition type 0xa5
makeactive
chainloader +1

Not ufs
Not ufs
No /boot/loader
 
>> FreeBSD/i386 BOOT
Default: 0:ad(0,a)/kernel
boot: 
Not ufs
No /kernel
 
>> FreeBSD/i386 BOOT
Default: 0:ad(0,a)/kernel
boot: 

I got my Grub off my desktop, which is current Debian "testing".  It
doesn't seem to include support for ufs.  But what I've read about
booting FreeBSD suggests that at least the chainloader method should
work, yet it doesn't.  I downloaded grub 0.97 sources (apt-get source
is broken for me for some reason, so I went to gnu.org) and built with
ufs support, but it wasn't clear to me how to install onto the CF
card, so I haven't tried that yet.  I did copy the ufs2_stage1_5 file
over, and ran grub's setup command on the WRAP box, but that didn't
help.

All of the instructions I've seen for dual-booting FreeBSD with Grub
assume you're doing a normal install onto a disk, then setting up
Grub.  I'm working with an image, and suspect that's part of the
problem.

Can anybody suggest a next step?

Thanks!

--Eric

You can try a vgscan which should scan the disk for LVM data. You might need
to do a vgimport after that if it shows up as exported when you do a
vgdisplay plus a vgchange -a y if its showing as inactive.

Well, you'll probably also need port 6000 (x11) and perhaps 7100 
(xfs).

The other thing is that most X configurations these days don't open 
themselves to remote access. You'll have to tweak things a bit. If 
you're using GNOME, you'll want to start in /etc/X11/gdm/gdm.conf. 
Look for the [xdmcp] section. (If you're using a more generic X login 
manager, look in /etc/X11/xdm/Xaccess.)

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

On Tue, 31 Oct 2006, William A Morita wrote:

> Paul,
> 
> I just thought to look at it under Ethereal...
> 
> The response from port 177 is:
>  Internet Control Message Protocol
>     Type: 3 (Destination unreachable)
>     Code: 10 (Host administratively prohibited)
> 
> This in spite of having used system-config-securitylevel to open 
> port 177.

Did you open up port 177 for all IP protocols or just TCP? You need to 
open it to UDP for XDMCP to work.

Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

      home to a number of major Open Source projects.  The 
      Open Source Education Lab and the Oregon State Linux Users Group
      are also neat and provide ways of getting undergraduates 
      involved with OpenSource software.

In-Reply-To: <20061028214611.40343e89@badger.had1.or.comcast.net.>

Message-ID: <20061031193942.GM11706@syrinxpc.com>

On Sat, Oct 28, 2006 at 09:46:11PM -0700, Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> I've tried all the things I can do to change the BIOS settings et
> cetera, and I honestly can't make it come up without a video card.
> Which is strange, because this is a relatively new problem- this system
> booted fine without a card last month.

You *sure* it doesn't have onboard video, that's just been temporarily
disabled or permanently fried?  Most PC systems give an 8-beep code and
many won't boot w/o video.

I'd recommend you hit the Free Geek thriftstore for (a) another 2U box,
(b) a replacement mobo, or (c) a short PCI or AGP video card.  You can
just leave the bracket off if it's too tall.  And they might have a
low-profile card floating around, or in the "untested" pile in the back
corner.

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Tue Oct 31 14:53:08 2006
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Tue, 31 Oct 2006 14:53:08 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] XDMCP and the Firewall -- Resolved
In-Reply-To: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0610310528290.7233@orange.madboa.com>
Message-ID: <00d701c6fd3f$5762d660$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

Group,

Thanks to Paul, I have succeeded.
I have summarized what it takes to get XDMCP to work through the firewall on
FC6 below:

XDMCP Setup
        /etc/X11/xdm/Xaccess, uncomment "# * #any host can get a login
window".
        /etc/X11/xdm/xdm-config, comment out "DisplayManager.RequestPort:
0".
        fc4:/etc/X11/gdm/gdm.conf, set enable status to True (or 1) in
[xdmcp].
        /etc/kde/kdm/kdmrc, set enable status to True (or 1) in [xdmcp].
        The system needs to run in init level 5 (/etc/inittab).

        To increase number of sessions:
        FC4:   Change/Set DisplaysPerHost in gdm.conf
        FC5/6: Use gdmsetup: Set remote same as local; Remote admin login .


        Firewall defaults will prevent operation.  
        fc5/6: Use /usr/bin/system-config-securitylevel
        Open ports (both TCP and UDP):
             xdmcp (177)
             x11   (6000)

        Reboot the system after modification.


- Bill Morita 

wamorita at hevanet.com 
 

-----Original Message-----
From: plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org
[mailto:plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org] On Behalf Of Paul Heinlein
Sent: Tuesday, October 31, 2006 5:41 AM
To: wamorita at hevanet.com; General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;civil and
on-topic
Subject: Re: [PLUG] XDMCP and the Firewall

On Mon, 30 Oct 2006, William A Morita wrote:

> Hi guys,
>
> I am trying to get XDMCP to operate on FC6 without disabling the 
> firewall. I have tried explicitly opening port 177 for XDMCP, but that 
> does not seem to be enough.

Well, you'll probably also need port 6000 (x11) and perhaps 7100 (xfs).

The other thing is that most X configurations these days don't open
themselves to remote access. You'll have to tweak things a bit. If you're
using GNOME, you'll want to start in /etc/X11/gdm/gdm.conf. 
Look for the [xdmcp] section. (If you're using a more generic X login
manager, look in /etc/X11/xdm/Xaccess.)

--
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

is quite limited (mostly very short snippets, such as used for fan-made

Yeah, they claim that. The problem is not so much the proprietary
technology, as their efforts to make even touching that illegal. Such as
making defeating DRM illegal, despite DRM being broken and defeating it
actually being fixing the problem. They are desperate though, that is why
they're resorting to using the legal system to gain them more money,
rather than trying to actually make good stuff.

From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)

In-Reply-To: <20061101001616.E58254785@ulke.nakedape.cc>

>From: "William A Morita" <wamorita at hevanet.com>
> Thanks to Paul, I have succeeded.
> I have summarized what it takes to get XDMCP to work through the firewall on
> FC6 below:

I hope you're aware that XDMCP/X Protocol is not secure by itself. You'll
be typing passwords in cleartext as they cross the Internet...

This is why SSH X tunneling is very much the prefered method of doing
things similar to this. SSH does a good job of tunneling the X
connections, and takes care of the security. Needs "-X" with OpenSSH.

From denish at dslnorthwest.net  Tue Oct 31 17:57:35 2006
From: denish at dslnorthwest.net (Denis Heidtmann)
Date: Tue, 31 Oct 2006 17:57:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] PC Purchase

	<1162193448.4545aa28d11d6@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
>> Only problem is the peltier devices use a *lot* more power than a fan.
>> All of the current generation of processors have a shutdown on overheat,
>> so that isn't too big a deal (well, they shutdown, but they don't fry).
> 
> Based on what?  Isn't the space shuttle covered on the bottom with pelsiar
> devices so that it can generate electricity when it's turned toward the sun
> off of the temperature gradient?  There's zero applied power, so I don't know
> what it is your talking about.
> 
> Having your processor not shut down is no big deal unless it isn't overheating.
> 
>    --  Michael Robinson

I assume you mean peltier, not pelsiar.  I do not know what pelsiar is.

Zero applied power would mean no sunshine.  On the shuttle, the device is 
operated as a source of electrical energy rather than a heat transfer device 
(runs the thing backwards compared it its use in a computer).

The use of peltier devices for heat transfer uses more energy than the heat 
energy being transferred, by a factor of two in many cases.  So it is necessary 
to dump the heat from the processor plus the extra heat generated by running the 
peltier.  This is worth it in some circumstances, such as keeping something 
cooler than the heat sink temperature.

-Denis


http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peltier-Seebeck_effect

http://www.heatsink-guide.com/content.php?content=peltierinfo.shtml

 From the above link:

Peltier elements have very low efficiency. They will consume more power than 
they transport! Actual peltier elements may consume twice as much energy (in the 
form of electricity) as they transport (in the form of heat). So, if you are 
using a peltier element, the heatsink it is used with must be much more powerful 
than a heatsink used for cooling a heat source without peltier element.

I hope you're aware that XDMCP/X Protocol is not secure by itself. You'll be
typing passwords in cleartext as they cross the Internet...

This is why SSH X tunneling is very much the prefered method of doing things
similar to this. SSH does a good job of tunneling the X connections, and
takes care of the security. Needs "-X" with OpenSSH.

From tonyr at hevanet.com  Tue Oct 31 19:40:05 2006
From: tonyr at hevanet.com (tonyr)
Date: Tue, 31 Oct 2006 19:40:05 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] /bin/loadkeys Runs Only As Root
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610311349320.18434@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0610310859410.18434@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<45479BAC.9090901@hevanet.com>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610311053541.18434@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<4547A33E.1000202@hevanet.com>	<e2aeccd60610311129l7a30e18fx8d9c72561930fd88@mail.gmail.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0610311349320.18434@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <45481715.5060001@hevanet.com>

I am not Slackware conversant, but the little bit of reading that I did
leads me to believe that there are two paths to keymap modification.
One is at startup, through the *rc* process, either via 
/*etc/rc.d/rc.keymap*
or *rc.local*, using *loadkeys* to read a keymap file in a known location.
In this case *loadkeys *would indeed be running as *root, *and so would 
succeed.
The other way is through an X session startup, in which case the
keymap modifications should be in *~/.Xmodmap*, who's format,
I believe, is not the same as a keymap file read by *loadkeys*.

None of my searching turned up an example where a local user's
*~/.keymap* (or ~/.<anyfoo> for that matter) was used, in Slackware
or anywhere else. I'm gonna guess that you've been using the
~/.keymap method for a relatively long time, and maybe even have
a *loadkeys* command in your .login or .profile or .bashrc or .cshrc
or whatever your shell startup file is.

Since my (old) Mandriva system shows that I own */dev/console*, and
your Slackware and my Ubuntu do not show that, I can only
conclude that somewhere along the way Slackware changed
it's mind about how keymaps should oughta be loaded. Interestingly,
in Mandriva I can READ and parse the file as user tonyr, but cannot
update the KeyMaps (permission denied). In Ubuntu, I have no access
to *loadkeys* functionality at all as user tonyr.

- tony
(who knows not whereof he speaks)

From johnxj at comcast.net  Tue Oct 31 22:35:06 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Tue, 31 Oct 2006 22:35:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] PDF viewers for editable PDF files
Message-ID: <20061031223506.f80b80f0.johnxj@comcast.net>

When Adobe came out with Adobe Reader 7.0 they included the ability to
fill in PDF forms. The user can type into blanks in the form and then
print it out. However, the user cannot save a copy of the edited form.
At the same time that this feature was added to Reader, Acrobat was
enhanced to add the ability to create editable PDF files.

I can create editable PDF files with OpenOffice.org Writer. I have
opened them in Adobe Reader 7.08 on my Ubuntu amd-64 Dapper laptop and
I can edit the forms. I can also print them, but I cannot save them.
However, I have a workaround for the latter problem ?- CUPS-PDF
printer. I have installed it and now I can create a PDF file from any
application that can print, including Adobe Reader.

There is an equivalent to CUPS-PDF printer on Windows, called
PDF-Creator. I haven?t checked it out, but it is free from sourceforge.
As for Macs, all modern Macs come with the ability to print to file as
a PDF. It?s built into the OS.

My goal is to create homework files as editable PDFs. The student can
fill them out, print to CUPS-PDF printer or to PDF Creator, and e-mail
the results back to the professor. Fast, simple, no paper, and the
professor can actually read the student?s work because there is no
handwriting. This is ideal for distance education. This could be really
cool because in OOo you can make the editable field a blank to type
into, a radio button, a drop-down list, or any of several other types
of controls. What a cool tool for creating exams, quizzes and homework
assignments!

However, there are some gaps and questions:

1) Macintosh users. Adobe Reader 7.0 for Mac can fill out editable PDF
forms the same as the Linux and Windows versions. But I don?t have a
Mac so I can?t test the built in print-to-PDF utility. I bet the reason
Adobe Reader can?t save a copy of the edited file is a DRM issue, in
which case I bet the built in print-to-PDF utility won?t work if the
file is an edited PDF. Being the owner of an iPod it would not surprise
me if Apple included that restriction. Are there any Mac owners here
who might know?

2) I can do it on Linux with Adobe Reader 7.08, but doing so is a pain.
Adobe Reader does not have a drop-down printer selection box in it
print dialog box in the Linux version. You have to type in an lpr
command. Thus, every time I want to print to a new PDF file I have to
change the command that says it is to print to my laserjet. And then
when I want to print to the laserjet, I have to retype the command
again. To get around this I tried Kpdf, evince, and Xpdf. All of them
have a proper printer selection drop-down window, but none of them can
edit the editable PDF file. Are there other PDF viewers that might be
better than these three?


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Tue Oct 31 22:51:35 2006
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Tue, 31 Oct 2006 22:51:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ifswitch interface chooser
Message-ID: <200610312251.35379.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

Hi all,

I wanted something to make it easy to switch networks on my wife's 
thinkpad and couldn't find anything that fit the bill.

I use ifup/ifdown with profiles defined in /etc/network/interfaces (e.g. 
"ifup eth0=eth0-home") and wanted to stick with that.  Also, I found a 
lot of gui apps for wifi, but nothing that worked with the interfaces 
file.

This uses kdialog to let you choose an interface (it reads the 
interfaces file to build a list and checks /etc/network/run/ifstate to 
tell you what's on at the moment) and then ifup's the selection after 
doing ifdown on everything else.

  http://scratchcomputing.com/svn/ifswitch/trunk/bin/ifswitch

You'll need to 'cpan IPC::Run'.  I think that's it.  Build is not really 
setup yet.  You will (of course) need ifupdown and such.

I hooked it to the thinkpad key with tpb, but it is designed to run as 
root and autodetect the user/display, so you can hook it to acpi events 
or whatever button daemon you like.

--Eric
-- 
Consumers want choice, consumers want openness.
--Rob Glaser
---------------------------------------------------
    http://scratchcomputing.com
